# The Lives Left in Them

# Chapter 1

The overhead board above table four had not flashed red yet, but Mira could feel it loading toward her lane. She snapped fresh gloves over her callused fingertips and slit open the next return sling before the Friday queue outran the vents. Her hair was tied back so tight it pinched at the mole near her left temple. Salt pushed in first from the canal loading door, then fryer grease from the market below the tram, followed by the sharper things the building tried and failed to erase: solvent, coolant, stale perfume, sweet tissue rot. By midshift the CommonFrame Claims Annex no longer passed for clean air. It smelled like Cordera after money had worn another body for a while.

Friday mattered because the city liked fresh flesh for the weekend. By six, labor frames had to be washed, patched, relabeled, and sent upstairs for whatever the next shift of paying strangers wanted from them. Dock crews returned broad-shouldered lift units with cracked metacarpals and tendon strain from pretending one body could do the work of three. Hotel clients sent back companion shells smelling of cardamom gin, latex-safe lubricant, and somebody else's expensive mouthwash. Adrenaline packages came in with gravel in the knees and seawall rash on the palms because no one who paid to be twenty-four and unbreakable ever stayed indoors. The Annex sorted what could be billed, what could be denied, what had to be pulped, and what could still be rented before dark if somebody like Mira moved quickly and did not start asking theological questions about the merchandise.

She was good at it. She could usually tell what kind of lie a renter planned to tell before she opened the bag.

A warehouse shift body from North Quay came back with both shoulders hot and swollen, skin crazed with old adhesive. The return note said stair slip. Mira found the rectangular ghost marks of an unauthorized brace under the deltoid and tagged it labor overuse, shared fault, partial fee reduction because the renter had been too poor to stop before something tore. A leisure body with a broken front tooth and yacht wax in the hair claimed accidental fall. Mira found the heel-web bruising from a restraint harness and the burst capillaries under both eyelids from autoerotic stupidity, then attached the right waiver code and moved on. Another came back carrying three hours of nightclub bass in the temporal ports and a split at the corner of the mouth where somebody had wanted a rougher version of themselves than their own face allowed. She flushed the residue packet, noted that the lessee had paid for deluxe discretion, and sent the shell toward wash with a damage surcharge that would never hurt the person who'd caused it.

The porter from intake rolled over another sealed return sling and nudged it against her table with the soft impatience of a man who had been on his feet since dawn. His orange rain hood was still wet from the canal mist, and he had a kingfisher tattoo under one ear that had gone green at the edges with age. The label hanging from the sling read `LM-127B / bargain aggregate / express turnover / private proxy lease`. Somebody upstairs had drawn a fast blue circle around `express`.

"Last one from harbor pickup," he said. "Then you've got six waiting by cold storage and one puking memory in bay two."

"Nothing in bay two pukes memory," Mira said, eyes still on the tablet. "It reclassifies liability."

The porter snorted through his nose, pleased enough to count as a laugh, and moved on.

Mira broke the seal on the sling. Cold preservative breath pushed out, chemical and faintly sweet. The body inside was an adult bargain frame from the cheap line that tried to look like nothing in particular and therefore looked familiar to everyone. It was medium height, with soft shoulders and rental-adjustable hips. The nape port was still capped with a paper privacy seal from a rush lease, damp at one corner where somebody had sweated through it. One iris, clouded under shutdown glaze, was a shade lighter than the other. There was a notch in the left ear that the intake sticker did not mention.

She peeled back the absorbent wrap from the chest and stopped.

The scar sat under the left rib line, pale and healed, a narrow curved seam the color of old milk. Fresh tears looked ragged and rental cuts looked clean; this had the settled ridge of old tissue. The kind of thing a printed body should not have unless it had lived long enough to scar or the print map had been built with one on purpose. Bargain bodies did come with cosmetic leftovers sometimes, freckles from bad calibration, asymmetrical nails, hairlines a little too human to be elegant. Surgical history with no bill attached belonged to another category entirely.

The groin told a different story and a more ordinary one, if ordinary was the word for what the city did with rented flesh when it thought liability had been prepaid. The interface skin was rubbed raw in two places. Dried lubricant had gone tacky along the inner thigh seam. There were thumb-shaped bruises high on both legs, one newer, one faded enough to be yellowing at the edges. The mouth was split slightly inside the lower lip. Nothing here would surprise the Annex. It was only the combination that made Mira keep looking.

She pulled the print schema onto the wall screen and overlaid it on the body. Factory map: smooth rib line, unnotched ear, standard iris pair, no custom marks, no surgical modifications, print timestamp eleven days earlier. The unit was eleven days old. It was fresh stock. The scar on the table was older than that by weeks at the bare minimum, maybe months. Mira touched the seam with a gloved finger, feeling the slight ridge under the skin. The body was cool and slack under her hand. Healed tissue should not have been waiting in something printed eleven days ago for anonymous use.

She swabbed the temple ports and fed the strip into the residue reader.

Most bargain-line bleed came back dirty but legible. It might arrive as a flash of elevator panic, a slap of club light, hunger, motion sickness, or somebody else's borrowed orgasm arriving late and stupid into the wash cycle. The reader translated residue into a shallow sensory packet, not enough to give back a life, just enough to sort sanitation failure from lawsuit risk. Mira expected noise. She got water.

The water was too bright and too clean for canal runoff or shower tile: green-blue chlorine under afternoon sun, bright enough to hurt. The camera angle sat low and small at child's height, looking across a municipal pool lane where a boy in orange trunks had gone under and not come back up on time. A whistle shrieked once. Adults on hot concrete started yelling. The packet cut out on the shape of the boy's heel vanishing below the chopped glitter of the surface.

Mira stared at the screen until the reader timed out and cleared itself.

Deep childhood residue almost never survived a legitimate wash on premium stock. On bargain stock it sometimes limped through as static, a smell, a phrase, one stubborn image that should have died in the rinse. But this unit had gone from private dock room to her table on rush handling with dampening paid in advance. Whatever sat in its ports had crossed more departments than claims before it reached her, which meant the problem was already larger than one damage fee.

She ran the packet again.

The same pool returned, and the same orange trunks flashed under the surface. This time the clip held one second longer, enough for a child's left hand to slap wet tile in panic. It was a small hand, with blue chipped polish on the thumbnail. The boy going under was not the hand's owner. Whoever had seen this had been close enough to taste chlorine and fear, and young enough that the tile edge towered high at chest level. The packet cut, leaving a smear of sunlight in Mira's eyes that was only the scanner's afterimage but stayed anyway.

She looked back at the body on the table. The lighter iris and ear notch no longer read like cheap-line noise. They read like details somebody upstream had failed to erase.

A bad wash could leave behind arousal. It could leave panic. It could leave somebody's one humiliating song lyric. None of that explained why the bruises on the thighs looked older than the return timestamp, as if the body had arrived pre-handled. The queue clock kept ticking anyway.

The overhead board chimed and flashed her lane number in yellow. It was a throughput warning. She had spent too long on one cheap frame.

Mira checked the intake tag again. Proxy lease through concierge intermediary. No renter name on the surface label, only account shielding and a privacy rider thick enough to kick anything sensitive up one floor if she let it. She almost did. Friday afternoon was not the hour for principles. Friday afternoon was for getting bodies back into the building's appetite before the overtime freeze made everyone's mistakes more expensive.

She should have classified the visible damage, attached the rider, and sent LM-127B to wash or pulp depending on what the deeper scan said. Instead she slid the body to the side rail, marked the file `verification pending`, and felt the board shave a little more money off her week.

Around her the Annex kept moving. A woman at counter three screamed because the body she'd returned missing a fingertip had better not be charged as custom damage when the fingertip had never belonged to her in the first place. Somebody in cold storage laughed so hard they coughed. The tram went past outside, a metal moan through the wall. Mira stripped off one glove, flexed the ache out of her fingers, and opened the lease file.

* * *

LM-127B's record came up under a shielded leisure account with three layers of indirection and no renter name anywhere a claims adjuster could touch. Proxy work liked that arrangement. It moved shame from the principal to a paid middle hand and called the transfer discretion. The surcharge alone could have covered Mira's rent for two months if CommonFrame had ever believed in paying the people who handled its ugliness.

She kept scrolling: nine-hour lease, pickup at a private dock room, return through harbor intake, emergency damage indemnity, genital remap. Then she hit `memory dampening premium` and sat straighter. Clients bought dampening for affairs, violence, grief tourism, and for corporate idiots who spent all week in arbitration language and did not want a borrowed body's panic crawling back into the spouse asleep beside the original. Failure was supposed to arrive as blur, not as a child at poolside watching a boy drown.

Mira opened a contact request. The system asked twice whether she intended to query a shielded account on a bargain-line unit. She checked yes twice and entered the official reason: undocumented physical modification with nonconforming residue profile. That alone would put a small amber mark on her activity log. She could almost feel Celia's expensive pen hovering over it somewhere in the building.

The proxy responded on the third ring.

The assistant appeared from the back seat of a hired car, one shoe off and tucked under her opposite thigh while she rubbed at the bunion bulging through a black stocking. She was in her thirties maybe, hair still pinned from a workday that had started respectable and turned punishing, lipstick worn away at the center from too much coffee. A garment bag hung beside her. On the seat behind it sat a paper carton from a pediatric pharmacy with cartoon whales printed on the side, half hidden under files. She glanced at Mira's badge, then at some message outside frame, and arranged her face into professional impatience.

"You have two minutes," she said. "If this is billing, send it to the account address."

"Mira Alves, CommonFrame Claims. I'm calling because the returned unit has damage outside standard rider assumptions and a print mismatch that may affect liability."

"It won't," the assistant said. "Bill whatever the rider covers and close it."

"I can't close it until I reconcile the mismatch."

The assistant exhaled through her nose. Traffic light and canal glare striped the side of her face as the car moved. "What mismatch."

Mira kept her voice in the register the company liked from people at her salary point: exact, calm, a little apologetic, as if ugly facts were regrettable weather. "The unit's got a healed scar under the left ribs that isn't on the production map. It's also holding memory it shouldn't. I need to know whether your principal reported old surgery, prior modification, or anything done off-book."

"No."

"No to which part."

"All of it." The assistant shifted the phone, then winced as the movement pulled something in her foot. "The shell was picked for discretion. That's the whole point. We're not filing a supplemental statement."

That little we did it. Mira saw the word turn a body into a paper towel and a night's work into a housekeeping issue. She kept her own face still.

"The dampening rider says your principal had privacy concerns," she said. "This wasn't normal bleed. A child saw someone go under in a pool, and that memory is still sitting in the unit. If water set something off, I need that noted."

For the first time the assistant looked fully at her.

"A child what."

"A kid by a pool. Somebody drowning. That's what's in the packet."

"Jesus Christ." It came out half disgust, half fatigue. The assistant pressed thumb and forefinger to her eyes, careful not to smear what was left of her makeup. "He paid extra for dampening because the last time a shell carried anything vivid, his husband saw the playback summary and I had to spend two days making brunch excuses. There wasn't a statement then and there won't be one now. Do you understand me?"

That gave Mira two worse facts. The principal did not buy bodies. He bought deniability. And he had bled through dampening before, enough that the assistant already knew how to manage the fallout. This call was not about whether the packet was real. It was about whether anyone with money had to admit it.

"Did your principal tell intake about any old surgery to the chest or ribs?" Mira asked.

"No, because he didn't have surgery through your shell."

"The scar predates the lease."

"Then the scar predates our problem."

"Not if the factory map is lying."

The assistant barked one surprised laugh, ugly with exhaustion. "Do you people hear yourselves? It's a discount body. He used it because nobody tracks burner shells the way they track bespoke stock. That's why we paid for the rider. That's why I'm taking this call instead of him. Charge the account. Destroy the unit. I've got a child waiting at urgent care and a principal who thinks discretion can be outsourced, so if you need me to say the body is disposable, I'm saying it now."

There it was: not just disdain but need and hurry, somebody else's child with a pharmacy carton and cartoon whales, and somebody else's man who could spend more on discretion than Mira earned in weeks and still make this woman do the apologizing. Mira felt her jaw set so hard the joint clicked.

"I need you to say that on the record," Mira said.

"You need what you can defend if someone above you asks why you made this call on a Friday. Put this on your record: no statement. No complaint. No interest in recovery. Bill the rider and pulp the shell."

The line went dead before Mira could answer.

For a second she saw her own reflection in the black screen, thin under the Annex light, dark curls escaping at the temples, mouth already setting into the line Gabe called her rent face. Then the internal messenger chimed.

`Why did you open a live query on shielded proxy leisure stock?`

Celia did not waste words even in text.

Mira typed back: `Undocumented scar. Deep residue. Needed claimant position.`

The dots appeared, vanished, reappeared.

`Then you have it. Close the exposure. My office in ten.`

Mira read the message twice, as if a third reading might make the wording gentler or less specific. It did neither. She could have ignored it for six minutes and maybe bought herself time to bury the file cleanly. Instead she pulled LM-127B's print history one more time. The unit was eleven days past fabrication. Its batch origin was partially masked by internal code and marked as aggregate line. Salvage component percentage was redacted unless she had factory clearance, which she did not.

She clicked into the residue packet metadata. The system had classified the drowning image as nonbillable mnemonic contamination, low litigation risk, auto-clear on disposal. In other words: if she let the body go down the chute, the image would go with it and no one above claims would ever need to admit it had existed.

At the next table, a coworker she barely knew was eating cold noodles one-handed over an open torso report, picking sesame seeds off the print gel with the absent concentration of the overworked. Nobody in the Annex looked over. Nobody cared what one bargain model had done to Mira's afternoon. The noodles smelled faintly of soy and old broth, and the ordinariness of it made her want to drive her thumb through the dead screen.

She closed the file, stripped off her gloves, and went to Celia's office with the chlorinated afterimage still hanging at the edge of her sight.

* * *

Celia Rusk's office had one glass wall facing the claims floor and blinds that were almost always half-closed, a compromise between surveillance and the performance of privacy. She kept the place too tidy to be natural, with one cup for pens, one stack of hearing packets, and one framed city permit from before CommonFrame had gotten large enough to stop hanging permits where workers could see them. Celia herself sat behind the desk in an ash-colored suit that looked expensive until you noticed the shine starting at the cuffs. Her silvering hair was cut clean at the jaw. Her hands, already knuckling with arthritis, occupied themselves with a black fountain pen that probably cost more than Mira's shoes.

"Sit down," Celia said.

Mira sat.

On the screen between them glowed LM-127B's file, thin and ugly with annotations. Celia had already read everything. Of course she had.

"You burned eleven minutes on a budget shell with a no-statement proxy," Celia said. Her voice in person was the same as in writing: controlled enough to sound kind if you were tired or naive. "At four-forty on a Friday."

"The map doesn't match the body."

"I saw that."

"The residue's wrong too."

"Mira."

Celia said her name the way managers said names when they wanted to remind you the company had loaned them your first name for disciplinary use.

"The hold shelf is full," Celia went on. "Cold storage is at weekend threshold. If you escalate every bargain anomaly into an existential inquiry, you will turn throughput into hearings by Monday."

"I'm not escalating every anomaly."

"No. Only the ones that interest you."

Mira bit the inside of her cheek hard enough to taste blood. It was a childish habit and she hated that Celia had likely noticed it months ago. "The file's wrong in ways the menu can't hold. The proxy wants it pulped, and the system has already marked the residue low-risk on disposal."

Celia twirled the pen once between stiff fingers. "There is no category available to you that will improve those facts."

"There should be."

"There isn't." Celia leaned back, watching her with an expression so nearly sympathetic it annoyed Mira more than open contempt would have. "We're claims. We classify exposure after the money has already made its choice. If the factory printed bad stock, that is factory. If a shielded client carried something embarrassing through dampening, that is premium services. If you keep a bargain shell on ice because it unsettles you, that becomes my problem and then yours."

The fountain pen clicked softly against the desk. Outside the glass wall, bodies kept arriving under white sling plastic. The claims floor moved around them like a tide around a piling.

"Do you want me to code it contamination and pass a fee to the account," Mira asked, "or code it manufacturing defect and let factory bounce it back as unauthorized modification."

"I want you to reduce risk." Celia's tone sharpened by half a degree. "The proxy has declined recovery. There is no claimant statement. There is no active renter challenge. Clear the shelf. If you need language, use chain discrepancy, wait the minimum, then dispose."

Chain discrepancy was the safest dirty category in the building. It bought seventy-two hours if you were careful and nobody looked twice. It also flagged the unit as dead inventory awaiting paperwork correction, which meant by Monday some night-shift idiot or automated sweep could still send it to pulp if Mira did nothing else. Celia had given her a procedural out and an instruction to stop thinking. That was more generosity than most managers offered. It left a thin cold stripe between Mira's shoulder blades.

"You checked the residue packet yourself?" Mira asked.

Celia's eyes flicked to the screen, then back. "Do not make me answer questions you already know I should not entertain."

There it was, very small. It was not agreement or confession. It was only the fact that Celia's caution had edges.

"Clear the shelf by end of shift," Celia said. "And log your time honestly. If audit asks why you queried a shielded proxy, I will tell them you got conscientious and then sensible."

Mira stood. "Right."

"Mira."

She paused at the door.

"Conscientious and sensible," Celia repeated. "In that order if possible."

Back on the floor, the overhead board had gone from yellow to a pulsing orange that made everyone work faster and think worse. Mira pulled LM-127B from the side rail and took the body through the staff door into hold processing, where the air dropped another five degrees and the sounds of the Annex flattened into distant machinery and tray wheels. The hold shelf ran along one wall: tagged bodies under thin plastic, waiting for paperwork, family disputes, court orders, or whatever other form of delayed violence the city could invent. Celia wanted it clear because a full shelf meant attention. Mira understood that. She also understood that attention had already found this unit whether anyone admitted it or not.

She laid LM-127B on the secondary scan cradle and peeled back the neck seal completely. The port at the nape stared up like a small round wound with tidy engineering around it. She checked the thighs again under cooler light. The bruises were real, layered in time. One was nearly fresh, and one was older. She checked the mouth, the ear notch, the rib scar. Under the magnifier the scar tissue showed mature collagen alignment, not cosmetic surface texturing. It had not been printed to look healed. It was healed.

As the scan model turned on the screen, Mira recognized the other cheap-stock ugliness she had signed off before: freckle maps borrowed from the wrong donor template, hands that kept curling around tools nobody had leased with them, uterine builds cramping from a wash that had not cleaned house. Claims called those nuisance carryover unless a lawyer called them something worse. The reinforcement blooming around this seam was not nuisance. It was a body grown around old damage.

She keyed in a deep residue review. The machine asked for disposal confirmation before proceeding because it was built on the assumption that nobody wanted to know more about a doomed body than billing required. She confirmed. Then, before the scan started, she opened the maintenance tab, slid a blank calibration wafer from the drawer under the cradle, and palmed it into the side port where the machine wrote temporary cache during service checks. Training staff used wafers like that to teach new adjusters how to grade bleed. Taking one was not theft in any way the company respected. Using it here was.

The scan arm descended with a mosquito whine.

Light moved under LM-127B's skin in a slow grid. The wall monitor filled with tissue density maps, port logs, wash abrasions, rental remap signatures. Mira watched the highlights bloom around the rib scar. There was internal reinforcement there too, not enough to alter function, just enough to prove the body had been grown around the seam rather than cut after printing. It had not been modified. It had been authored.

Her hands stayed very still on the console.

The mnemonic packet opened deeper this time, and it was not just the pool.

Heat pressed on bare shoulders. Rough municipal concrete chewed shallow crescents into wet toes. A child breathed too fast. The chemical bite of chlorine and sun-baked sunscreen sat at the back of the tongue. The boy in orange trunks laughed because he was still above water right up until he wasn't. Adults shifted from annoyance to panic in one ugly second. Someone dropped a ring of keys. Somebody yelled a name that the packet lost in static. Then the perspective lurched forward, a child's own body unable to decide whether to run or jump, and the certainty came huge and immediate that this was how a day split open and stayed split.

Mira yanked her hands back from the console as if the surface had gone hot. The packet cut. The room returned in pieces: coolant hum, tray wheels somewhere beyond the door, her own breath rasping loud in the cold.

Post-lease arousal haze came smeared and stupid. This packet had bones. The memory carried structure, the scar carried internal reinforcement, and together they said the unit had been grown around somebody else's past.

Her first thought was practical because that was how she had been trained by poverty and office work both: if this got kicked up, who would own the paperwork. Factory would bury it. Premium services would deny it. Claims would document the visible damage and let the rest go down the chute because no fee schedule accounted for history.

Her second thought came meaner and more honest. CommonFrame sold blank stock to people who wanted not to be themselves for a while. What if the bargain line was only blank at the price point where workers like Mira had to call it blank? What if cheap bodies came loaded because loaded was cheaper than clean?

The scan completed with a cheerful tone that made her want to hit the machine.

`Temporary cache available,` the screen informed her. `Clear for disposal.`

She copied the packet to the calibration wafer and watched the progress bar crawl. Six percent. Twenty-one. Fifty-eight. She listened for footsteps the whole time and heard only the distant metal cough of the pulp chute cycling somewhere below. When the copy finished she slid the wafer free, wiped it once on her sleeve out of useless instinct, and tucked it into the folded hem inside her left cuff where the plastic sat flat against her skin.

Now came the part that counted.

The classification screen offered its usual menu in tidy gray boxes:

`renter damage`

`unauthorized modification`

`manufacturing variance`

`mnemonic contamination`

`biohazard disposal`

`administrative hold`

Anything honest would move the body toward attention she could not control. Anything efficient would kill the file. Mira looked through the narrow hold-room window at the shelf and saw all the other units she had signed away in her years here, every one a solved problem as long as the paperwork balanced. The assistant in the car saying burner shell came back to her next, making the night's damage sound morally compostable. Then the little hand slapped wet tile again, followed by the scar the factory had pretended not to print.

Mira selected `administrative hold`.

The system opened a smaller box asking for reason. She entered: `chain discrepancy between production schema and physical unit; disposal suspended pending verification`. The lie had enough truth in it to survive first glance. It still could not explain why her pulse had shifted into the hard exact rhythm it took when she knew she was about to cost herself money.

Expected duration, the system asked.

`72 hours`.

Then the screen asked for an authorizing supervisor.

Mira hesitated, then entered Celia's general floor code for provisional review, the one used when managers gave verbal instruction and did not want to come downstairs to sign for it themselves. The system accepted it. The soft confirmation chime dropped straight through her stomach.

She printed the hold tag, looped it around the body's wrist, and watched the shelf light at station H-9 turn amber.

The change was small and quiet, and it felt irreversible enough.

LM-127B slid into the hold berth with the other delayed bodies, but now it looked less like inventory than a timer she had started herself.

Outside the hold room the Claims Annex kept feeding the weekend. Someone laughed. Someone cried at a counter. A printer spat out invoices fast as breath. Mira stood with one hand over the hidden wafer in her cuff and understood, not fully and not yet in words she could defend, that the irreversible part was not the body. It was the theft in her sleeve, Celia's code in the log, and the seventy-two hours she had carved out against procedure. The cuff pressed the wafer's edge hard against her wrist with every pulse. Seventy-two hours, she thought, and then made herself move.

She logged the hold, closed the screen, and went back out to the floor before she could talk herself into being sensible. H-9 would sit amber through the weekend if nobody got ambitious.


# Chapter 2

By Tuesday afternoon the appeals room smelled like filtered air losing the fight. Solvent and coolant still came first, the clean corporate lie, but under it the damaged bargain frame on cradle three kept giving off the sweet edge of warmed tissue, sugar left too long on a burner. The H-9 hold light from the weekend kept burning at the back of Mira's head. On Mira's monitor a yellow bar counted down twenty-six minutes until provisional liability posted to the renter's ledger.

Nidia Solis sat across from her in a housekeeping polo the color of weak tea, the hotel name stitched over one breast in gold thread already gone gray with bleach. Her real wrists were strapped in pharmacy braces that had been tightened with safety pins from the tiny sewing kits nicer guests left unopened beside minibars. One of those kits had spilled in her lap while she dug for paperwork. The spill held black thread, white thread, and two pearl buttons the size of aspirin. She had lined them up in a row on Mira's desk without seeming to notice she was doing it.

"I told intake the shoulder was wrong when I picked it up," she said. "It clicked before I got to the tram."

Mira rotated the scan. The bargain body on the cradle was female-coded, medium build, house line musculature, the right arm hanging away from the torso with the sloppy give of a torn support web. Fresh damage glowed angry on the upper layers. Beneath it, buried where a pickup booth camera never would have caught it, an older band of puckered matrix ran around the socket like a healed burn.

If she submitted the scan as it was, the system would do what it always did with undocumented older damage. It would turn the body's history into the renter's fault.

"The pickup attendant note says no visible impairment at release," Mira said. Her public voice came out dry and almost courteous, like she was offering an umbrella instead of the sentence. "Did you sign the acceptance screen?"

Nidia barked one laugh that scraped the room raw. "The one that's already signed when they hand you the stylus? Yeah. I signed that."

On the monitor the pricing ladder waited behind a red triangle. Mira knew the categories by touch now: preexisting substrate irregularity, undisclosed structural anomaly, unauthorized load stress if the system decided the renter had asked too much of leased flesh printed for cheaper work than the work it got used for. Every choice had a dollar amount tucked behind it like a knife folded into a sleeve.

She touched the older scar band on the scan, zoomed in, and let the software estimate age. The answer came back broad enough to protect the company and specific enough to hurt the poor: prior to current rental window, not verifiable to manufacturer release state.

Not verifiable meant billable.

Nidia watched Mira's face the way people watched weather rolling toward laundry strung outside. "Just tell me the number."

Mira clicked through the preliminary fee tree because not clicking through it did not make it less real. The figures populated in stacked white boxes.

Frame retrieval and bench intake: $410.

Joint-lattice reconstruction, estimate only: $1,160.

Tissue contamination scrub: $275.

Manual provenance review, customer-contestable: $340.

Service interruption penalty and temporary account suspension: $1,480.

Security deposit forfeiture pending adjudication: $300.

Provisional total customer liability: $3,965.

The number sat there in clean corporate sans-serif, almost tasteful. Mira made about a quarter of that in a week if payroll had not found a new reason to shave deductions. Brine Court would have loved that number. Brine Court would have sent flowers if she ever came up with that much at once.

Nidia closed her eyes. Her mouth kept moving a second after the rest of her face had gone still, as if she was finishing some private argument. "That's for one shoulder."

"That's before collections fees if it goes to claims court."

"Jesus Christ."

Mira let the silence sit. There was nothing humane to pour into it. Behind the glass wall another adjuster was explaining contamination surcharges to a man in a marina jacket while the man smiled the frightened smile of somebody determined to stay agreeable in front of a machine that could ruin him. The room's white-noise emitters hushed and hissed. From deeper in the annex came the clack of cradle locks and the soft wet slap of a returned body being turned for scan.

Nidia opened her eyes again. They were bloodshot in the corners and bright with the kind of fatigue that had got baked in overnight, not from one bad morning. "If I ask for review, does that stop it?"

It stopped nothing. It froze the debt long enough to wrap it in more language.

Mira kept her hands flat on the desk because if she started rubbing at her cuticles she would look as tired as she felt. "It pauses final posting for up to forty-eight hours. It also creates an adjudication file. There's a fee attached."

"Another fee."

"Yes."

"And then what?"

"Then they look at release records, shift class, strain pattern, maintenance history." Mira heard herself using the house nouns and hated how smooth they sounded. "If they find manufacturer defect, coverage applies. If they don't, the charge stands."

Past Mira's shoulder, the frame on the cradle caught Nidia like a hook. Its face was slack and beautiful in the budget way CommonFrame did beautiful, broad symmetry stamped out to survive strangers. Dried disinfectant had left a milky line along the neck port. "And while they look, I can't book another one."

That was not a question. Mira checked the side panel anyway because ritual mattered in rooms like this. The account flag showed bargain line and labor-class user. She was right.

"Your access would suspend during review."

"So I can't work."

"Not through CommonFrame."

Nidia breathed in through her nose and then stopped because the appeals room had no air worth taking. "I clean rooms at the Saint Meridian. Twelve floors if we're short. Fourteen if the convention people leave early and management gets greedy. I don't mean I supervise the cleaners. I mean I strip sheets and scrub the glass showers where rich assholes shave and leave little gray hairs around the drain like they're doing you a favor. I need the rentals because these" - she lifted both braced wrists a little off her lap - "these won't hold a spray bottle by hour three."

The turn came out plain. It came with no performance and no plea, just the fact laid on the desk between the thread cards and the fee boxes.

Mira had asked hundreds of renters what happened to the frame. Most of them lied from vanity, panic, or habit. Nidia had the scraped-clean air of somebody who'd run out of spare invention somewhere around dawn.

"Show me," Mira said before she could decide whether she should.

Nidia's mouth tightened. Humiliation moved over her face in a shallow wave, quick and visible. Then she put the sewing kit aside and worried at the safety pins one by one with clumsy fingertips. The braces loosened. Her wrists underneath were narrow in the ugly way repeated pain made things narrow. The skin at the base of both thumbs was shiny and overworked. Old steroid injection bruises bloomed green-brown along the inside of one forearm. When she turned her hands over, Mira saw the slight wrong angle in the right wrist where something had set and kept setting itself badly.

"Tendon burn first," Nidia said. "Then the tunnel shit. Then a manager who liked to tell us everybody's body hurts if they want to stay employed. I can still fold napkins. I can still do receipts. I can't wring out a rag and I sure as hell can't lift wet linen all day. So I lease." She nodded toward the rental frame. "That one was my Tuesday-through-Thursday body. Ten-hour housekeeping class. I was on eleven when the arm gave. I had both hands under a king duvet and then I didn't."

The older damage in the scan kept snagging at Mira: chronic strain and scar tissue printed over bad substrate or old injury. The shoulder had been compromised before Nidia ever signed the stylus. CommonFrame had sold her three days of borrowed strength with a break already hidden inside it.

"Did you report the clicking at pickup?"

"I told the kid in the booth the arm sounded cheap."

"Did he log it?"

Nidia gave her a look. "He told me they all sound cheap until they warm up."

Of course he had. A note at release would have made the frame harder to bill later.

The yellow countdown bar slid another notch to the right. Twenty-one minutes.

Mira clicked open the release-booth retention policy anyway, because there were still forms a person had to perform belief in before they stopped believing. Standard pickup footage for bargain labor lines was kept seventy-two hours absent incident escalation. Nidia's rental had released four days ago. Even if it had still existed, review would take longer than a woman with dead wrists and hotel floors to clean could afford.

"If I fight it in court?" Nidia asked.

Mira pictured Municipal Arbitration Hall in the civic core, all polished stone and water stations and seats designed to look generous while they ground a day of wages out of anyone who sat in them. She pictured Nidia there in her tea-colored polo, wrists strapped, taking a number beneath screens that called her liability exposure a neutral matter.

"You'd need the release footage, your shift logs, any complaint made at pickup, maybe a third-party tissue read if you wanted to challenge their age estimate."

"How much is that?"

"More than you want it to be."

Nidia leaned back. The vinyl chair gave a small farting sigh under her. "You people always say it soft."

Mira bit the inside of her cheek. "They train us to say it soft."

Nidia worried the row of tiny thread cards, nudging the black one out of line with a fingernail. "The funny thing is I like the high floors. Everybody thinks I like the tips. It's the windows. You get a slice of the harbor between towers if the weather's clear. Sometimes the ferries look like toys from up there. I rent for the windows as much as the money. My own body's five-two. You spend all day in a service corridor at that height, you start thinking the city forgot to build any sky."

There it was, the detail that had nothing to do with leverage and everything to do with being alive. Mira took it in like something she had no right to keep.

She rechecked the shoulder. The older matrix band had the dense pearled look she had started noticing too often lately, damage or memory or growth trouble nobody wanted to call by a manufacturer name. She thought of the held model in H-9, the undocumented scar under pale ribs, the image copy hidden in her locker. The company line had already cracked in one place. Now here it was again, except this time the crack had a hotel laundry card clipped to it and a woman's wrists tied up in cheap elastic.

"If I don't sign anything," Nidia said, "what happens at twenty minutes?"

"The file moves to provisional capture. Your deposit goes. Your account suspends. Collections mail follows if you don't settle or request review."

"And if I request review, I still lose the account."

"During review."

"Which means I still lose the job."

The yes sat in Mira's throat and spoiled there. Every office version of it turned glossy the second it reached language.

Outside, somebody laughed too loud in the intake queue and got immediately shushed by a receptionist. The sound came through the glass flattened and embarrassed. The appeals room was built to make everyone lower their voice. Pain, debt, and sex residue all had to pass through it at conversational volume.

Nidia pulled the braces back on with slow, ugly effort. Mira watched her fail to close one strap, then watched her use her teeth on it. That decided more than the numbers had.

She clicked away from the fee tree before the system could autosave the preliminary valuation. "Don't sign the liability consent."

Nidia's eyes narrowed. "You saying that because you're kind or because there's some other trap."

"Because once it posts cleanly, I can't slow it without making it worse."

"Slow it how."

Mira opened the manual exception menu. Technical review. Bench re-evaluation. Tissue aging variance. All of them came with audit hooks. She chose the one that bought the most time while sounding the dullest.

`Request: additional substrate aging analysis prior to submission.`

The system asked for justification.

She typed: `Age spread inconsistent with visible tear. Requires local bench comparison before adjudication.`

That was true, which made it easier to lie with.

"This gives me until morning," she said. "Maybe less if somebody upstairs gets ambitious."

"And in the morning?"

Mira clicked confirm and felt the file move from easy corporate certainty into a folder with her name on it. "In the morning I need the shoulder to stop looking like this."

Nidia stared at her. "You got a miracle drawer under there, Ms. Alves?"

One corner of Mira's mouth tried for it and failed. "No."

She closed the scan, printed the tissue layers to old paper because paper still moved through offices quieter than data, and slid the sheets face down into a generic intake folder. Her own reflection flickered in the black of the monitor between windows, dark hair tied back too tight, mole near the temple, mouth already set for whatever bad idea came next.

She had not called Tavi Sloan in three years because a claims adjuster keeping a black-market repairer's number was the kind of stupidity that deserved unemployment. The number still sat in the analog pocket of her wallet, written on the back of a tram receipt by a dock clerk whose illegal knee patch had fooled intake long enough to save him from bankruptcy. Mira had kept it because curiosity and class fear often lived in the same place. You did not throw away exits just because you were still pretending to be indoors.

"Can you get to South Canal after eight?" she asked.

Nidia blinked. "For what?"

"For a second look."

"By who?"

"Don't ask me anything yet that makes me answer out loud."

That got Nidia's attention in a way the fee tree had not. Her face sharpened, pain and suspicion lining up instead of fighting each other. "How much?"

"Bring whatever cash you can stand to lose. Not cards. Cash."

"You're serious."

The damaged shoulder sagged on the cradle. Inside the torn socket, the older scar sat there waiting for the company to call it unverifiable and billable. "More than I should be."

Nidia's throat worked. "If this is some kind of setup-"

"Then you've already got the wrong shoes for it." Mira nodded at the foam slippers peeking from under the chair, patterned with faded cherries. They were clean on top and grimed at the soles, house slippers doing street work because there was no spare pair. "Be at South Canal and Mercer. Blue sign that says laundry. Eight-thirty. If I'm not there by nine, go home and don't answer any collection call until you hear my voice first."

Nidia searched her face like she was checking an unlabeled bottle for poison by smell alone. "Why?"

The honest answer had too many moving parts already. The useful answer was smaller.

"Because that shoulder is older than your contract and they know how to hide that in the wording," Mira said. "And because if I send this up clean, review will turn your wrists into a debt instrument by breakfast."

Nidia let out a breath that shook once at the end. "That's a real sentence."

"I know."

She gathered the spilled sewing-kit pieces with awkward braces and tucked them back into her tote. Before she stood she hesitated, looking again at the rental frame on the cradle.

"If somebody can fix it," she said, "don't make it fancy. I just need it to lift linen. Fancy always breaks faster."

Mira nodded. The line landed harder than anything else had. Around here survival stayed brutally specific: lift the linen, finish the shift, make it to payday.

After Nidia left, the appeals room seemed colder because there was no longer anyone in it trying not to look humiliated. Mira stood with the intake folder under one hand and the timer on her screen running silently toward tomorrow's trouble. Across the annex, cradles moved in steady sequence toward wash, pulp, resale. Claims was not the place where the city's body economy became cruel. Claims was the place where cruelty got grammar.

She slipped the printout into her bag, logged the frame to overnight local review, and spent the rest of her shift hearing the hotel's high floors in the back of her head whenever somebody said premium view surcharge or elevated leisure package or any other phrase that turned somebody else's sky into a line item.

At seven fifty-eight, with the office thinning and the canal outside going the color of old cutlery, she signed the damaged frame out of the bench queue under a waste-transfer code she had no right to use. Sloan's number sat in the analog pocket of her wallet like a hard little splinter.

The body was heavier than its file.


# Chapter 3

Sloan's number had gotten Mira as far as Blue Hour Laundry. Forty open dryer doors breathed heat into the market dark.

The place sat on the canal market corner under a blue sign that buzzed hard enough to make the window glass tremble. Heat came out every time the front door opened, detergent heat and boiler heat and the sweeter animal note of damp cloth left too long in a basket. Under that there was Cordera itself, salt and fryer grease and tram brake dust. Her work badge stuck damp against her waistband. She stood in it with a milk crate of stolen-looking parts against her hip and watched Jo Villanueva snap a fitted sheet into obedience so sharply the woman folding beside her actually laughed and flinched at the same time.

Jo had a silver braid down her back and the hands of someone who had wrung water out of other people's lives for forty years. The skin over her knuckles looked sanded by soap. Her shoes were the practical black kind that promised no beauty and no mercy. She kept a cigarette tucked above one ear even though the city had not allowed indoor smoking in fifteen years and she had not been caught obeying anything in at least twenty.

"No," she said, before Tavi had even got the second crate through the door. "Absolutely not. You're not making my laundromat smell like cooked skin."

Tavi nudged the door shut with his heel. He had two salvage cases hanging from one hand and wore the same grin he used when the job was difficult enough to interest him and stupid enough to insult him. "Good evening to you too, Jo."

"You can have good evening when I stop hearing from three different women that some claims girl and my upstairs disappointment fixed a hotel cleaner in a room with no license."

Mira shifted the crate before it cut deeper into her forearm. The top layer held tubing, clamps, and a cracked sterile hood whose sterility had become mostly spiritual. "We need a room with running water," she said. "A drain, a locking door, and a circuit that can take a pump."

Jo turned and looked at her properly then. The look wasn't unkind, but kindness had nothing to do with it. Mira knew it from claims, the quick assessment that asked what category of trouble this was and whether it paid. Jo's eyes dropped to the CommonFrame badge Mira had forgotten to take off her waistband. They came back up.

"You need a church," Jo said. "I've got a laundromat."

The front room was full. A man in restaurant whites fed quarters into a machine with one hand while reading game scores off his wristpad with the other. A girl in a green school polo did algebra on the lid of washer nine and licked powdered cheese from her thumb between steps. At the back, an older woman in leopard-print slippers had claimed an entire folding table for hemming a church dress and looked up only to enjoy the argument. Nothing in the room was private. That was exactly why Tavi had asked for it.

People saw things in laundromats and decided, together, what had happened.

Mira had spent the day at CommonFrame writing damage notes gentle enough to survive management review and ugly enough to survive court. She had eaten crackers from a break-room machine for lunch and counted, twice, how far next week's paycheck had to stretch before it reached rent. The milk crate dug into her arm where sweat had already slicked the skin. She felt stupid standing there with illegal equipment and a corporate badge on the same body.

Tavi set his cases down beside a row of humming dryers. "We're not asking for the front room. We need the back. One night to start."

"One night starts all the others." Jo looked at the crate in Mira's arms. "What exactly are you fixing back there?"

"Whatever comes in broken enough to bury somebody," Mira said.

Jo's mouth twitched. "Use a real answer, mija. 'Whatever comes in broken' is how bad churches fill folding chairs."

Mira bit the inside of her cheek. She was tired enough that honesty felt cheaper than salesmanship. "Bargain line repairs. Quiet ones. The kind people can't bring through licensed intake without getting turned into fees."

The woman in leopard slippers gave a low whistle like she'd just seen a decent card on the table.

Jo glanced toward her and raised one finger without looking. The woman went back to her hem.

"I don't want cops," Jo said. "I don't want tissue leaks in my grout. I don't want strangers dying behind my dryers and I don't want some rich idiot wandering in here because he got adventurous in a rental body and thinks the neighborhood owes him discretion."

"Nobody rich is coming to me for discount bench work," Tavi said.

"People with money love bargains almost as much as they love pretending they found them."

That was true enough that even Tavi did not answer right away.

Jo put the folded sheet into a basket, smoothed it once, and came around the table. Up close Mira could smell cloves on her breath and the ghost of smoke in her hair. "What do I get besides criminal exposure and one more thing to mop?"

Tavi opened his mouth. Mira got there first.

"A cut," she said. "Cash, up front, per case."

Jo's eyebrows went up.

Mira kept going because stopping would make it sound negotiable. "We pay for water, electric, and the room. You approve who hears about it. If it draws heat, we shut it down when you say. No arguments."

"How much?"

Mira had done the math on the walk over and hated the math because it assumed clients, parts, and luck. "One in five."

Tavi looked at her, not offended exactly, just surprised. That was fine. Surprise was cheaper than getting thrown out.

Jo sucked her teeth, considering. "One in four if you bleed on my floor."

"One in five and we clean the floor ourselves," Mira said. "Bleach, drain, linens replaced if we ruin them."

Jo let the silence hang. The dryers knocked behind them with the soft blunt rhythm of big bodies turning over in sleep.

"Who else knows?" she asked.

"Right now?" Tavi said. "A hotel cleaner with a repaired shoulder, one courier with loose morals, and whatever saints monitor bad ideas."

"That courier's name is Luz's cousin Berto," said the woman in leopard slippers, without looking up. "He has no morals and two bicycles."

Jo pointed at her. "See? This is what I mean. Nothing stays secret in a room full of socks."

The room laughed, not loudly but enough.

Mira felt herself flush, then got angry at the flush. The whole point was that nothing stayed secret in a room full of socks. Claims had taught her what silence cost when only the company got to keep records. A laundromat ran on another kind of documentation: stains noticed, tears discussed, whose card had bounced, whose kid needed a ride home. Jo was not a neutral hideout. She was a switchboard.

"Then let it spread your way," Mira said. "Call it something members hear about if they need it, not a clinic or surgery."

Jo's eyes narrowed, interested now. "Members."

"Laundry members," Tavi said, catching up. "You already have loyalty cards, don't you?"

"I've got index cards and a pencil."

"Perfect. Old-school criminal infrastructure."

Jo slapped his shoulder as she passed him, not hard but not gentle either. "If anyone asks, you're doing boiler maintenance in the back and special garment restoration for members only. Nobody says clinic. Nobody says repair desk. Nobody says CommonFrame where customers can hear it. If anybody throws up in the folding area, they clean it themselves."

She jerked her chin toward the curtained hallway behind the change machine. "Come see your kingdom."

The back room had once been storage and then, by the look of it, three or four other things after that. Metal shelves held detergent boxes, paper-wrapped lightbulbs, a crate of orphaned socks, and two ledgers thick with Jo's handwriting. A utility sink leaned under a cracked mirror. Heat from the boiler made the small room damp and soft-edged; steam gathered along the ceiling and turned the single bulb into a moon behind gauze. It was the first place Mira had stood all week that felt less like a workplace than a body cavity.

There was enough room for a folding table, a stool, and maybe two people if they liked each other or had no choice.

"This is terrible," Tavi said, with obvious affection.

"It's the room you're getting."

Mira set the crate down and looked at the floor drain, the sink taps, the outlet by the boiler. "The line's grounded?"

Jo snorted. "Mostly."

Tavi leaned to inspect the socket. "Mostly is how great art happens."

"Mostly is how you got that nose flattened," Jo said.

He touched the bridge of it reflexively and grinned. "That was actually an argument about baseball."

"That was actually an unpaid invoice."

Mira found herself smiling before she wanted to. It slipped out because fatigue had taken too much off the top of her self-control. She looked away before either of them noticed.

Jo did notice. Mira could tell by the angle of her head. But Jo only crossed her arms and said, "Here are my rules. You lock the back door. You keep the front room boring. No one under sixteen comes behind that curtain. If the Bureau or city inspections show up, you're boiler maintenance and you look offended that anybody asked. You leave my customers enough machines that they can still wash clothes, because unlike your various crimes, laundry is a basic service."

"Fair," Mira said.

"Also, if you make money in my building, you feed somebody besides yourselves once in a while. I'm not running a starvation startup."

That one landed harder than the rest. Mira thought of the crackers in her bag, of Gabe pretending he had eaten when he had only had coffee. "Fair," she said again, quieter.

Jo nodded once. "Tonight only. If I hate it by dawn, I put the room back to mops."

She started toward the curtain, then turned back. "And take off the badge, mija. You look like you came to deny a claim."

Mira unclipped the CommonFrame badge and put it face down on the shelf beside the orphaned socks.

For the first time since she'd walked in, the room felt possible.

***

The wetbench took shape the way bad neighborhoods rebuilt after storms, from whatever had not yet been hauled away.

Tavi worked fast and talked faster. The main frame had been an old clinic table with one leg replaced by stacked transit guides and a shim cut from a detergent bucket. The pump came from salvage, the filters from a fertility lab that had gone bankrupt, the tissue warmer from somewhere Tavi would not specify because the answer would either be gross or expensive and he was enjoying having one of those left ambiguous. He fitted parts together with the reverence of a mechanic resurrecting a machine no one respectable would admit still mattered.

"Hold that line up," he said. "No, higher. If it sags, it backs up and then everything smells like overcooked bone broth."

"You keep selling the experience," Mira said.

"You'd rather I called it collagen runoff? Because that is the other option."

She held the saline tube while he clamped it into place. Her hands knew bodies from the outside in, bruises graded by color, joint damage logged against liability tables, dried fluids read like weather. Repair work asked for another intimacy. Here the question was not what happened and who owed. It was what still functioned, what could be persuaded, what the body would forgive if given half a chance and cleaner tools than the city thought the poor deserved.

The room got hotter as they worked. Laundry steam seeped under the curtain and mixed with the sour-metal scent of sterilant tabs dissolving in the sink. Tavi had brought six sealant packs, four sterile sleeves, a tray of reusable needles wrapped in paper that had once been white, and half a canister of anesthetic foam whose gauge looked personally offended by how low it was.

Mira set items out in rows because rows calmed her. "This is maybe three serious cases."

"Four if nobody is dramatic."

"Nobody ever says, I can only afford a calm emergency."

"That's because you work in claims and I work in miracles."

"You work in salvage."

"Exactly. Lower overhead."

He snapped on gloves, then peered at the sterilizer setup Jo had let them build out of a stockpot, a rack, and the boiler line. "You know what our real problem is?"

Mira looked at the supplies. "Money."

"Besides money."

"Cover."

"Besides cover."

She glanced at him. "You want me to say everything."

"I do. It makes me feel accompanied."

He tapped the dry-erase board he had scavenged from behind the detergent shelf. The ghost of an old note still showed through: ORDER BLEACH. "We need rules before the first panic walks in here and lies to our faces."

Mira had been thinking the same thing since Jo said yes. Rules were what made chaos billable. They were also what let you sleep without inventing fairness from scratch every twenty minutes. She took the marker from his hand.

At the top of the board she wrote:

BLEEDING / LOAD-BEARING / RETURN-FEE RISK

"That's not a rule," Tavi said. "That's triage in block letters, which, to be fair, I respect."

"It becomes a rule if we obey it."

"Fine. Add one for idiots who wait until something smells sweet."

She wrote:

SWEET ROT JUMPS THE LINE IF FEVER

Tavi nodded, satisfied.

"Price?" she said.

He leaned against the table, thinking. His burn scar showed pale along his forearm where the glove cuff stopped. "Parts cost, plus what we can stand to ask. Cash preferred. Barter only if the barter isn't insulting."

"I can't put 'what we can stand' on the board. Give me numbers."

"Schedules are how you end up sounding like CommonFrame."

He was right and she hated that he was right. She wrote anyway.

PARTS PLUS LABOR. QUOTE BEFORE CUTTING.

"Better," he said. "No tabs unless Jo vouches."

She added it.

"No names on paper," he said.

Mira capped the marker. "Absolutely not."

"You want evidence?"

"I want follow-up."

"I want not going to jail."

She looked at the board, then at the stack of laundry tickets Jo kept by the door. Thin pink slips, numbered, carbon-backed, anonymous unless somebody chose otherwise. They looked like nothing. Which meant they could hold a lot.

"Ticket number only," she said. "Model line if they know it. Symptom, part, and whether the contract is still active."

Tavi groaned theatrically. "You're making forms."

"I'm making memory. Those are different things."

"Barely."

The curtain shoved aside before she could answer. Jo came in carrying two mugs of coffee and the expression of a woman already regretting her own generosity.

"Drink this before you both get clever," she said. "Also, your first customer is asking the front room if the boiler fixes knees."

Tavi looked at Mira over the rim of his mug. "See? Marketing."

Mira set hers down untouched. "We're not even stable yet."

"You're as stable as you can afford," Jo said. "And he's dripping on my tile."

She stepped aside.

The man behind her could not have been more than twenty-eight, but the bargain runner frame he wore had the lean overbuilt look CommonFrame sold to couriers and dock sprinters, calves braided under cheap dermal gloss, shoulders narrow for cutting wind. His left pant leg was slit from knee to cuff, and pink synovial gel had soaked through the fabric and turned the hem dark. He held a bike helmet under one arm. A tiny paper saint was tucked into the helmet foam, and one thumbnail was painted a chipped, hopeful blue.

"Berto Llamas," Jo said. "Luz's cousin. He swears he didn't fall because he was showing off."

"I wasn't showing off," Berto said. He tried to stand on the hurt leg, failed, and laughed through his teeth in a way that was almost a sob. "I was making a left."

"On what, the moon?" Jo asked.

"Subway grate by Ocho. Wheel slipped."

Mira was already looking at the wound. The kneecap housing had split on the lateral edge. Cheap shell, cheap seal, but the real problem was deeper. Every time Berto shifted, the quadricep bundle spasmed ahead of the pain, anticipatory, as if bracing for a second impact.

"Contract still active?" she asked.

"Till nine tomorrow. I've got to return it clean or they hit me with the whole limb."

"How much cash?" Tavi said.

Berto's face did the fast ashamed math poor people did when they had to name a number out loud. "Seventy-six and some transit credit."

Mira looked at Tavi. The parts alone would eat most of that.

Jo reached into her apron, slapped three folded twenties onto the tray beside the wetbench, and said, "You can owe me, not them. And I don't trust him."

"Cruel," Tavi said.

"Accurate."

Berto stared at the money. "Jo, no."

"Pay me back by bringing your aunt's sheets when she stops pretending she can do stairs."

His mouth tightened. That was the first real thing Mira saw on him, deeper than panic or pain. "She told you?"

"She tells everybody the wrong facts and me the useful ones. Sit down."

Berto sat.

The desk began because there was no room left for it not to.

***

By the time Tavi cut Berto's pant leg away, the front room had worked out that something expensive and embarrassing was happening behind the curtain.

Laundry slowed in little pockets. Voices dropped, then rose again with the strained brightness of people performing privacy for each other. Mira could hear Jo out front pushing the room back into its normal shape by force of will.

"Machine six is free if anyone wants to stop eavesdropping and wash those uniforms today," she called.

Tavi laid Berto's leg into the bench cradle and peeled back the cheap dermal seal around the knee. A pocket of fluid burped warm against Mira's glove. Beneath it, the lattice had frayed where the shell's load-bearing mesh should have taken the twist and had not.

"That's a nasty one," Tavi said. "You hit anything after the grate?"

"Just dignity."

"Unrepairable."

Berto barked a laugh, then sucked air because laughing moved the knee.

Mira checked pupil lag, pulse sync, contract port glow at the ankle. Everything was ordinary except the tremor. She rested two fingers against the runner frame's shin and felt the muscle firing before Tavi touched it, overreactive, defensive.

"Berto," she said, "any other symptoms before the fall?"

"Pain?"

"Anything strange."

"Define strange."

Tavi got the irrigation line into place. "In my profession that question has an excellent range."

Berto wet his lips. "Twice this week I got on the platform at Mercado and my whole body locked up. I don't even take trains when I'm working, but suddenly I was sure my foot was stuck between the train and the edge. Like really sure. I could feel the sole peeling off. Smelled hot wire and piss and..." He swallowed. "I thought it was low sugar."

Mira looked up.

"Did it happen after the fall?" she asked.

"Worse after. On the walk here I heard the tram and nearly threw up."

Tavi's eyes flicked to hers for half a second. It was enough. He had heard it too. This wasn't a pain response or ordinary panic. Something had been carried in.

He said, deliberately casual, "Any history of seizure flags on the contract?"

"No. Clean rental." Berto tried to lift his head and look at the wound. Mira put a hand lightly on his shoulder to keep him back. He smelled like chain grease, sweat, and the mint gum he was chewing too fast. "Is it bad bad?"

"It's expensive bad," Mira said. "We're aiming for less expensive."

She heard herself and knew it was a claims sentence, dry enough to travel. Berto almost smiled anyway. Sometimes process language was mercy if it gave pain a number-shaped edge.

Tavi ran the scanner wand over the joint. The cheap display lit the torn mesh in jerking red. Around the tear, faint static fluttered through the superficial signal map. It wasn't supposed to be there. Mira had seen residue artifacts on claims scans, little storms in tissue that should have been fully washed. This one pulsed when the scanner tone rose, as if the body recognized not the machine but the remembered approach of something larger.

Berto heard it too.

His whole leg kicked. "Jesus Christ, platform."

The words came out before he seemed to know he was saying them. He grabbed for the table edge, eyes wide, and Mira felt the panic transfer through the bench frame into her own wrists. For a second the little room narrowed to metal lip, bad angle, the sensation of a shoe sole smearing away under weight that was about to become train weight. It did not belong to her. It did not belong to Berto either. But it touched the room anyway.

Tavi killed the scanner tone.

Silence came down hard. Out front a dryer door slammed. Someone laughed too loudly.

"Okay," Tavi said, gentler now. "Okay. Stay with me. You're here, not there. Knee first, ghosts later."

"I don't have ghosts," Berto said, breathing fast.

"Sure," Tavi said. "Bad wording. Sorry."

Mira reached for the anesthetic foam, checked the gauge, and used less than she wanted. "Listen to me. We can patch the load-bearing damage tonight. The other thing, the platform panic, we can damp some of it if it rides the local lattice. But if this model is carrying overlap, it may flare under stress."

"Overlap," Berto repeated, blank.

"Borrowed residue," she said. "Things not yours."

"Can you take it out?"

The honest answer was not with what they had in a laundry back room on seventy-six dollars and Jo's loan.

"We can get you through return without the knee collapsing," Mira said. "And we can tell you what to watch for next time."

Berto shut his eyes. "Fine. Do it. I need the body till nine."

Tavi worked then with the focus Mira had first noticed in the cramped room where they fixed the hotel cleaner. Outside the joke, outside the performance, he became nearly tender with ruined tissue. He trimmed frayed mesh, laid fresh lattice like careful patchwork, sealed the torn housing in layers so the stress would distribute instead of splitting again. He talked while he worked, not because he needed to hear himself but because the patient did.

"This part's ugly but boring. If I say rude things about CommonFrame engineers, that's for my morale, not because you're dying. Hold still. There you go. That tendon line is cheap as paper, by the way. If you sprint in the rain, they're basically begging to delaminate."

"You say that like I chose the deluxe package," Berto muttered.

"Nobody in this room chose the deluxe package."

Mira irrigated, blotted, checked seal integrity. She could feel the shape of the work settling into her hands, less foreign each minute. Claims had made bodies into documents. Repair made documents into flesh again, or something close enough to keep a person earning.

When she placed her palm above Berto's kneecap to steady the joint for sealant, another jolt ran through the muscle. Weight hit damp concrete. A thigh was already shaking. Somebody behind him shouted not to look down. The borrowed sensation was so quick it barely deserved the name memory. It was still enough to turn the room colder in her mind.

Pattern, she thought.

One impossible model on a Friday shelf she could have lied to herself about. A pattern meant queue.

She wanted that not to be true because it scaled fast. Whatever CommonFrame was doing in the bargain line had already leaked into the city hard enough that couriers were puking at tram bells and calling it low sugar.

Tavi sealed the final layer and sat back. "Try weight."

Berto eased off the table. The repaired knee held. He bent once, twice, face pinched, then nodded. "Shit. Okay. Okay, that's..." He looked from Tavi to Mira and seemed to realize gratitude would cost him more than he had cash for. "That helps."

"Return it slow," Mira said. "Don't sprint on wet metal. If you hear rail noise and your stomach drops, step away from the platform edge before your body decides something for you."

"That sentence is terrible."

"It's also useful."

Berto dug the seventy-six from his pocket in damp folded bills and transit chips. Jo took her sixty back out of the pile without comment and pushed the rest toward Mira. Tavi counted parts on his fingers and made a face like a man swallowing his principles whole.

"Next time," he said, "you come in before the joint opens like a peach."

"Next time I come in for a tune-up, not a harvest."

"That costs extra."

As Berto limped out through the curtain, Jo was already intercepting him.

"You tell anyone useful?" she asked.

"Only people who need it."

"Useful to who? Give me names."

"Luz. My aunt. Maybe Pilar if her hand still does the weird church thing."

Jo closed her eyes briefly, as if consulting a patient god. "Fine. But they come with laundry."

Mira looked at the board, then added another line under the rest.

SAY THE WEIRD PART FIRST

Tavi read it and laughed under his breath. "That might be the whole business plan."

She did not laugh. The scanner's little red flutter was still in her head like afterimage. "How many times have you seen that?"

He wiped his hands on a towel and avoided the question by answering a narrower one. "Enough that I hate bargain-line knees on principle."

"I asked for a count, not a principle."

He met her eyes then. Humor still on the surface, but thinner. "Tonight's not the night for a lecture, Mira. Tonight's the night for keeping the room upright."

That, she thought, was also not an answer.

Before she could push him harder, the curtain moved again.

Jo put her head through. "Congratulations. Pilar brought laundry."

Behind her, Mira saw the first edge of a line.

***

The next six hours made rulemaking feel less like governance than triage with handwriting.

Jo took the front room by the throat and turned it into cover. Every person waiting for the back got a pink laundry ticket, a wire basket, and some version of an instruction. Buy soap. Fold something. Sit near machine four and look bored. If anybody asked, Blue Hour had started a members-only garment restoration service for delicate fabrics and private embarrassments. The story improved each time she told it. By midnight it had acquired a waiting list and a loyalty discount.

People obeyed because Jo sounded like the kind of woman you obeyed if you liked surviving community spaces.

Pilar came first. She was a Dominican hair braider with lemon-yellow glasses and a tiny tattoo of a safety pin behind one ear. She carried a mesh laundry bag full of salon capes and held her left hand close to her chest like a bird she did not trust. The hand itself was uninjured. That was the problem.

"It keeps doing this," she said.

She set a washcloth on the table to demonstrate. Her fingers found the corners, folded, rolled, and twisted the thing into a pressure bandage so fast Mira barely tracked the steps.

"You braid for a living," Mira said.

"I do heads, not trauma."

"Maybe you contain multitudes," Tavi said.

Pilar did not smile. "I was doing a christening set this afternoon and my hand tied off a vessel knot on a baby's dress ribbon. A real one. I know because my brother's a medic and he almost cried laughing. Then at dinner I reached for my glass and my whole arm wanted to brace for impact. I haven't braced for impact in my life, unless you count my first marriage."

That got a short, grateful snort out of Jo, who was leaning in the doorway with her ledger open against her stomach.

Mira checked the port at Pilar's wrist and the superficial motor map. There was no structural damage, just a noisy loop in the borrowed response layer. It was a cheap body with an active contract through Monday morning. The return-fee risk would be high if she reported "uncommanded procedural reflexes" through official channels. CommonFrame would translate that into instability, then debt.

"We can damp the loop," Tavi said. "We can't scrub it clean tonight."

"How much?"

He named a number. Pilar winced, then reached into her bag and added three packets of salon dye beside the cash. "My cousin imports these without taxes. They're worth more than they look."

Tavi examined the packets. "What color?"

"Copper smoke."

"That means nothing."

"It means women pay extra."

"Accepted."

While he worked the dampener patch across her forearm, Pilar told Jo from the table that she had finally left the man who hated her glasses because he said they made her look smarter than him. Jo said any man threatened by corrective lenses deserved his own extinction event. Mira found herself studying Pilar's face while the patch took hold. There was nothing generic about her. She had a tiny notch in one front tooth, probably from childhood, and a habit of tapping her ring finger twice against any hard surface before saying something true.

When the patch settled, the twitch in her hand eased.

"If it starts doing emergency medicine again," Mira said, "step away from whatever sharp thing you're holding."

"You say that like there is a version where I don't hold sharp things."

"Then at least point them away from customers."

Pilar left with her salon capes, two boxes of detergent for appearances, and the expression of somebody not exactly reassured but at least no longer alone with the problem.

After her came a newlywed called Saira Quon in a pearl-pinned scarf and a hotel robe under her street coat because she had dressed in a hurry and had not realized until the laundromat line noticed. She was twenty-three at most, with henna still dark on her hands and the red pressure marks of too-tight ceremonial bangles circling her wrists. Her husband stayed in the front room with a basket of towels on his lap and the fixed, desperate politeness of a man who would rather fight a bus than discuss his honeymoon with strangers.

Saira did not sit until Jo pulled the curtain fully closed.

"I'm not hurt," she said at once. "I mean, not torn. I checked. I just..." She swallowed and made herself continue. "I rented the upgraded body for two nights because my mother said if I was only doing this once I should do it properly, and now every time he touches the back of my neck my stomach drops out and everything below my waist goes rigid like I'm waiting for bad news. It's not his fault."

Mira did not ask whether the mother had helped pay. She did not need to. The robe cuffs were monogrammed with a hotel logo so fancy it practically sneered. The wedding body itself was a bargain-adjacent glamour line, smoother skin, better cheekbone architecture, cheaper internal calibration than the premium brochures promised. Mira had seen the upsell sheets: Romance Package, Confidence Suite, Memory-Muted.

Tavi crouched to scan the lumbar signal. "Could be residue caught in the parasympathetic loops. Could be the body's original stress scripts lighting up under intimacy. Could be your husband is bad with his hands."

Saira stared at him, horrified.

"Ignore him," Mira said.

"I help by widening the sample set."

"No, you don't."

Saira made an unwilling noise that was almost a laugh and then covered her mouth with the heel of one hand. "He's not bad," she said into her palm. "He's careful. That's almost the problem."

Jo, from the doorway: "Men are often careful exactly where they should be useful."

That got the laugh the room had been circling.

Once Saira settled enough to let them work, the problem proved real and ugly in a way Mira recognized. The rear cervical contact points lit with a stress cascade that did not match Saira's breathing or voluntary response. Somebody else's fear was sitting in the body like a hand over the mouth. No consciousness rode in it, nothing that whole. All it held was a preserved flinch with nowhere to go.

Mira hated the specificity of it because specificity made the company harder to excuse.

She held the scanner steady while Tavi laid a soft suppressor strip along the neck and upper spine. "This won't make the body brave," he said. "It'll just stop it from borrowing the wrong caution."

Saira nodded too fast. "That's enough. Brave is my problem."

When the suppressor synced, the body's involuntary lock released in a visible unwinding. Saira sat very still, feeling the difference. Her eyes filled before she looked up, furious at herself for it.

"Don't cry in front of your husband if you want him to keep being normal," Jo advised.

"Jo," Mira said.

"I'm helping. Men panic when women cry in robes."

Saira laughed again, more openly this time. "You sound like my aunt."

"Your aunt sounds competent."

At the door Saira hesitated, then reached into the robe pocket and produced a tiny frosted cake from the hotel minibar, still boxed. "We can't eat this now," she said. "He's too nervous and I'm too embarrassed. Take it."

Jo took it with solemn gratitude. "This is the nicest fee anyone has paid me tonight."

By one in the morning the back room had acquired layers. Used wrappers sat in one bin, clean instruments in another, pink tickets clipped along a string over the sink, marker rules crowding the board. Mira added categories as the cases educated them.

NO PHOTOS

CONTRACT ACTIVE? SAY SO

IF THE BODY HEARS SOMETHING YOU DON'T, TELL US

Tavi added one underneath in bigger letters:

IF YOU WAIT TILL IT ROTS, I WILL MOCK YOU

Jo underlined it twice.

At 1:17 Jo shoved the curtain open with her hip. "I need a yes or a no before she paints my floor."
