The woman behind her wore grocery-delivery green gone black under the right ribs. A dish towel was knotted hard around her waist. Each breath pressed pink gel through the cloth in slow glossy beads. She was maybe thirty, with rain-plastered hair and one delivery-app bud still lodged in her ear as if denial needed audio support.

"I can still finish the route," she said.

"That's not what I asked," Mira said.

"Neither did Jo," Tavi said, already moving.

"She's Nessa Aguirre," Jo said. "Third-floor walk-up, case of bottled water, heroic stupidity. She kept telling me it's just leaking a little."

Nessa untied the towel. The bargain stair-flex body had opened along the load seam under the lowest right rib, not a clean cut but a peeled split where cheap shell had sheared off the mesh below. Clear gel and diluted blood glossed the opening. Every inhale widened it another millimeter. The room filled with the sweet-rancid smell of stressed tissue and hot plastic.

"Contract active?" Mira asked.

"Till eight. If I return it like this, they bill me the whole torso."

Mira looked at the tray. Three sleeve wrappers already sagged in the bio bin. One sterile sleeve remained in its paper. The foam gauge had sunk into the red.

"How honest do you want it?" Tavi asked quietly.

"Clinically," she said.

He scrubbed his hands again and flicked the foam canister with a fingernail. "Proper internal weave takes both of us gloved and more anesthetic than that sad little hiss. What we can do is irrigate, lay surface lattice, seal the seam, and bind her hard enough to keep it from opening if she goes straight home."

Nessa's face tightened. "I can't go straight home."

"You can," Mira said. "You can't finish the route. If you lift with that side again, it'll unzip to the mesh and then you'll owe them a torso and a sermon."

"Price?" Jo said.

Tavi named it. Lower than the labor. Higher than mercy.

Nessa flinched, then nodded once. "Do the ugly version."

Mira wanted to tell her there was no version tonight that wasn't ugly. Instead she washed in, set out irrigation, and watched Tavi pull on the last sterile sleeve.

"This is the last clean one," she said.

"I noticed," he said. "Let's act like professionals till we run out of costume."

The foam gave them one pale ribbon across the split and died there. Tavi looked at the dead canister, then at Nessa. "That was all the polite chemistry."

Nessa laughed once, sharp with fear. "Great."

"After this," Mira said, "you get pressure, not comfort. If you need to yell, do it before he anchors the mesh."

"I can hear you both."

"Good," Jo said. "Then we're all aligned."

Tavi had to work one-handed inside the seam, guiding forceps with the sleeved hand while Mira held retractors and gauze at the edge because they could not afford a second person going in dirty. He hated the compromise; she could feel it in the clipped precision of his breathing. Nessa bit down on the rolled washcloth Jo gave her and still made a wet animal sound when he set the first anchor. Her heel slammed the pump stand. The suction line skipped. Mira caught the basin before it tipped, but the motion jerked the curtain half open.

Five faces appeared in the gap. One of them said, "Holy shit," with the full devotional force the phrase deserved.

Jo moved fast enough to blur. She hit the curtain shut with her forearm and turned on the room. "If you aren't the body on this table, you don't touch that fabric. Anybody who peeks loses their place and folds towels till dawn. You hear me?"

Silence answered her, then the timid clink of quarters.

Nessa was crying now without seeming to know it. The tears ran sideways into her hair. "Don't stop," she said around the washcloth. "Please don't stop halfway."

"We're not stopping halfway," Mira said. "We're stopping at what we can close."

That was the truest promise she had in stock. She held to it while Tavi laid external lattice over the split instead of the deeper weave he wanted, sealed the seam in stubborn glossy strips, and cinched a compression binder around Nessa's ribs so tight the woman swore at all three of them and then at whatever god had invented stair deliveries.

When it was done, the tear sat flattened under patchwork and pressure instead of properly repaired. It would hold if Nessa treated her own torso like borrowed glass. It would fail if she forgot.

Tavi peeled off the ruined sleeve and dropped it into the bin. "That's it," he said. "No more open work tonight unless somebody has a miraculous relationship with sterile packaging."

Mira looked at the inside-out sleeve in the waste and felt the night narrow. "Then we stop pretending otherwise."

Nessa swung her legs off the bench slowly, one palm clamped to the binder. "Can I make the return?"

"You can try," Mira said. "You can't run, lift, twist, or be brave for capitalism. If it starts leaking warm, you sit down wherever you are and call somebody who likes you."

"That's a very short list."

Jo held out the pink ticket. "Then don't waste it."

While Nessa counted out damp bills and transit credit with shaking fingers, Jo disappeared into the front room and came back dragging a narrow cashier table from storage. She wedged it outside the curtain, planted the cigar tin on top, stacked pink tickets beside it, and sat down like a woman assuming office.

"New rules," she called. "You tell me if it's open or weird before you get near my curtain. One body past the fabric at a time. If they say morning, you take morning. If you're waiting, you wash something."

Mira went to the board and wrote two more lines in block letters:

ONE BODY PAST THE CURTAIN

NO OPEN WORK AFTER LAST SLEEVE

The new rule got tested within minutes. The teenage busboy with the immaculate side part and the fresh braces stepped up to the table holding his left elbow under a bar towel. He had caught the prep-body on a steam-table hinge and waited because he thought leaking did not count as broken if the joint still bent.

Jo looked at the towel, then at the board. "Morning," she said.

His face went blank with the kind of fear only debt could make that fast. "I start breakfast at six."

Mira checked the elbow at the sink. The seam was small, crescent-shaped, wet at the edges. Fixable in ten clean minutes with supplies they no longer had.

"Listen carefully," she said. "I can wash it, seal the surface, brace it, and keep it from opening wider. I can't repair it properly tonight."

"Will it hold?"

"Till dawn if you're gentle. If you pretend you're fine, maybe not."

"He's a busboy," Jo said. "No one is gentle to busboys."

The kid let out one miserable laugh. "Yeah."

Mira irrigated the elbow, laid a shallow seal over the split, and wrapped it tight enough that he hissed through his new braces. The wrap bought him till dawn and no farther, a delay dressed up with decent bedside manner. She wrote his ticket number at the top of the pink slip and, under it, PRIORITY WHEN WE RESTOCK.

He stared at the words. "You're writing me an appointment in a laundromat."

"I'm writing you priority," Jo said. "Try gratitude."

With the table in place, the rest of the night changed shape. People announced themselves to Jo first, and Jo translated panic into categories harsh enough to be useful. Open or weird. Contract active or done. Cash, barter, or shame. The widower from the fish market became ticket fifteen after Jo wrote SHOULDER PATS ME on the back and shoved him through. The night porter whose calf kept boxing one invisible dance step became ticket sixteen and went in laughing so she would not limp in front of strangers. Nobody else touched the curtain.

A little after two, Mira added another rule:

NO SURPRISE BILLS

If the work was only a patch, they said patch. If the symptoms would come back, they said that too. The lie people could afford least, she realized, was hope sold as completion.

By then the front room no longer sounded like gossip. It sounded like intake. Quarters knocked into metal slots. Jo called numbers. Someone explained model lines near machine four while folding still-warm towels to keep their hands busy. The laundromat had stopped pretending to be cover and become procedure in public clothes.

When the sanitation widower with the clove jar finally sat down and said, "My shoulder keeps trying to comfort me," nobody laughed first. Jo had already taught the room what counted as useful detail. Mira took ticket eighteen from the stack and did not have to explain why the number mattered.

***

By dawn the laundromat had a desk.

By then metaphor had burned off. The same narrow cashier table Jo had dragged out after Nessa Aguirre's scream pulled half the laundromat to the curtain had stayed put long enough to acquire authority. A cigar tin held cash. A stack of pink tickets leaned against the fish-shaped ceramic dish where hair ties, transit chips, and tips nobody wanted to count in public had begun to collect. Machine nine still sat disassembled in the corner like a martyr to cover stories. On the wall behind the desk, the whiteboard had filled almost to the edges.

BLEEDING / LOAD-BEARING / RETURN-FEE RISK
SWEET ROT JUMPS THE LINE IF FEVER
PARTS PLUS LABOR. QUOTE BEFORE CUTTING.
NO TABS UNLESS JO VOUCHES
TICKET NUMBER ONLY
SAY THE WEIRD PART FIRST
NO PHOTOS
CONTRACT ACTIVE? SAY SO
IF THE BODY HEARS SOMETHING YOU DON'T, TELL US
IF YOU WAIT TILL IT ROTS, I WILL MOCK YOU
ONE BODY PAST THE CURTAIN
NO OPEN WORK AFTER LAST SLEEVE
NO SURPRISE BILLS

Under that, in Jo's block handwriting:

DO YOUR LAUNDRY WHILE YOU WAIT

The sky outside had gone from black to the bad gray that made canal water look like dishwater. First tram of the morning rattled somewhere uphill. Mira could hear it before she saw the light smear across the window. Her spine felt packed with sand. She had been on her feet so long the soles of them no longer quite belonged to her.

Tavi sat on the stool with his gloves off and his head tipped back against the boiler, eyes closed. In the weak morning light he looked older than thirty-eight, all the bench years visible at once in the stoop of his shoulders and the pinch between his brows. There was dried sealant on his wrist and frosting on his shirt. One of Jo's orphaned socks had ended up hanging from his back pocket like a defeated flag.

Jo counted cash from the cigar tin in quick clean snaps. "Don't let this inflate your egos. Half of this is because night people are sentimental."

"Night people and your extortionate room fee," Tavi said without opening his eyes.

"Pay it or perform surgery in the canal."

Mira leaned against the sink and did her own count on the margin of an old detergent invoice: cash in, parts out, Jo's fifth. Berto still owed Jo twenty she would never chase like a creditor because she preferred leverage with a human face. Pilar's dye packets sat by the mirror next to Saira's little hotel cake box. The busboy's pink slip, marked PRIORITY WHEN WE RESTOCK, hung from the line over the sink. Nessa's aftercare notes were still trapped in Mira's head because ticket numbers were safer than names. They had made more in one night than Mira had expected and less than they needed. It was enough to buy supplies and enough to continue, but not enough to fix rent, which was worse in some ways.

"We need sterile sleeves, period," she said. "More foam. Better signal pads. And actual records."

Tavi cracked one eye open. "Ticket numbers are actual records."

"Ticket numbers are laundry pretending."

"Exactly. Good records with plausible deniability."

Jo slid a smaller pile across the desk toward Mira. "Your share."

Mira looked at it. The bills were damp at the edges from too many pockets. A ten smelled faintly of fish. One of the twenties had lipstick on the corner. It was cash from the city's private humiliations, and it was cash she needed. It had come because CommonFrame and everyone like it kept turning injury into debt faster than people could heal.

She took it.

She didn't take it with noble reluctance or with shame dramatic enough to purify the act. She took it because Brine Court wanted twenty-four hundred dollars they did not have and Gabe's old frame debt still circled his legal wages like gulls over trash. Relief arrived first, plain and muscular. The moral bookkeeping could wait.

"We're still missing the secrecy part," she said.

Jo laughed so hard her cigarette nearly fell from behind her ear. "Honey, secrecy is over. We have replaced secrecy with manners."

That was true too. By three in the morning the line had stopped pretending not to know what the back room was for. What Jo had built instead was etiquette. Nobody stared when someone came out walking better than they had gone in. Nobody asked which part had seized or torn unless invited. Towels, baskets, and the ordinary chores of the place gave everyone something to do with their eyes.

The older woman in leopard slippers, who had stayed almost the whole night under the pretext of finishing one impossible hem, tapped the glass on the way out and called, "My nephew wants the Tuesday slot if you decide you're respectable."

"We're never being respectable," Jo called back.

"Then put him down for Tuesday."

The bell over the front door jingled again before Mira could answer.

A young father came in carrying a sleeping toddler on one shoulder and a bag of work coveralls in the other hand. He had the pinched expression of somebody who had gone from night shift to parenting without a legal amount of sleep between them. Behind him a woman in grocery-delivery green held her right wrist in her left hand and tried not to look urgent. Neither of them seemed surprised by the desk.

"We closed," Tavi said automatically.

Jo glanced at the two waiting figures, then at the board, then at Mira. "Did we?"

The grocery courier lifted her wrist a little. "Pilar said mornings are for tickets."

The father shifted the toddler, who snored into his neck. "Berto said if I brought wash it counts."

He held up the bag of coveralls like proof of citizenship.

Mira looked at the wetbench, the board, the pink ticket stack, the fish-shaped dish with transit chips glinting in it. She looked at Tavi, who was already too tired to fake annoyance properly. She looked at Jo, who had known this outcome before any of them and was enjoying not saying so.

Preparation had ended somewhere around ticket twelve, though she had not noticed it at the time. By dawn the room had hardened into an address. Favor, one-night crime, revenue stream: those were yesterday's names for it. People had already started navigating toward the new one.

She stepped to the desk and pulled the first pink ticket from the stack.

"Contract active?" she asked the courier.

"Till tonight."

"Say the weird part first."

The woman let out a breath that almost shook. "My wrist keeps trying to sign someone else's name."

Mira wrote the ticket number at the top, then the symptom underneath.

Out front, obediently, somebody picked up a towel.

Jo reached for the front lock and flipped the sign from LAST WASH to MEMBERS.

"Blue Hour restoration desk," she said toward the room, like it had existed long enough to deserve a name. "Morning intake starts now. If you're waiting, fold something."


# Chapter 4

Blue Hour had gone over to its night noises by the time Mira rolled LM-127B through the back hall, long after Jo had flipped the front sign to MEMBERS. The customer bell was dark. One dryer still turned with a bad bearing whine, then knocked once every revolution like a loose tooth. Steam had settled into the hanging shirts and uniforms so the room felt upholstered in damp cloth. The body rode under a motel sheet on one of Jo's wire carts, ankles tapping the rail each time a wheel found the cracked grout by the sink.

She had signed the transfer under salvage review, which bought her until morning if nobody matched the hold shelf against the internal movement log. Morning would turn clerical lag into a list of things she could not afford: tampering, removal of company stock, falsification of disposal routing. She could have written the categories herself. Claims taught you the difference between a mistake and a billable act. It also taught you how quickly both became the same thing.

Tavi looked up from the wetbench when she came in. He had his tools laid out in weight order on a folded towel printed with lemons, because he treated every borrowed surface like it had to survive him. His long fingers were already gloved, but he still tapped each vial against the burn scar on his forearm before he opened it, a nervous little click-click that he would have denied if anyone named it. Steam had put a shine on the bridge of his flattened nose.

"Tell me again," he said, "why this one gets midnight bench time ahead of the people who are actually paying us."

Mira braked the cart with her knee. "Because if I leave them on hold another day, somebody starts asking why."

"That's a reason you stole a body. It isn't a reason I should touch it."

She pulled the sheet back.

Cheap print skin always looked more expensive asleep. CommonFrame paid for a neutral face and saved money everywhere else. The body was adult, medium height, built to pass through labor shifts, sex shifts, and weekend vanity without offending any target market. Under the back-room bulb the injuries showed what the catalog never did. There was yellowing bruising high on one thigh, fresher mottling at the left shoulder, a pressure mark at the mouth where someone had held it shut too hard or kissed it with their teeth, and the pale rib scar that had brought her here in the first place, a clean old line under the left ribs with no manufacturing note and no repair authorization attached. One ear had a notch in the rim as if somebody had once missed with scissors. At the nape, half-hidden by short damp hair, the serial port sat like an extra idea the body had never agreed to carry.

Tavi's face changed in increments. He was still suspicious. He was also hooked now, and they both knew it.

"That scar is old," he said.

"Older than the renter file."

"The bruising isn't." He leaned in, not touching yet. "Neither is that mouth."

Mira heard herself answer in the dry voice she used at work when people wanted facts without judgment. "The lease declaration listed light leisure, no sex package, no specialty mods, no hazardous use. The residue scan threw a child in water, a hand-counting pattern that didn't match the renter's job history, and a panic spike during pelvic exam. Nothing aligned."

Tavi gave her a look over the cart. "You really know how to sell a nightmare."

"I'm not selling it."

"You dragged it uphill after midnight. That counts."

She ignored that. "Can you wipe the carryover and see what's underneath?"

He stayed bent over the body, eyes moving from scar to shoulder to nape port. "Maybe. Depends what this is. Could be dirty wash and rough use. Could be aggregate bloom. Could be somebody inside CommonFrame getting creative with salvage."

"They're already getting creative."

"Mira."

"I know what I saw."

He straightened. The stoop from bench work was more pronounced when he was tired, and tonight it made him look older than thirty-eight, less roguish than frayed. "I didn't say you didn't. I said maybe don't sound so eager to prove the worst thing first."

That landed because he almost never asked for optimism. Mira folded the sheet down to the body's waist, exposing the scar and the shoulder. The skin was cool from transit, but not storage-cold anymore. She had felt that through the cart handles the whole walk from the tram stop: a returning heat she did not want to interpret.

"If this is an overlap," Tavi said, quieter now, "the wipe might wake it up before it clears it."

She looked at him. "Bodies don't wake up from residue."

He did not answer quickly enough.

The room grew more crowded around the delay. Steam pressed at Mira's back. The dryer with the bad bearing gave another mournful knock. Out front, somewhere past the hanging uniforms, coins ran through a counter tray and stopped. Jo was closing the till. They had maybe an hour before she'd either come back here for the stain solvent or decide she was done lending the room to other people's bad ideas and turn off half the power.

"Tavi."

He reached for the conductive gel. "I said might. Don't make a religion out of it."

That was as close to admission as he was going to give. Mira filed it away because she had spent too long in claims not to notice when people who knew more than the paperwork got careful with verbs.

He worked fast once he started. He clipped two monitor leads beneath the jaw, cleaned the nape port with iodine until the skin around it shone amber, and threaded in a slim diagnostic line while Mira steadied the head. The body gave a reflex swallow at the first touch. Tavi's eyes cut up.

"Residual motor."

"I saw."

"If they buck, hold the left shoulder. That side's already chewed up."

There it was, casual and terrible, the shift in his language before hers. They. Mira did not say anything about it. She slid one hand under the shoulder blade and felt the cheap musculature wake under her palm in shallow, uncertain twitches, as if three different jobs were trying to remember themselves through the same strip of meat.

The wetbench hummed on. A wash map opened over the body in bands of blue-white. Mira did not know the software the way Tavi did, but she knew what bad looked like. Signals bloomed where there should have been one clean lane. A pressure ridge around the rib scar. Dense retention at the throat and pelvis. Cross-fire at the motor strip like a crowd trying to get through a single door.

"Jesus," Tavi muttered. "Look at that."

"What am I looking at?"

"A body that should've been pulped twice."

"Useful."

"I'm serious." He scrubbed a hand over his mouth with the back of his wrist. "There are stacked pathways in here. It isn't just memory soot. It's patterned retention. Somebody didn't wash this line, they composted it."

The word made the room smell worse, dragging up not just detergent and hot cotton but the sweetish tissue stink that lived under every repair if you opened enough skin. Mira looked at the pale scar. Somebody had cut in there once, neat as a paid surgeon, and left no note, no fee record, no correction order. Somebody had already treated this body like a place to bury work.

"Start the wipe," she said.

Tavi looked at her a second longer. Then he set the pulse low and put his thumb over the contact pad.

The first current made the body tense from heels to throat, a flinch so large it had to spend the whole frame. Mira tightened her grip on the shoulder. The right hand opened and closed once, fingers making a hard twisting shape around nothing. Then the wrist jerked inward, knuckles scraping the bench, as if it had become a different hand halfway through the motion.

"You saw that?" she said.

"Yep."

The next pulse sent a ripple across the abdomen. The feet pointed sharply, swimmer-clean, and then the left leg kicked sideways with the blunt force of somebody trying to force open a jammed train door. Tavi killed the current. The monitor did not settle. Lines kept running under the skin map, bright and crossing.

"That isn't postwash noise," Mira said.

"I know it isn't."

The body inhaled.

It was a small sound. Wet, catching, too deliberate to be reflex. Mira froze with her hand still under the shoulder blade. The chest lifted again, deeper this time. The mouth opened around the diagnostic bite block Tavi had set between the molars.

"Take that out," she said.

"If they clamp down on the line-"

"Take it out."

He moved fast. The block came free slick with saliva and a string of pink gel. The body's face tightened as if waking hurt in very specific places. Its eyelids fluttered. Opened. One iris was lighter than the other, gray chipped into brown like old paint showing through.

The body looked at the ceiling first, then at the hanging shirts, then directly at Mira.

"No," it said, hoarse around the gel. "No, don't send me back. Mira, please. Please don't."

The name went through her like the shove of a tram starting up.

Tavi swore and reached for the sedative pen. The body saw that too. Panic hit all at once, not abstractly but in the body itself: the shoulders heaved, the stomach seized, and it rolled half onto its side hard enough to tear the diagnostic line from the nape port. Saline and a thread of blood striped the sheet. Mira caught the torso before it pitched to the floor.

"Easy," she said, hearing how useless it was. "Easy. You're all right."

"No tanks," the body gasped. "No cold room, no, I can work, I can do nights, don't put me-"

The sentence fractured. Its right hand shoved at the bench rail like it was bracing against water. The left hand made a pinching motion in the air, quick and practiced, the sort of hand that could peel shrimp or pick glass out of a drain. Then both hands grabbed Mira's forearm with an animal, desperate strength.

"Hold them," Tavi snapped.

Them. After that the old pronoun would not come clean in Mira's head no matter how hard she tried.

She got her weight over the thrashing body without pinning it flat. The skin was hot now, hotter than it should have been, heat rising through the damp motel sheet and the thin shirt she had worn to work. Under her palm the shoulder kept trying different histories: a swimmer's roll, a laborer's brace, an instinctive curl inward like someone waiting for a blow.

Tavi slapped a coolant patch against the sternum. "Listen to me," he said, too fast, too sharp. "If you tear the line again you're going to cook your own cortex."

The body stared at him in wild incomprehension.

Mira said, "You're in a laundry back room. You hear that dryer? You're here. That's steam. That's soap. Nobody's putting you anywhere right now."

The grip on her forearm did not loosen, but it changed. There was less fight in it now and more proof of contact.

"Hot soap," the body repeated. It swallowed and winced. "Coins. Wet cotton."

"Yeah."

"Your hand's rough."

Their grip on her wrist made Mira stupid for a second, as if they had discovered something private instead of obvious. Calluses met calluses. Ink smudge at the base of her thumb from the transfer form. A crescent dent in her skin where their nails had gone in. The body tracked all of it with the horrible focus of somebody whose entire world had narrowed to this room and the woman leaning over them.

Tavi had gone still in a different way. He wasn't calm. He was measuring. His humor had burnt off so completely that Mira could see the shape of the technician under it, the man who had spent years with wet tissue and expensive mistakes under his hands.

"Can you follow what I'm saying?" he asked.

The body turned its head toward him. "You're the one with the hot scar."

For one second Tavi looked almost offended on behalf of his own forearm. Then the expression broke under the fact of the answer.

"Okay," he said. "Okay. Good. Stay with me. Do you know where you were before this?"

"No."

"Do you know who you are?"

The body shut its eyes hard. Mira felt the lashes brush the heel of her hand when they opened them again.

"I know stairs," they said. "I know a bus depot bathroom that smelled like bleach and pennies. I know how to hold a tray with six coffees if the lids are bad. I know somebody's little boy in yellow water and I hate him because he's dead and he's not mine to hate. I know this room now. I know you said Mira."

Their breathing hitched on the last word.

"Don't wipe me," they said. "If wipe means less than this, don't."

Mira heard Tavi inhale through his teeth. She knew what he was about to say because she had spent years listening to frightened people wrap themselves in procedure.

We do not know what this is.

It may not be stable.

There are protocols.

He did not get the chance. The body turned their face into the inside of Mira's wrist, as if even the smell of laundry soap and transit dust there might count as shelter, and said in a voice gone raw and small, "Please don't make me go away because it's expensive."

That did it. Not the philosophy of it, not even the impossible speech. The money in the plea landed harder: the assumption that annihilation would arrive as a cost-saving measure and everybody in the room would know the terms.

Mira's throat tightened on the next breath.

"Tell me that's reflex," she said.

He met her eyes over the torn line, the lit monitor, the hot damp room that suddenly could not contain what it knew.

"It isn't," he said.

***

They stopped shaking first.

That was how Mira started measuring the next stretch of time, not by minutes but by what the body no longer did. The kicking stopped. The incoherent hand motions stopped. The breath lengthened enough that Tavi could clean the torn skin around the nape port and seal it with strip mesh instead of holding pressure. Mira got water into them by teaspoon at first, because the first full swallow made them cough until their eyes watered. By the time the dryer out front finally went quiet, they were sitting upright on the bench in one of Tavi's washed-thin T-shirts with the motel sheet wrapped around their legs, both hands around a paper cup of tap water as if the cup might try to leave without permission.

Steam still hung low in the room, but without the emergency in it the heat felt more intimate and worse. Mira had blood drying on the heel of one hand where the line had cut the skin at the nape. Tavi had changed gloves twice and still looked contaminated by the whole thing.

The conscious body on the bench watched them both with exhausting care.

They were not blank, infantile, or machine-still waiting for prompts. They watched the way people watched when they had learned too early that adults liked to decide your future in front of you if you let them think you were not listening.

Tavi peeled off his gloves. "This can't stay here."

Mira did not answer right away. She was looking at the left ear, the notch in it, the small involuntary movement they made with their thumb along the paper seam of the cup, rubbing it smooth and then rough again. A private habit, maybe new, maybe old. Something to do with having hands and needing them occupied.

"Mira."

"I heard you."

"No, I don't think you did." He pitched his used gloves into the red bin and missed. He did not stoop to fix it. "This isn't a weird repair case. This isn't a client with spillover. This is a talking overlap body sitting in Jo's laundry with my bench log open and your company probably already wondering why one of their units took a field trip."

From the bench, very softly: "Overlap."

Mira turned. "It's the word for more than one memory pattern staying active in one body."

They tried to answer, but their right hand cramped hard around the paper cup first. The seam split. Water ran over their knuckles and into the sheet around their thighs. They went rigid, staring past Mira at the swaying uniforms.

"Cold room," they whispered. "Metal shelf. No. Hot soap. Coins. Wet cotton."

Tavi was already moving. He pressed two fingers under their jaw, felt the heat there, then peeled the spent coolant patch off the sternum and slapped a fresh square higher at the throat. He taped it down with a rough cross of white adhesive that bit into the damp skin.

Tavi dragged a stool over with his foot and sat on it backward, forearms across the backrest. That was how he sat when he needed something between himself and what he was saying. "That's what it looks like. That's also what I'm talking about."

"So that's what it looks like," they said, voice frayed.

"I don't know enough yet to say more cleanly than that."

Mira did. Or she knew enough for the only part that mattered. "If we report this, CommonFrame takes them."

Tavi's shoulders went tight. "Maybe. Or maybe this goes to a lab instead of straight disposal."

"That's not better."

"I didn't say it was."

"Report means seizure, internal review, wash, pulp if unstable, salvage if not. I know the language."

"You know claims language."

"I know what vanishes off my desk."

The body on the bench kept their eyes on Mira, not because they trusted her yet but because she was the one using verbs with the fewest curtains around them.

"And wipe?" they asked.

Nobody answered.

They looked from one of them to the other. "When you say wipe, what stays?"

Tavi's mouth tightened. Mira had seen him bluff nurses, clients, inspectors, neighbors, and one very drunk man with a detached thumb. She had never seen him search this hard for a sentence.

"The body would stay," he said at last. "Basic motor, probably. Leaseable function. The present pattern likely wouldn't."

They retched dryly into their own shoulder with nothing in the stomach to bring up. Their left leg kicked once under the sheet, sharp enough to knock the stool. The room filled with the sour chemical smell of stress sweat and hot adhesive.

"So the pattern goes," they said hoarsely.

"I know how that sounds."

"Like cleaner words."

Tavi flinched, very slightly. Mira caught it because she was close enough now to recognize how his face changed when a joke failed to arrive and left him alone with the thing itself.

"Yeah," he said. "Like cleaner words."

The room held there. The dryers were off. Out front a tram bell drifted up from the street, thin through glass and steam. Somewhere under it Jo laughed at something on the radio, one hard nicotine rasp and then silence again. Ordinary city sound. Ordinary late summer heat. Inside the back room, none of it reached far enough.

Mira said, "I'm not sending them back."

Tavi rubbed both hands over his face. "You say that like there's a fourth option where nobody comes looking."

"There is."

"Hide them."

"Yes."

"In what? Under the folding table?" His voice went louder, then rougher, then he reined it in because Jo was twenty feet away and had already been generous enough. "Mira, this isn't just stolen stock. If Tissue Safety gets a conscious body out of an unlicensed room, they don't fine us like idiots running gray-market stitch work. They make an example. They seize everything. They bury Jo. They bury you. They bury me."

Before Mira could answer, their head jerked toward the hanging shirts again. Their pupils went wide enough to darken both irises. "Blue tile," they said. "Somebody scrubbed blood off grout. No. No, this is lemons. This towel has lemons." Their fingers hunted blindly until they found the printed towel under Tavi's tray and clutched it with sudden force. "Tell me which room I'm in."

Mira crouched closer. "Blue Hour. Back room. Steam, soap, bad dryer bearing, and Tavi's ugly towel."

The words brought them back by increments. Their grip eased from the towel.

"I know," Mira said.

"Do you?" Tavi asked.

She could have answered with statutes. She could have told him the civil fine range for unauthorized tissue handling, the seizure schedule for illicit biological inventory, the way hearing officers said contaminated asset with the same tone some landlords used for mold. Instead she thought of her rent arrears, Gabe's debt notices tucked under the salt shaker because neither of them wanted to see them on the table, the exact shape of Brine Court's stairwell at four in the morning when the hallway light was out again. Hiding a person there was insane. Hiding a person there was also more possible than trusting a corporation that had printed them by the thousand and called it discount inventory.

"I know what the company does to bodies that make administration complicated," she said. "That's enough."

Tavi leaned back from the stool and looked at the bench, not at Mira. When he spoke again the humor had not returned, but something else had: that private register she only saw in him while he was repairing people who could not afford elegance.

"I am not arguing they're property."

The body's eyes snapped to him at the uncontracted form, precise with stress.

Tavi noticed and corrected himself with a grimace. "I'm not arguing that. I'm arguing that I don't know what keeping you alive looks like yet. You're running hot. Your lattice map is filthy. There are four or five retention bands in you at least, maybe more, and if one of them starts eating the rest I can't promise this doesn't turn ugly fast."

As if the sentence had called it up, they pressed both palms to the sides of their head. Their shoulders locked. A thin line of blood ran out of one nostril. They looked at Mira without seeming to see her and said, very clearly, "I smell chlorine."

Tavi swore, grabbed gauze, and pinched the bridge of their nose. "That's new."

"What does ugly fast mean?" they asked through the blood and gauze.

"It means you could start dropping pieces," Tavi said. "Time, motor control, speech. Maybe more."

"Would reporting me keep me alive?" they asked.

"Alive as what?" Tavi asked, and hated himself for how fast it came out.

That was the wrong question. Mira heard it land. So did he.

The body lowered their eyes to the torn cup. Their thumb went back to the soaked seam as if the paper could still be smoothed. "Alive enough to be useful," they said. "That's usually what people mean."

Mira crossed the room before she had fully decided to. She took the stool from Tavi with one hand and sat in front of the bench so the body did not have to look down at either of them. Up close, the mixed irises were more striking, but what got her was the attention in them. It wasn't innocence; calculation would have been easier. They were sorting every word for shelter and threat at once.

"Look at me," she said.

They did.

"Reporting you means putting you back inside the system that printed you. I don't know what they'd call it, but I know what they'd do. They'd take you away from the people in this room. They'd decide in private whether you're evidence, salvage, or a defect to clear. You would not get a vote."

"And wiping me?"

Mira made herself answer plainly because anything softer would be a lie. "I think that kills the part of you that's talking to me."

They nodded once. The motion turned wrong halfway down, as if some other neck had borrowed theirs for a second. Tavi steadied the back of their head until the spasm passed.

"Then don't do it," they said.

Tavi let out a breath that almost became a laugh and did not. "You hear how easy you make it sound?"

They turned their head toward him. "It isn't easy. You're both just saying the same thing with different shame."

That shut him up completely.

Mira almost smiled despite herself. The joke mattered less than the ownership of it: the first sentence in the room that belonged to the person saying it instead of the emergency they had been born into.

"Do you remember anything that belongs to you?" she asked.

They looked down at their hands. "I don't know how to separate that yet."

"Try."

They closed their eyes. For a moment Mira thought she had asked too much. Then their breathing changed. It went shallow, then fast, then arrested on a sharp inhale that made the tape at the throat jump. When they spoke, the words came slowly, as if they were lifting things one at a time from muddy water while the water kept trying to close over them again.

"There's steam on painted walls," they said. "Not here. A stairwell. Green once, then not green because the damp ate it. Somebody downstairs is frying fish or plantains or both. The banister sticks if you drag your hand too slow. Somebody keeps shouting up for..." Their right hand made that earlier twisting shape again, fingers grasping at air. "Lin."

The name hung there, small and exact.

Mira said nothing.

"It wasn't at me," they said. "Maybe it went through me or around me. But it comes clean. Lin, get down here if you're taking the salt with you. Lin, if you let that pot burn I'll skin you. Lin, answer me." They opened their eyes. "I liked how the voice expected an answer."

Tavi's gaze dropped to the floor, then came back. "You want that one?"

They nodded. "If I've got to borrow, I'd rather borrow something somebody called because they wanted me in the room."

Mira felt that under the breastbone, a hard little inward catch that had nothing to do with romance or pity and everything to do with recognition. So many people spent half their lives being summoned only for payment, labor, explanation. To want a name because it carried ordinary impatience from a kitchen or stairwell made more sense to her than any grand speech about identity ever could.

"Lin," she said, testing it.

Lin looked up fast because the name had been handed back without argument.

At the nape, under Tavi's strip mesh, something gave a bright insect chirp from the monitor. Lin folded hard around the sound, one hand flying to the port. The other arm slammed into the metal rail with enough force to ring it. The chemical smell in the room sharpened again, hot and swimming-pool wrong.

"Shit," Tavi said. He was at the monitor before Mira had fully stood. "There. That's a handshake attempt."

The monitor threw blue stutter across Lin's bent back. "From what?" Mira said.

"From the serial. Every time they spike, the port reaches for a network that shouldn't be able to hear it in a laundry." He yanked the diagnostic lead free of the board and slapped the monitor dark with the heel of his hand. "All right. Lin." He said it like he was fitting a part and checking whether it held. "If we're doing this, we do it with our eyes open."

Mira stayed seated. "Meaning?"

"Meaning this bench log dies now. The transfer dies now. The serial ping gets muffled before the next spike if I can manage it without frying the port. Jo does not get surprised by a corporate retrieval team because we got sentimental in her back room. And Lin does not stay here."

Lin said, "I can hear when you're afraid."

"Good," Tavi said. "Use that skill generously."

It was the nearest thing to a joke he had managed since the waking, and even now it carried too much fear to float. Still, Lin's mouth moved at one corner, not quite a smile, more the first test of whether one might be allowed.

Mira stood. "They can come with me."

Tavi went very still. "Home."

"For tonight."

"You live in a building made of eavesdropping and overdue rent."

"I know."

"Your brother-"

"Will deal with it in the morning."

He stood there long enough for the practical objection to strip down to its real shape. Beneath the risk sat commitment. Once she took Lin uphill to Brine Court, this stopped being an emergency in a borrowed room and became part of her life in the way rent, blood, and family were part of it. Tavi saw that before she let herself.

"Mira," he said, quieter now, "if somebody traces this back, you lose more than the job."

She thought of Lin saying expensive. She thought of the shelf at CommonFrame, the file language waiting to flatten them back into anomaly rates and disposition codes. She thought of the way their fingers had gone from combat grip to simple human need around her wrist when she said steam, soap, dryer, here.

"I know," she said again. "I'm still not sending them back."

For a second she thought he would keep fighting. Then he walked to the sink, took a cheap utility knife from the magnet strip, and came back to the bench. He crouched in front of Lin.

"I'm going to cut the wrist tag," he said. "Hold still."

Lin looked to Mira. She nodded. Tavi slid two fingers under the plastiskin band CommonFrame used for internal chain-of-custody and sliced upward. The barcode snapped. A tiny thing. Less sound than the click of his vials against his scar. It still felt like a door giving way.

He handed the severed band to Mira.

"Congratulations," he said to nobody in particular. "We're done pretending this is repair."

He went to work on the monitor after that, hands quick and ugly with fatigue, wiping the active log, forcing a hardware error into the last two minutes of recorded trace, soldering a cheap signal-damp patch onto a length of mesh that he told Lin to keep at the nape under their hair. Mira understood enough to know he was helping in the only language he trusted when things got this bad.

While he worked, she found clothes. A pair of drawstring scrub pants Jo kept for bleach accidents, rolled twice at the ankle. Tavi's oldest work coat, canvas gone soft at the cuffs, which Lin buttoned wrong and left that way. A pair of rubber-soled kitchen shoes from the lost-and-found bin, too big but better than bare feet on the alley grit. Lin stepped into each item with wary concentration, as if clothing itself might contain a test. When the coat snagged at the shoulder, they muttered, "This sleeve remembers somebody broader than me," and then looked up quickly, embarrassed by their own sentence.

"The coat remembers beer and solder," Tavi said. "Ignore it."

Lin surprised Mira with a small breath through the nose, not quite laughter, more the first attempt at it.

When they were dressed, Mira tucked the severed wrist tag into the pocket of her work pants and immediately took it back out. Keeping it felt too much like evidence. Throwing it away felt worse. In the end she dropped it into the sharps tin and listened to it hit metal under gauze, scalpels, and cracked ports. Tiny, final, insufficient.

Out front the lights were half down. Jo had gone home after leaving a note by the register that said LOCK THE GODDAMN BACK and had drawn a smiley face so angry it looked like a wound. Mira switched off the bench lamp. The back room fell into a dim industrial blue from the streetlight in the alley window.

Lin stood very close to the door once she opened it, not crowding her, just staying within the range where a body could follow if the room changed its mind. The night outside smelled of salt, fryer grease, hot brick, and the canal's chemical rot. Somewhere up the hill the tram rattled unseen.

Tavi leaned against the sink, exhausted all the way through now. "Call me if they spike a fever. Or if they start losing time. Or if anybody in your building asks stupid questions."

"That narrows it down."

"Shut up and listen." He pointed at Lin, then at the mesh patch he had fixed over the port. "Keep that dry as you can. If it peels, don't touch the contacts. If you get dizzy, if the room starts doubling, if you smell chlorine where there isn't any, tell her immediately."

Lin nodded with grave attention. "You think I might come apart."

Tavi scrubbed a hand over his mouth. "I think somebody built you out of corners that were never meant to meet. That's not the same thing."

Lin absorbed that. "All right."

One hand on the door, Mira let the alley heat push around her wrist and took one last inventory: Tavi by the sink, the wrecked sheet, the torn line, the blanked monitor, the room where the whole book of her life had just lost one of its oldest nouns.

"I'll get them out before dawn," she said.

"They're already out," he said.

It was not reassurance. It was worse and truer than that.

Mira put her palm lightly between Lin's shoulder blades and led them into the alley. They moved with caution but not helplessness, one hand holding the oversize coat closed, the other brushing the brick wall once as if to be sure it was solid. Behind them Tavi locked the service door, one bolt and then the other.

The walk uphill to Brine Court would take twelve minutes if Lin could manage the grade and the shoes did not betray them. Twelve minutes through canal-market runoff, through the smell of fried fish and coolant, through a city that had room for leased bodies, pulped bodies, damaged bodies, debtor bodies, sex bodies, labor bodies, but none at all for the fact of one deciding to stay alive.

Mira spent the first block pretending that made the decision practical instead of permanent.

Lin kept close enough that their sleeve touched Mira's elbow each time they turned a corner.

By the top of the block she was no longer moving stolen stock through the dark. She was smuggling a person home, and every step after that belonged to a new category of crime.


# Chapter 5

On the third-flight landing at Brine Court, Mira paused and looked back at Lin while dawn turned the stairwell windows the color of old dishwater. The building held every smell it always held before the day got hot: salt pushed uphill from the seawall, fryer grease from the breakfast stall on the corner, old mop water, the bleach-bright ghost of detergent drifting over from Blue Hour's vents, and beneath all of it the damp mineral breath of a place that had been patched more often than repaired.

Lin climbed carefully, one hand on the rail, wearing Tavi's coat buttoned wrong over a pair of borrowed scrub pants that had to be rolled twice at the ankle. The coat hid most of the tape at their throat and the sealed port at the nape, but not the newness of every movement. They kept pausing at each landing, listening to the building settle around them. Mira had spent the walk from the laundry telling herself she was doing something temporary and practical, safer than leaving them in the back room and safer than letting Tavi keep a conscious bargain frame next to a wetbench and a stack of stained towels. They were evidence, she had told herself, and this was only hold-shelf logic brought home for one night.

Then Lin stopped on the second landing to let an old woman pass with two grocery sacks biting into her wrists and flattened themself against the peeling wall so politely it hurt to look at.

"Sorry," Lin whispered, though they had taken up almost no space at all.

Nobody who was only evidence apologized to old women in stairwells.

Mira unlocked 3F, shoved the swollen door with her shoulder, and let them in ahead of her. The apartment looked smaller every time she entered it in daylight. It was a galley kitchen the width of a hallway, a front room with a foldout table, a couch that ate springs, and two chairs that disagreed with the floor in different directions, one actual bedroom Gabe used because his back punished couches, and the half-enclosed sleeping nook Mira had claimed behind a curtain clipped to a wire. A radiator under the window knocked even in August, as if rehearsing winter. Rent notices and utility warnings lived on the fridge under a crab magnet with one missing eye.

Gabe's brace was gone from the hook by the door, which meant he had gone out early. His coffee mug sat rinsed and upside down by the sink. He had taken the blue work hoodie too. Mira felt an immediate, ugly wash of relief.

She locked the door, set the chain, and put Tavi's paper bag on the counter. He had packed it like a medic and a worried aunt at the same time: antiseptic strips, a fresh port cap, two nutrient gel tubes, the antibiotic patch he insisted Lin wear another twelve hours. At the bottom sat a stale peach hand pie from the bakery cart that rolled past Blue Hour at five.

Lin had stopped just inside the apartment. Their eyes kept moving, not suspicious, just intent, as if every ordinary object had to be learned from the outside first, from the dish rack to the warped cabinet door under the sink to the wet circle Gabe's mug had left on the table.

"You can sit," Mira said.

Lin touched the back of the green chair, then let their hand fall. "That sounds like a test."

"It isn't. The green one just throws people."

They chose the metal chair and lowered themself with exaggerated care. The chair squealed but held. Mira took off her shoes and checked the time on the cracked wall panel above the sink. She should have been on the tram in twenty minutes. Missing a shift meant money gone, one more attendance mark, one more little official bruise on a job she already needed too much. But she could not bring Lin upstairs half-healed, half-dressed, and carrying someone else's memories in their skin, then leave them alone with a lock they did not know how to use and neighbors who noticed everything.

She picked up the handset by the fridge and called the CommonFrame absence line.

"Food poisoning," she said after the tone, using the calm customer-service voice that made ugly facts sound processable. "Started around four. I can't keep water down. I'll send a clinic note if you need it."

The system chirped once, accepted the lie, and cut her off. She stood with the dead receiver in her hand a second longer than necessary. She was out forty-some dollars after deductions, maybe more if Celia's assistant decided to be exact about coverage. She hung up and opened the fridge.

There were two eggs, yesterday's rice in a plastic tub, half an onion going soft at one edge, a heel of sausage Gabe had been stretching all week, and six hot sauce packets he had skimmed from the dock canteen and stuffed into a mug by the stove. It was enough for breakfast if nobody expected generosity.

"Are you hungry?" she asked.

Lin did not answer immediately. They pressed a hand just below their ribs as if checking with the body itself.

"Yes," they said. "Also frightened. It feels different."

"Good. Means the wiring's still making distinctions."

Lin looked up. "Is that a joke?"

"Not a very good one."

Mira set the pan on the burner. The ignition clicked, caught, and gave her the low blue flame she trusted more than the wall heater. She put in the onion first, then the sausage, sliced thin enough to pretend there was more of it than there was. The smell came up fast, hot and sweet and greasy. Behind her, Lin made a small sound.

Mira turned. "What?"

Lin kept looking not at the pan but somewhere just to the left of it. "She used a plate because the board smelled like bleach," they said. "She kept sliding the onion into the pot with the side of her hand. There was a little burn scar here." They touched the inside of their own wrist. "I don't think it was mine. I mean, not mine exactly."

The onion hissed louder. Mira scraped it before it could catch.

"Do you get that all the time?" she asked, and hated how quickly the question came out in claims voice, not as compassion but as patterning, frequency, trigger, duration.

Lin shrugged one shoulder. "Not all the time. When something fits. Or when it nearly fits. Like a word you can feel in your mouth before you know it."

Mira added the rice. It went cold and white into the hot fat, then loosened under the spoon. She had expected impossible memory, if she let herself expect anything at all, to be rich-people residue: roof decks, hotel sheets, private clubs, stupid expensive leisure with somebody else's blood still in it. The bargain line got abused by the rich, yes, but what Lin kept touching through smell and heat and cheap apartment texture did not feel like borrowed luxury. It felt like the city that stood in line at daybreak with heel pain and lunch in foil.

She cracked the eggs in. The yellow spread, set, broke. The room got warmer by a degree that mattered.

"Can you eat spice?" she asked.

"I don't know."

"Neither does Gabe. He treats that like a political position."

One corner of Lin's mouth twitched. Mira tore open three of the hot sauce packets and worked them through the rice. When she set the plate down, she split the hand pie in half and added one piece to Lin's side without comment. Lin looked at the food with a concentration that made her feel intrusive.

"You don't need permission," she said.

"I know." Lin picked up the fork, frowned at it, and traded it for the spoon with sudden certainty. "She was standing at a sink. The bus came in six minutes. She burned her tongue every morning and kept eating anyway."

"Did she have a name?"

"Not one that stayed."

They took a bite. Their eyes closed, not theatrically, just because the body seemed to need a second to sort out hunger from memory. Mira took her own plate to the sink and leaned her hip against the counter while she ate. She wanted to ask a dozen questions about how many lives were in there, how recent they were, whether these were donor traces, renter traces, or waste left in the lattice from bodies nobody had ever been told were contributing to anything, and what exactly CommonFrame had been printing into its cheap stock when it decided a full wash cost too much.

Instead she said, "After you eat, you should shower. It'll help with the antiseptic stink."

Lin nodded, still focused on the plate. "Do I know how?"

"Probably. But the handle falls off unless you pull toward yourself first. And the hot side lies."

"That sounds more manageable."

Mira washed up while Lin finished. The apartment made its usual morning noises: tram grinding somewhere below the hill, pipes knocking two units over, a radio leaking market prices through the wall, gulls raising hell out at the water. Lin listened to each sound as if choosing which ones belonged in this room and which ones were still threats.

When Mira handed them a towel, they rubbed the fabric between finger and thumb.

"What now?" she asked.

Lin's thumb paused on the towel. "Hear that?" they said quietly. "A stroller wheel kept chirping every few turns. Somebody sang through her teeth so the baby stayed asleep through the radiator banging."

The towel remained only a towel in their hands. That might have been the strangest part. They did not perform the fragment. They just reported it and let it go.

Mira went to the drawer under the sink and found the emergency toothbrush she kept for nights when she was too exhausted to trust herself with the walk from couch to bathroom. It was still wrapped, which was good. She set it on top of the folded towel, then dug through the clean laundry pile for clothes that might pass: one of Gabe's old transport tees, washed thin at the collar, a pair of her charcoal drawstring pants, and two pairs of socks because nobody in the apartment ever found a matched set in time.

She brought the stack to the bathroom door and hesitated before knocking. In claims, naked printed bodies were routine. They came in bruised, split, leaking, carrying other people's perfume and semen and seawater, and the work only happened if she kept her eyes useful instead of embarrassed. In her own apartment, with one extra toothbrush and a towel that still smelled faintly of detergent and her own skin, nakedness felt different. It did not feel clinical or neutral.

"I've got clothes," she said through the door.

"Thank you."

The bathroom latch clicked. Lin opened the door just enough to take the bundle and the toothbrush. Up close Mira could see where Tavi's tape crossed their throat and where the lighter iris caught the weak bathroom light. They looked less uncanny in the apartment than they had on the wetbench. That made them more alarming, not less.

"The body wash is watered down," Mira said. "So if it doesn't foam, that's not you failing."

Lin stared at her for a beat, then laughed once, startled by it.

"All right," they said.

The shower pipes screamed awake. Mira went back to the kitchen and cleaned the pan before the egg welded itself permanently. She moved on instinct, wiping the counter, folding Tavi's paper bag flat, checking the window blind angle so nobody in the opposite building got a good look inside. Her hands wanted lists: medication at noon, port dressing tonight, concealment strategy, what to tell Gabe, what not to tell Gabe, and whether to make Lin stay out of sight if the landlord came for arrears again.

The water ran, then stopped. For a moment there was only the tram and the ticking metal ping of cooling pipe.

"You all right?" Mira called.

"Yes." A pause. "I knew where to put my foot so the tub edge wouldn't cut me."

Mira set the glass down too hard on the counter.

"A lot of people know that," she said, because it was the closest thing to comfort she had.

"Yes," Lin said from behind the door. Water dripped once, twice, into the tub. "That's what frightened me. I just knew where to step, then there was black water after it and a ladder slick with algae. A man kept counting the rungs out loud because if he stopped talking, he'd go under."

When they came out, their hair was damp and curling at the ends, the old transport tee hanging low on one shoulder. Mira's drawstring pants were short on them but workable. The borrowed clothes changed their outline more effectively than any concealment plan had, not because they hid what Lin was, but because they made the wrongness crueler. Someone had always been meant to go on living in a shirt soft from too many wash cycles, someone ordinary enough to pull it on without thinking.

Lin touched the sleeve and went still. "Orange plastic chairs. Wet coats and old fryer oil. Somebody slept sitting up between shifts because going home would've cost the whole rest period. The machine ate half the coins."

Mira wiped her hands on the dish towel. "A bus depot?"

"Maybe."

"That narrows it down to this whole city."

Lin looked down, unsure whether she was mocking them.

"That was a joke," Mira said.

"I know." After another small pause, they added, "It was better than the first one."

Mira snorted before she could stop herself. Lin stood there in Gabe's shirt and her pants and two unmatched socks, still not fully steady in their own balance, and watched her kitchen window brightening over a row of roofs. They had stopped looking like a problem she could file long enough to start looking like somebody who needed a place to put the damp towel when they were done with it.

She pointed to the hook behind the bathroom door. "There. Or Gabe will sit on it and complain like it's a personal betrayal."

Lin nodded solemnly, as if she had given them a rule worth learning.

Mira had just put water on for coffee when the key scraped at the front lock.

***

Gabe came in sideways, shoulder first, paper sack under one arm, brace showing through the back of his T-shirt in two hard lines. He always brought home things from the dock canteen as if he were skimming taxes from a hostile empire: sugar packets, plastic cutlery, the little foil hot sauces he treated as public resources reclaimed for the people. This morning a fistful of them stuck out of his shirt pocket like bright paper feathers.

He kicked the door shut, turned toward the kitchen, and stopped.

"Mira," he said, too calm, which meant three bad theories had already formed. "Please tell me that's a friend and not a debt."

Lin was seated at the table with both hands around the coffee mug Mira had given them to warm. The green chair wobbled under them but held.

"Morning to you too," Mira said.

"I'm serious."

"So am I."

The antibiotic patch wrapper on the counter and Tavi's folded paper bag were enough; Gabe's mouth flattened. He did not straighten all the way. He rarely did first thing after a shift. The left foot dragged just enough on the linoleum to make the fact of it unavoidable if you loved him. If you didn't, he usually talked fast enough to keep your eyes elsewhere.

"Did you bring CommonFrame home?" he asked.

"No."

"That wasn't the question."

Mira opened her mouth and closed it again. Every answer she had came out wrong in advance. Lin read the whole exchange faster than most people read forms and shifted slightly in the chair.

"If it helps," they said, almost shy, "I don't think I'm the most dangerous thing in this room. This chair has been trying to kill me for fifteen minutes."

The chair chose that moment to give a betrayed metallic squeal. Gabe barked out a laugh before he could stop himself. The laugh bent him. He caught the edge of the counter with one hand, rode the pain quietly, and straightened in stages.

"All right," he said. "Fair. The green one has a body count."

He set the paper sack down, peeled the hot sauce packets from his shirt pocket, and dropped them into the mug by the stove. Mira watched the routine settle him. He did it every time he came home, like the apartment needed proof that certain small economies could still be counted on.

"Name?" he asked Lin.

"Lin."

"Gabe." He hooked a thumb at Mira without looking at her. "I came with the apartment."

Lin glanced at the bedroom door, the curtain over Mira's nook, the table with three unmatched mugs on it, the fridge notices, the brace under Gabe's shirt. Their attention made the room feel both shabbier and more honest.

"You want to know if I'm trouble or expense," Lin said.

Mira winced. Gabe's mouth tightened.

"Everybody in here is both," he said. "Question is scale."

Lin's grip loosened on the mug. They nodded once, accepting the terms.

Gabe took the kettle off the burner before it screamed itself dry and poured coffee into his mug. He drank it black and too hot, always had. Then he looked at the extra plate in the sink.

"You called out?"

"Yeah."

"Jesus. You never call out unless there's blood or vomiting."

"There was almost both yesterday."

"Useful answer."

He leaned against the counter and shifted weight off his left leg. The little movements were never little to Mira. She could read a body faster than she could read mood. She could see the strain in the right shoulder from compensating, the careful way he kept his hips square, and the pain patch at the base of his spine that had started to peel at one corner, sweat loosening the adhesive.

Lin watched it too, not rudely. Just accurately.

"Does it hurt all the time," they asked, "or only when you have company?"

Gabe blinked, then laughed again, softer this time. "Mostly when I've got to be charming for free."

"That seems expensive."

"You have no idea."

Mira pulled the paper sack open. Gabe had brought day-old rolls, slightly flattened, and two bruised nectarines wrapped in the market circular. He always picked the fruit no one else wanted, not out of virtue but out of price.

"Eat one before it turns to liquor," he said to Lin, nodding at the fruit.

"Gabe."

"What? If we're pretending none of this is weird, I need structure. Structure is breakfast fruit."

Lin held the nectarine untouched in both hands, waiting for permission. She hated that they still did that.

"Take the nectarine," she said.

They did. Their thumbnail broke the skin. Juice ran over the heel of their hand. They went still a second too long.

"What?" Gabe asked.

Juice ran to Lin's wrist bone. They flinched before the words came. "He swore before the fruit even hit the deck," they said, eyes fixed somewhere past Gabe's shoulder. "Cold enough to make your teeth ache. It had taken two days to go soft enough." They licked the juice from their wrist, embarrassed by the memory and the motion both. "Sorry."

Gabe set his own nectarine back in the sack. "Don't apologize for fruit," he said. "The city charges enough for it already."

He crossed to the table and lowered himself into the safer chair with the deliberate, insulting care of a man half his age and three times as pissed off about it. The effort put a shine across his forehead. He ignored it.

"All right," he said to Mira. "Try again. Who's Lin, why are they in my shirt, and how much of my day is this about to ruin?"

Mira sat opposite him. Lin stayed quiet, mug in both hands.

"They're with me," she said.

"That's not information."

"It's the information that matters first."

Gabe rubbed one palm over his mouth. His eyes cut to Lin, then back to her. He had her same dark hair when he let it grow, though his was cropped close now for convenience and sweat, and the same mole family along the left side if you knew where to look. But his face had always been easier with people than hers, quicker to joke, quicker to deflect, quicker to make strangers think he was less hurt than he was.

"Are we hiding somebody?" he asked.

"Yes."

"From who?"

"Everybody official."

He let out a breath that was almost a whistle. "Great. Love it when the list is concise. Meaning CommonFrame, Tissue Safety, and the landlord if he smells leverage?"

"Yes."

Gabe tapped the table once with two fingers, harder than before. "Then let's use the real version. If one of them clocks Lin in here, Brine Court Housing stops sending pink little threats and finally throws us out. We end up on the curb with twenty-four hundred in arrears and me hauling our life down three flights with a back that already hates brushing my teeth."

Mira held his gaze. "I know."

"Do you? Because you've got your claims voice on. You're saying 'everybody official' like it's a delay fee."

"If Lin goes back, they get wiped or pulped or sent out again under cleaner paperwork. So yes. I know what this can cost us, and I'm still asking you to take it with me."

He looked at her for a long second. "You don't get to spend my spine like petty cash."

Her mouth went dry. "No. That's why I'm saying it plain now. If you tell me to move them, I'll move them before noon. But I'm not going to keep calling them temporary evidence just because that phrase sounds cheaper."

Lin set down the mug. "I can leave if this puts you in danger."

Mira's fingers locked around the mug handle. Gabe's chair legs came down flat on the linoleum.

Gabe looked at them properly for the first time, not just as a problem that had walked into his kitchen wearing his old shirt. He took in the careful posture, the patched throat, the way Lin had chosen the edge of the chair and kept their knees tucked in as if trying not to take up billable square footage.

"Leave and go where?" he asked.

Lin opened their mouth, then closed it.

"Exactly." Gabe rubbed one palm over his mouth. "I needed the price said out loud. Not hidden in Mira-language."

Mira let out a slow breath.

"All right," he said. "Then we keep you. If Brine Court wants one more reason to hate 3F, it can get in line."

Lin almost smiled.

"Does Tavi know?" Gabe asked.

"Yes."

"And?"

"And he sent antibiotics and a hand pie."

"Romantic."

"Don't start."

"I'm injured, not dead."

Lin's eyes flicked between them again, reading the sibling grammar of it faster than Mira liked. Gabe noticed too.

"Ground rules, since apparently we're a boarding house now. The bathroom door sticks. Mrs. Vale on two hears through concrete and believes every problem in the building is caused by unmarried people. If the landlord comes, don't open. If anybody asks, you're Mira's cousin from Bay Four because half the city has a cousin from Bay Four and no one follows up."

"Why Bay Four?" Lin asked.

"Because the landlord hates admitting Bay Four people know where he lives. It short-circuits him."

"That's true," Mira said.

Gabe pointed at the chairs. "Green one throws. Metal one pinches. If you find a third option, tell me. I've been searching for years."

"There's the crate in your room," Mira said.

"That's a stool of principle, not comfort."

Lin looked down at the green chair and said, with more confidence now, "I respect an enemy that announces itself."

Gabe grinned. There it was, the younger-brother swagger he could still pull on like a jacket even when pain had sweated through the collar. "See? They get the place already."

He reached for the nectarine, stopped halfway, and flexed his hand instead. Mira knew the move. It was a pain spike. He would wait it out before anyone could offer help.

Lin said quietly, "You don't have to act more fine because I'm new."

Gabe's gaze snapped to them. For a second Mira thought he might flinch away from the accuracy of it. Instead he huffed through his nose.

"That's an extremely rude thing to say in a stranger's kitchen," he said.

"I'm sorry."

"No, you're not. You're just correct."

Mira stood, crossed behind him, and pressed the edge of the pain patch flat where it was lifting. Gabe let her. That was how bad it was.

"You should lie down for an hour," she said.

"I just got home."

"Exactly."

He tipped his head back to look at her. "This is why people hide things from you."

"And none of you get better at it."

Gabe pushed himself up with a grunt he tried to disguise as annoyance. At the bedroom door he looked back at Lin.

"There's an old blue hoodie on the hook in there if you're cold," he said. "Take it. I hate the cuffs."

"Thank you."

"Also, if Mira gives you one of her serious little practical speeches, nod until she's done. It's easier on the rest of us."

"Go to bed," Mira said.

"See? Like that."

He disappeared into the bedroom and shut the door most of the way, never fully. A few seconds later the bedsprings complained. Then the apartment went quiet except for the tram and the pipes.

Lin looked at the half-closed door. "He was joking before he knew whether I was dangerous."

"That's how he buys time."

"For what?"

Mira gathered the plates because her hands needed occupation. "For the part where he figures out how much something hurts and whether anybody needs to know."

Lin absorbed that. The kettle ticked softly on the stove.

"I think I know people like that," they said.

Mira rinsed the pan. "You probably do."

She meant it one way. Lin heard three.

***

By afternoon the apartment had done what poor rooms did when asked to accept another body. It had shrunk first, then started inventing. Gabe slept behind the bedroom door with the radio low and a heating wrap under his back. Mira had gone out once, fast, while Lin stayed upstairs with strict instructions not to answer the door to anybody who did not say their name first. She had spent nine credits and loose coin at the key kiosk under the tram on a copy of the apartment key, fourteen more at the canal market on a secondhand pillow and a lamp with a shade the color of weak tea, and hated each purchase with the precise irritation of a woman who could convert every object in a bag into rice, rent, or avoided attention.

The money was one cost. The other waited behind the curtain: the only place in the apartment where she could pull fabric shut and go unobserved for six minutes. The copied key in her pocket had receipts for both.

When she came back, Lin was standing in the middle of the front room holding the blue hoodie Gabe had offered but not yet put on, as if they were waiting to be told whether taking it would count as overstepping.

"You can wear it," Mira said, setting the bags down.

"I wasn't sure if 'take it' meant now or in a colder month."

"With Gabe it meant now. He offers things like he's losing an argument."

Lin pulled on the hoodie. The cuffs swallowed their hands. It made them look younger and more tired at the same time. Mira handed over the lamp.

"Help me clear the nook," she said.

Lin turned toward the curtain. "Your bed?"

"It isn't much of a bed."

"It's still yours."

Mira knelt and unhooked the curtain from the wire one clip at a time. The nook behind it was exactly as mean as she had left it that morning: narrow cot, milk crate nightstand, stack of claims manuals under one corner to keep the frame level, one shelf with three shirts, two bras, a tin of coins, and the cheap paperback she had been taking six weeks to finish because she never got far enough through a paragraph before sleep took her. The wall at the back held a watermark shaped like a continent nobody wanted.

"You're taking it," she said. "I'm on the couch. Gabe needs the real mattress more than either of us."

Lin did not move. "That's because of me."

"That's because couches are terrible and I already know exactly how terrible."

She stripped the cot anyway, yanking off the sheet and blanket and tossing aside the flat pillow she had been folding in half for months in favor of the one from the market. Dust came up from the floorboards in a thin bitter smell. Lin knelt beside her and started stacking the crates without waiting for instruction, moving with the strange grace of somebody using half-remembered habits to negotiate new space.

"You don't have to do the heavy part," Mira said.

Lin looked at the crate in their hands. "I think this one mostly has paper in it."

"That isn't the point."

"Then what is?"

Mira sat back on her heels, annoyed before she could explain at whom. "The point is I said leave it," she said. "You're still half patched and I'm trying to get a bed made before Gabe wakes up again."

Lin held her gaze for a moment too long. "I don't know how to let somebody do this."

Neither did she, Mira thought.

They kept working. She moved her clothes to the hall shelf by the bathroom. Lin wiped the nook's single shelf with a rag and came away with gray water. She clipped the curtain back up after shaking it over the tub. The lamp from the market worked if the cord was held a certain way at the base, which felt honest enough for Brine Court. Gabe, half awake at some point, pushed the bedroom door open, squinted at them, and slid an old plastic drawer unit into the hall with his foot without asking what they were doing. Then he closed the door again and left them to it.

When Mira unpacked the pillow, a faint smell of camphor and somebody else's closet came off the fabric. Lin pressed the case flat with both palms.

"What now?" Mira asked.

Lin's fingers stayed on the cloth, moving over the trim as if checking for a satin edge that was not there. "She kept rubbing the satin edge of a blanket till it went gray," they said. "Somebody hummed low so the baby wouldn't hear the pipes knock. There were rust spots on the wall where the radiator spat. She hated that song and asked for it every night."

Mira looked at the cheap floral blanket in her hands. It had no satin edge, only thinning synthetic trim. Lin was not claiming sameness. It was only contact.

"How old was the girl?" she asked.

"Small enough to kick the wall in her sleep." Lin swallowed. "Maybe not small. Maybe I only feel her feet because she was on top of somebody when she slept."

Mira folded the blanket once, then again, suddenly careful with it in a way the blanket did not deserve.

"You don't have to tell me every time," she said.

"Do you want me to stop?"

"No." She set the blanket on the cot. "I just don't want you thinking you owe me a report."

Lin nodded, though they looked as if the difference between telling and reporting still needed translation.

They hung the blue hoodie on the hook at the end of the nook. The one clean shirt Mira had left herself went beside it. The cheap metal bar dipped a little under the extra weight. Something pulled tight under Mira's ribs, ugly and soft at once, and she had to crouch by the floor before Lin could look at her too closely.

She crouched by the floor and shoved the tin of coins farther under the cot with her fingertips. Twenty-three dollars and change was emergency money, not much of an emergency against anything that would actually come through the door. The copied key in her pocket seemed suddenly loud, brass against denim, commitment with teeth.

Lin picked up the little paperback from the shelf. "Do you want this somewhere else?"

"No. Leave it."

They turned it over, reading the back with the care they brought to everything written. Their reading had been functional in the laundry, all labels, monitors, and warnings. Here they lingered. Mira watched their mouth move once over an unfamiliar word, silently testing shape before sound.

"Can I ask something?" Lin said.

"You just did."

Lin looked up, wary again, unsure whether that answer had been permission.

Mira exhaled. "Yes."

Lin held the book against their chest. "What happens to unwanted bodies?"

The radiator knocked once in the wall. Mira shifted and felt the copied key tap her thigh through the denim. From the bedroom came Gabe's muffled cough, then quiet again.

"Why are you asking that now?" Mira said.

Lin glanced around the nook. At the cot, the curtain, the crates that had been moved out to the hall. "Because you've spent money you don't have and given me your bed," they said. "I need the real answer before I put my weight on any of it."

Mira sat down on the bare floorboards because standing had become stupid. The wood pressed lines into the backs of her thighs through her pants.
