"Officially?" she said. "Returned bodies get logged, drained, checked for recoverables, then pulped if nobody can lease them again without losing money."

Lin did not move.

"Unofficially," Mira went on, because the tidy lie had already failed between them too many times today, "they get held. Reassigned. Stripped for parts. Sometimes passed around under cleaner paperwork than they deserve. Sometimes nobody even bothers changing the name on the damage history before they send them back out."

Lin's face stayed very still. That was one of their stress tells already, Mira saw. Stillness so complete it turned into its own form of bracing.

"And if a body is..." Lin searched for the word. "Not compliant with that?"

Mira knew too many answers: sedation, wipe, pulp, shelves, the cool filtered smell of CommonFrame disposal, men in aprons hosing pink residue down drains built for scale.

"Then the people who own the paperwork decide what counts as a defect," she said.

"Do you?"

The first lie rose clean and familiar: defect, salvage, inventory. Mira bit it back and felt the cut edge of the copied key press her thigh through the denim.

"No," she said.

Lin waited.

Mira pulled the new key out of her pocket. It was warm from her leg. It was cheap brass, and one fresh cut edge was still slightly sharp. She had put it on a bent ring with a plastic blue tag from one of Gabe's old locker keys because bare keys vanished too easily in this apartment.

"I got this copied," she said.

The blue tag swung once from Mira's hand. Lin went very still, not understanding yet.

"For the front lock," Mira said. "You lift the door a little with your shoulder and turn twice. If the chain's on from the inside, you don't open unless you know the voice. Mrs. Vale will pretend she needs sugar if she thinks there's gossip to be had. Don't give her sugar."

"Mira."

She put the key in Lin's palm and closed their fingers over it. Their skin was warm and dry from handling laundry and curtain clips and the cheap book.

Lin's mouth parted slightly. "Does this mean I live here?"

The room was too narrow for the question. Gabe slept behind one door, her cot waited behind the curtain, and the copied key warmed Lin's palm.

"It means you're not going back," Mira said. "This is your door now. You leave, you come back."

Lin's fingers tightened around the key. When they spoke, their voice had gone very soft.

"No one's ever given me one of these."

Mira almost said, No one ever gave me one either. That was not true. She had had keys before, plenty of them, and none had ever made her chest feel this tight.

"Well," she said, because if she got more solemn than this she might ruin it, "don't lose it. I can't afford another nine credits this week."

Lin laughed through whatever had tightened in their face. It was not much of a laugh. It was enough.

They stood, crossed the room to the front door, and waited with their hand on the lock for instruction. Mira followed. Gabe's blue tag swung lightly from the ring.

"Lift first," she said.

Lin set their shoulder against the swollen wood, raised it the fraction the lock required, and turned the key once, then twice. The bolt drove home with the same ugly scrape it had always made. Behind them, old receipts slumped on the table under a stub pencil, and Gabe's faded green dock-union shirt hung over the radiator to dry, the gull on the pocket cracked nearly through. The apartment was still cramped, still damp, still carrying too much debt and three people's weight badly. But when Lin looked back at her with the key still in the lock, Mira heard the sound for the first time as welcome.


# Chapter 6

Lin was awake before the tram started screaming down the hill.

They sat at the table in Gabe's old dock-union shirt, the green one with the cracked white gull on the pocket, sorting used receipts by size. The neat stack on the left was for grocery paper, the soft stack on the right for the glossy warranty slips CommonFrame mailed to renters after every disputed fee. The copied key Mira had given them last night sat by the glossy stack, flashing dull brass whenever Lin nudged it with a knuckle. Lin liked the glossy ones because the pen skated. They had written their name three times in block capitals and once in a slanted hand that belonged to somebody older, somebody who had once filled out school forms in a hurry.

"Do offices always smell cold?" they asked without looking up.

Mira had one shoe on and her lunch jammed into the side pocket of her work bag. "This one does."

Lin considered that seriously, then wrote COLD on the back of a tram notice and underlined it twice as if it might help.

Gabe came out of the bedroom dragging his left foot a little, brace still open under his T-shirt, hair pressed flat on one side. He had a spoon in his mouth and a bowl of dry cereal in his hand because milk cost more than cereal did this week. "You going in early or hiding from us?"

"Audit."

"That's worse."

Gabe was right. The audit had started as an ugly rumor on Friday, passed desk to desk with the same flat voice people used for mold, layoffs, and surprise fee reviews. People whispered about missing disposal paperwork, a return packet that did not reconcile, and someone from compliance staying late. Yesterday an all-staff notice had come through with three passive verbs and no names. Records were being reviewed. Access would be standardized. Staff were expected to cooperate fully.

Lin looked up then. Their lighter iris caught the kitchen's weak morning bulb and held it. "Should I not open the door?"

"Don't open it unless Jo knocks first or Gabe says so."

Gabe tipped his bowl toward them. "And if some bastard says he's from maintenance, ask him which apartment flooded last month. If he doesn't know, he can go to hell."

Lin smiled at that, small and real. They had not yet learned how much of neighborhood knowledge was test and how much was affection. Mira lifted the key from beside the receipts and put it back in their palm anyway. It was still rough at the edge where the kiosk machine by the canal market had worried it. Lin closed both hands around it as if it might leak.

"I'm serious," Mira said.

"I know."

She did not kiss their head or say anything that would make the room softer than it was. Gabe was already calculating how long he could keep the brace off before his back punished him for the vanity. The sink had a plate crusting over in bean grease. Rent still existed. Mira took her bag and left before she could stand there thinking about keys like they meant anything against a warrant.

Outside, Cordera smelled of salt pushed uphill through fryer grease and the canal's machine stink. The annex sat three tram stops down in its usual posture, all white panel and glass trying to look cleaner than the work inside it. On claim-heavy mornings the lobby usually held renters in expensive coats pretending not to look at the bargain-line queue. Today it held employees and security bins.

People filed toward the tables without the usual lobby sarcasm, lunches held out in both hands.

Two guards from building services stood behind folding tables with gray tubs for bags and lunches. The younger one, a woman with a prayer scar on her chin and glitter caught in the seam of one eyebrow, kept apologizing in the exact same sentence to every person she stopped, which made the whole thing feel worse. Mira put her bag down, watched the lid come off her lunch, watched a gloved hand lift the wrapped sandwich Jo had sent with her and squeeze it like contraband could be hidden in bean spread.

"Randomized enhanced exit and entry review," the guard said. She was chewing fennel seeds; Mira could smell them through the filtered lobby air. "Thank you for your patience."

"You keep saying that like anybody has a choice," someone from billing muttered.

The guard's mouth twitched but she did not rise to it. Human resources must have drafted the sentence and told her not to improvise.

Mira got her bag back and badged through. The green light took half a beat longer than usual to answer. It was not enough to mean anything. It was plenty to mean something.

The claims floor had been combed into false neatness. Weekend return bins that normally overflowed with tagged limbs and sealed fluid jars had been lined into straight metal grids. Audit seals sat over cabinet latches in strips of orange that would tear if opened. The smell was the same filtered cold as always until it wasn't, until something sweet and rotten from the intake lab drifted through the vents and reminded everybody what the building really did. Nobody had music on. Nobody had customer scripts open on their side screens. A compliance checklist glowed on every monitor like a threat in bulleted form.

Celia's assistant did not make Mira wait. She rose at once and opened the glass door, which was how Mira knew the morning had already gone bad.

Celia Rusk's office wall was glass on two sides, a kindness only if you liked being visible. She stood when Mira came in, which she almost never did for staff. Her silvering hair was cut blunt at the jaw and sat exactly where she wanted it. She had one hand wrapped around an expensive fountain pen and the other hidden under the opposite sleeve, likely working the sore knuckles she kept busy when she was angry. A tray of printed packets sat on her desk, all tabbed in different colors, the private weather system of a woman who believed paper could still control disaster.

"Sit down," Celia said.

Mira sat.

Celia slid a file across the desk but kept two fingers on it. "Unit LM-127B."

The file itself was thin. The consequences inside it were not.

"You put this unit on administrative hold eleven days ago," Celia said. "The tissue contamination review never closed. The disposal packet was opened, voided, reopened, and then disappeared between intake and archive. I've spent my morning learning about a body I should never have had to know existed."

Mira kept her eyes on the file, not on Celia. "The marrow haze came back inconsistent on the first pass. I held it for a second wash because I thought the renter file was dirty."

"And then?"

"And then the weekend happened."

"It always does."

Celia's tone did not lift. She could make contempt sound like a scheduling note; the insult landed only after the sentence was over. Mira could hear people on the floor through the glass, chairs moving, keyboard taps held too carefully apart. Her own mouth had gone dry enough that she caught herself pressing the inside of her cheek with her molars, the old tell. She stopped.

"There are three other files," Celia said. "They are not identical, but they are adjacent. They are units you touched, with delayed anomaly codes, disposal lag, and one packet with a chain-of-custody gap. That would have been a manageable humiliation on its own. It's now sharing a room with a Tissue Safety inquiry."

Mira looked up.

"About what?"

"If you don't already know, I'm not going to educate you by accident."

Celia did not answer. She turned the pen in her hand, click after click, and waited to see whether Mira would ask twice.

"I don't," Mira said.

Celia watched her for a beat. Her thumb worried the cap ring of the pen, tiny metal clicks. "Inspector Yaret Sanz from the bureau will be here at eleven. He's reviewing unauthorized repair indicators in returned bargain stock and missing disposal documentation. Because your queue intersects both issues, you will speak to him."

The room stayed the same temperature. Mira felt colder anyway.

"I've got nothing for Tissue Safety," she said.

"I'm sure you'd prefer that to be true."

Celia released the file. Inside were the usual things made malignant by attention: intake stills, tear maps, residue notes, barcodes, timestamps. On top sat Mira's own hold code in her own exact handwriting from eleven days ago. She had used the most ordinary words possible: Secondary wash pending. It looked innocent enough to charge interest.

"Did compliance ask for me by name?" Mira asked.

"Not yet. They asked for the files. The bureau asked who handled them. That is how names happen."

Mira turned one page. A copy of the disposal requisition had a blank square where final pulp confirmation should have been. No forge, no elegant sabotage, just a hole. The kind of hole that drew more hands.

"I can reconstruct the packet," she said. "Records loses things all the time."

"Records doesn't lose four linked discrepancies and then invite a regulator in for lunch." Celia leaned back. "Do not insult me with a clerical weather report."

Celia's courtesy skinned back for a beat. Under it sat the worker who knew every cheap theft a broke office could breed. Then the polish sealed over again.

"Then what do you want from me?" Mira asked.

"Precision." Celia folded her hands. "I want a version of events that is narrow, boring, and supportable. I want to know whether there is a stupid story that can still be cut off before someone from the city turns it into a criminal one. If you made a sentimental call on a damaged unit, tell me now. Sentiment is easier to bury than fraud."

Lin's hands closing around the key came back so fast it felt like a blow. Mira heard the kitchen chair scrape, smelled Gabe's dry cereal. She kept her own hands flat in her lap because if she touched the file she might crease it.

"I held a questionable unit too long because I didn't want to bill the wrong renter on bad data," she said. "That's the story."

"Is it the truth?"

Mira should have said yes quickly. Instead she did what she always did under pressure and reached for procedure like a rail. "There was residue mismatch and undocumented scar tissue. I needed a second wash to avoid an invalid fee assessment."

Celia's eyes narrowed by less than a millimeter. "That was not my question."

"It's my answer."

For a moment Mira thought Celia might smile. She did not. She tapped the file once with the pen and then winced, the smallest tell, as if the movement had sent pain into her knuckles.

"You're not good at lying," Celia said.

"I didn't know that was in the job description."

"Everything is in the job description if it protects the company."

She said company the way priests said God in bad neighborhoods: as both shelter and appetite.

"Here's what will happen," Celia went on. "You will continue your shift. At ten-thirty you will pull an anomaly crosswalk from archives for these four units and any linked disposal tickets. You will bring the summary directly to me. At eleven you will sit with Inspector Sanz. You will answer only what is asked. You will not volunteer. You will not speculate. And if there is anything in your life that has touched unlicensed tissue work, this is the last quiet room in which I can hear it."

Mira kept her face still. Not because she was calm, but because if she let it move, it might move toward home.

"My life is payroll and rent," she said.

"Yes," Celia said softly. "That is true of most crimes worth committing."

When Mira stepped back onto the floor the room seemed louder, though nobody had raised their voice. Chairs rolled. Seal printers spat labels. Somewhere in intake, a returned leisure frame hit a metal tray with the dull lovely weight of fresh meat, and someone swore under their breath because the sound was always worse when it still felt warm.

Mira sat at her station and brought up the day's queue. The audit banner sat across the top of the screen in municipal yellow. ACCESS STANDARDIZATION IN EFFECT. PRESERVE ALL NOTES. DO NOT MODIFY HISTORICAL ENTRIES WITHOUT AUTHORIZATION.

The file on LM-127B was flagged with three separate icons now: internal review, disposal discrepancy, external inquiry support.

Names happened. Celia had been right about that.

***

At ten-twenty-eight, Mira printed the archive request and went downstairs.

The records corridor was colder than the rest of the building because old tissue data still had to pretend it was material. Half the bulbs in the ceiling had a faint blue cast that made everybody look briefly dead. Archive techs moved in fleece vests over office clothes, practical about the cold in a way management always forgot to be. Nasrin Vale was at the service window with a thermos tucked in the crook of one arm and two tiny silver fish sewn crookedly onto the cuff of her cardigan. She sewed one onto every cardigan she owned, one of the processors had once told Mira, because her son believed clothes without fish got lonely in the wash.

"Claims apocalypse?" Nasrin asked, taking the request sheet.

"Apparently."

Nasrin scanned the code and clicked her tongue. "Rusk wants it herself?"

"Direct handoff."

"Then she's afraid." Nasrin lowered her voice and leaned in just enough for Mira to smell clove tea. "When they start asking records for chain-of-custody history before lunch, somebody in a good blouse is afraid."

Mira managed half a smile.

"Bay three," Nasrin said. "You still remember how to mount an archive pull?"

"I'm not that old."

"You're claims old. Different species."

Bay three sat behind a partial screen of frosted plastic. The station was a flat black console, a reader slot, one haptic pad, and a purge light that blinked red when the cold store was spinning up. Mira badged in, keyed Celia's requisition, and waited while the system called old data up out of storage with the soft insect whine of expensive equipment doing something it hated.

On the screen, four serials appeared. LM-127B at the top. Three other units below it that Mira recognized by body damage before memory: a torn quadriceps, a split scalp seal, a throat bruise logged as pressure artifact because the renter had money and the escort agency had more lawyers than shame. All four linked out to disposal tickets with missing terminal verification. All four had anomaly codes entered late or corrected after initial filing.

Celia had asked for a crosswalk. She had asked for it to be boring, narrow, and supportable.

Mira stared at the anomaly fields instead.

The same residue cluster kept surfacing in different combinations: a childhood image, a cross-sex contact pattern, an undocumented scar, drowning panic. She had seen those things one body at a time, ugly and private. In a table they looked like inventory. Her stomach turned not from philosophy but from the very practical obscenity of columns making a market out of people's leaked lives.

There was a filter tab for secondary routing. Claims almost never used it; it belonged to disposal and salvage. Mira clicked it anyway.

The screen asked for elevated authorization. Celia's requisition token was still live because archive pulls stayed open until the requesting supervisor signed for them upstairs. Mira hesitated long enough to know she was doing something stupid and then pressed her thumb to the pad.

Access granted.

The four tickets branched open.

LM-127B: PULP HOLD -> DISPOSAL REVIEW -> ROUTE OVERRIDE W9-SALV/RICH.

The second unit: ROUTE OVERRIDE W9-SALV/MEM.

The third: W9-SALV/RICH.

The fourth: W9-SALV/COMPOSITE.

They were warehouse codes, not public ones. They were internal transfer shorthand, the kind claims was not supposed to see because once people who billed fees started asking where damaged bodies actually went, somebody higher up had to spend the week lying.

Mira felt the room tighten. The feeling was physical, not abstract fear: the cold desk edge against her forearms, the console heat at her knees, the knowledge that Lin had once been a route override with a different name.

She pulled the official summary first because she still understood sequence. The terminal wrote four pages to a clean report file and sent them to print at Nasrin's station. Then a smaller prompt opened at the bottom of the screen:

TEMPORARY ARCHIVE MIRROR LOADED. INSERT SERVICE WAFER FOR LOCAL REVIEW OR PROCEED TO PURGE.

Claims used service wafers when the network stuttered or a supervisor wanted to carry raw data into a room without trusting the building cloud. Thin amber circles in paper sleeves, cheap enough to treat like office junk until one of them held something ruinous. A single supervisor sleeve sat clipped to the side of the console for off-network handoff. Mira stripped it open with her thumbnail. Her fingertips were dry from the cold; she nearly dropped the wafer.

If she wrote the mirror to the wafer and logged it properly, the system would stamp it, number it, and attach it to Celia's requisition. If Celia got it, Legal got it. If Legal got it, it vanished into a folder named privilege and came back six months later as a sentence designed to bill a poor person for breathing wrong.

If she did nothing, she went upstairs with four boring pages and lost the only thing in front of her that looked like a door.

Nasrin laughed somewhere beyond the screen, low and tired. A printer started, stopped, started again.

Mira slid the wafer into the slot.

WRITE MIRROR TO LOCAL MEDIA? the screen asked.

Yes.

Progress climbed in a blue bar. Forty-two percent. Fifty-eight. Eighty-one. At ninety-four the slot clicked and the wafer pushed halfway out. The purge light changed from red to amber and a second line of text appeared.

SCAN MEDIA SERIAL TO COMPLETE LOG.

Of course it did. The mirror wrote first and the chain of custody came after, when somebody married the sleeve barcode to the requisition. Celia's live token had opened exactly that loophole. Her hand was already sweating against the cold plastic edge. She thought of Celia saying You're not good at lying and of Gabe eating cereal dry because milk cost money they did not have.

Mira took the wafer.

The console barked in offense.

MEDIA REMOVED BEFORE LOG.

AUDIT EXCEPTION RECORDED. PURGE CACHED MIRROR?

Nasrin's chair scraped back.

"You jammed bay three?" she called.

Mira dropped to one knee as if her lace had come loose, peeled up the foam insole of her left shoe, and shoved the wafer under it. The edge bit the pad below her toes hard enough to make her suck air through her teeth. The torn sleeve paper sliced her thumb when she crammed it into her pocket. Then she slapped PURGE with the same hand and left a small bloody crescent on the haptic pad.

PURGE CONFIRMED. CACHED MIRROR CLEARED. EXCEPTION FLAGGED FOR REVIEW.

Mira let out air through her nose so slowly it hurt.

Nasrin appeared at the edge of the frosted screen, thermos still in hand. Her eyes went from Mira crouched over her shoe to the console banner.

"Bay kicked the sleeve before I could scan it," Mira said. She got the lace tied on the second try and stood. "I purged the mirror."

Nasrin frowned at the haptic pad. "You bled on my station."

"Paper cut."

"Convenient." Nasrin looked at the gray exception tag still hanging in the corner of the screen. "Records will want that note if compliance comes sniffing."

"Then tell them the bay is old."

"The bay is old," Nasrin said. "You are the one who looks guilty."

Mira took that without answering.

"Good. Your pages are ugly."

At the window Nasrin handed over the printed summary and peered at Mira's face.

"You look freezer-burned," she said.

"Didn't bring a sweater."

"That's because claims people think panic is a coat."

Mira took the pages. "Thanks."

"If this building catches fire, records did not start it," Nasrin said, already reaching for the next request. The line came out worn smooth from use, funny on the surface and rigid underneath.

When Mira turned away from the counter, she shortened her stride to keep the wafer from skidding under her toes.

Upstairs, conference room two waited with the door open.

Yaret Sanz was already seated at the far end of the table. He had a municipal badge clipped neat to his jacket pocket, close-trimmed hair, and the stillness of a man who believed movement was a confession. A paper cup of annex coffee sat by his hand untouched. Celia stood by the wall screen with her arms folded, one hand massaging the opposite knuckle so discreetly it might have been nothing if Mira had not just spent ten minutes lying to a machine.

"Ms. Alves," Yaret said. He stood because courtesy was one of his tools. "Thank you for coming."

His voice was soft enough that Mira leaned in before she caught herself. By then he had already made her do the moving.

She put the printed summary on the table in front of Celia and sat.

"This will be brief," Yaret said. "At least at first."

Celia took the pages without looking at them. "My employee has archive work to return to, Inspector."

"Then I will try not to waste her paid time."

There was no edge in the sentence. That made it land harder.

Yaret opened a slim paper folder, not a tablet. Paper again, because everybody got more archaic when they wanted consequence to feel official.

"I am reviewing unauthorized tissue intervention linked to bargain-line returns processed through this annex," he said. "Separately, your employer is reviewing disposal irregularities. Those matters may intersect. I need your account of several files."

"Okay," Mira said.

"You handled claim 77-KP-044, renter Marisol Roldan, six days ago."

Mira knew the file by the shape of the injury before she knew the name. A torn adductor the company had tried to reclassify as misuse because poor people were always accused of using their rented bodies wrong when the factory saved money on tissue.

"Yes."

"Her body was denied extended repair coverage at eleven-forty-three. By nineteen-hundred it had undergone off-license stabilization. Tissue sealant present on return was not CommonFrame issue. Can you explain that?"

"Customers do things after denial."

"I did not ask what customers do in general."

"Then no," Mira said. "I can't explain what happened after she left."

Yaret made a note with a narrow black pen. His handwriting was severe and beautiful. Mira hated him a little for that.

"Did you refer Ms. Roldan to any outside repairer?"

"No."

"Did you suggest that official review could be avoided if she sought unofficial care?"

"No."

The second no stayed on the table and spread. It had to cover the address, the canal, and the favor disguised as a warning. She had not referred Marisol by name. She had written down an address and said, If somebody near the canal still knows old vat work, I didn't tell you that.

Yaret turned a page. "Do you know Tavi Sloan?"

Celia's eyes lifted from the summary for the first time.

Mira kept her own on Yaret. "I know the name."

"From where?"

"He used to work vat maintenance."

"That is not what I asked."

"It's where I know it from."

Yaret regarded her with that maddening care, as if he had all day and only one living specimen to study. "Mr. Sloan's name has appeared in a licensing inquiry connected to unauthorized tissue work in the canal market. Your queue intersects cases that later show his repair signature."

Celia said, "Inspector, my staff are not in a position to authenticate black-market stitch work."

"I am aware of their limitations."

The answer sounded deferential until the insult surfaced underneath it. Celia heard it. Her shoulders altered by a degree.

"For the record," she said, "Ms. Alves is a claims adjuster. She assesses damage and fee exposure. She is not a field operator."

"For the record," Yaret said, "field operators do not usually control anomaly coding, hold statuses, and disposal timing."

He did not raise his voice. He did not have to. The words lay down on the table like evidence bags.

Mira had the absurd thought that if she took the shoe off right now, the wafer would tick against the tile and announce itself.

"I code what shows up in front of me," she said. "If a file lags, it lags. Half this building still runs on patched readers."

Yaret's attention sharpened, though not visibly to someone who did not already fear him. Mira saw it because she was busy surviving him.

"That is an interesting answer," he said.

"It's a true one."

"Perhaps. What is less clear is why you become more exact when the questions get dangerous."

Mira said nothing.

"Most employees in your position get offended," Yaret went on. "Some of them get frightened. You start itemizing process. You answer implications with workflow. It suggests practice."

Celia said, "It suggests competence."

"Sometimes the two overlap."

He slid one photograph across the table. It was not a body, not directly, but a close tissue still from inside a thigh repair, pale matrix knit shut with a stitch style Mira knew from watching Tavi work shoulder-deep in steam and fluorescent spill. At the lower edge of the image a thread of blue lint was trapped in the sealant.

"This was recovered from a return processed here yesterday," Yaret said. "Commercial laundry fiber embedded in illegal repair resin. It is not proof on its own, but it is enough to make me curious about the canal market, Mr. Sloan, and who is steering rejected renters toward places that can keep them walking long enough to miss a bill hearing."

Mira looked at the lint. Blue Hour used blue press cloth because Jo bought it cheap in wholesale lots. There were probably a hundred laundries in the city that used the same color. There were probably ten.

"Do you know Blue Hour Laundry?" Yaret asked.

"Everybody in this neighborhood knows Blue Hour Laundry."

"Do you know it as a place of business beyond laundry?"

"No."

That one felt worse coming out than the others because it had to pass through Jo's name on the way. Mira kept her hands flat on the table to stop them from going to her face.

Yaret let the silence stand. He was good at that. Better than Celia. Celia filled silence to drive people. Yaret left it empty and waited for people to climb in by themselves.

Finally he said, "Has any unit under your handling been removed from formal disposal without authorization?"

"No."

"Has any conscious or semi-conscious overlap subject been concealed from CommonFrame or the bureau?"

The question was so precise it almost made her flinch. Celia went very still against the wall.

Mira heard the annex around them through the conference glass: a cart wheel ticking once per rotation, a printer beeping for more feed paper, somebody laughing too loudly three rooms away because pressure always made at least one idiot louder. Lin in the apartment, writing COLD on the back of notices. She could not tell if Yaret knew anything or was simply good enough at his job to ask the question that would crack the whole room on probability alone.

"No," she said.

Yaret studied her a moment longer, then closed the folder.

"Very well."

He left her nothing to push against, no clean accusation to deny, only the bureaucratic quiet that meant he would keep coming back.

He took out a second document and slid it across to her. Acknowledgment of inquiry. Preservation order. Her name printed there in bureaucratic black, correct spelling, employee ID beneath it. She was required to preserve messages, notes, physical media, and contact records relevant to the named matters. She was prohibited from destroying, altering, or removing company information without authorization. Failure to comply could lead to civil penalties and referral.

The words named civil penalties. Mira thought of the rent ledger tacked above the sink at home with the amount in red marker. Another fine would not feel like law. It would feel like weather with signatures.

"This does not mean you are being charged," Yaret said. "It means your access and your knowledge are now material to an active inquiry."

"That's comforting," Mira said.

"It is not intended to comfort you."

She signed. The pen scratched louder than it should have.

Celia stepped forward only after the document came back across the table. "I will need a copy for compliance."

"You will receive one," Yaret said.

"Now."

"By end of day."

Their eyes met. Celia wanted the copy in her hand now, under her letterhead. Yaret preferred delay, the city's seal arriving when he chose. They recognized the move in each other.

Mira stood because the room had become too small to sit in.

"Am I done?" she asked.

Yaret looked at her as if the answer might be philosophical. "For this interview."

"Go back to your desk," Celia said before Mira could say anything else. "Do not leave the building without clearing it through me."

That sentence hit harder than the preservation order. It came from management, not law, which made it closer.

Mira took her copy of the notice and went.

***

The rest of the afternoon acquired the ugly slow motion of injury. People on the floor had heard enough to know something had happened and not enough to know what, which was the most efficient way to turn colleagues into furniture. Nobody asked directly. One of the intake women, Inez, pushed a pack of stale crackers onto Mira's desk without comment. Inez always clipped the corners off her ID photos before laminating them because she thought her own face looked kinder in an oval. Mira took one cracker and left the rest where they were.

The next file in Mira's queue was a bargain leisure shell with a split shoulder seam and a renter already contesting damage fees from the lobby phone. The intake stills were too flat to price honestly. She tagged the claim for bench review, took the print slip, and crossed to the lab door with her badge between two fingers.

The reader blinked green, thought better of it, and struck red across the glass.

Mira badged again. The lock gave a sour little buzz and stayed shut.

Inez was behind her now with a tray of sealed sample jars balanced against her hip. "Mira?"

A compliance aide peeled himself away from the copy station before Mira could answer. He wore one of the temporary blue lanyards and held a tablet to his chest like a warrant he was still breaking in. "Ms. Alves, don't reattempt," he said, soft enough to sound polite, loud enough for half the row to hear. "Your lab permissions are supervised pending audit review."

For a second Mira stayed there with the dead badge in her hand, looking at her own reflection blurred in the door glass. Chairs stopped rolling behind her. Someone in billing found a sudden need to cough into her sleeve.

"I need a physical look on this seam," Mira said. "The stills won't tell me if it's pre-strike fray."

"Then route it through intake," the aide said. "Or ask Director Rusk to accompany."

Inez shifted the tray higher against her ribs. "I can walk it in."

Every eye not looking at Mira was looking at her. She handed Inez the slip. "Bench three. If the lattice is feathered under the split, waive half."

Inez took it without pity, which helped. "Yeah."

When Mira got back to her station, a municipal-yellow notice was already open across her monitor: ACCESS RECLASSIFIED. SUPERVISED PENDING AUDIT REVIEW.

At three-forty Celia sent a one-line instruction: OFFICE. NOW.

Mira went in carrying nothing.

Celia had removed her jacket. Without it the office looked slightly more honest. The sleeves of her blouse were rolled once, exposing forearms that had long ago belonged to somebody who lifted boxes, not just papers. The official summary Mira had pulled from archives lay on the desk with notes in the margin and one page circled so hard the pen had nearly cut through.

"Sit."

Mira sat.

"Internal compliance has opened an employee review attached to the external inquiry," Celia said. "You're the named staff subject for the disposal chain variance. Congratulations on becoming administratively interesting."

Mira let the word subject settle. In bureau type it looked neat enough to file and mean enough to follow her home.

"Are you suspending me?"

"Not today."

"Tomorrow?"

"That depends on what else I learn tonight."

Celia picked up the fountain pen, rotated it once, set it down again. Her arthritic hand had started to swell at the knuckles; the skin looked shiny over the joints. She was angry enough to hurt.

"Your archive summary is too careful," she said. "Records also sent up the bay-three exception you threw under my requisition."

Mira kept her face blank. "You asked for careful."

"I asked for useful. These route overrides shouldn't have been visible on a claims pull, and yet here they are, trimmed into a polite four-page packet with every dangerous implication sanded off. Either you saw more than you gave me or you did not understand what you were looking at. Which embarrasses me less?"

The building hummed around them. Mira could hear a vacuum somewhere in the hall, the night cleaning crew starting early around the conference rooms because compliance hated dust almost as much as it hated witnesses.

"You told me narrow and supportable," Mira said.

"Do not hide inside my instructions."

Celia leaned forward. "Look at me."

Mira did.

"If this is ordinary employee stupidity, I can still route it. If this is outside work with a tissue operator, I need to know before the bureau arrives with seizure forms. If this is something stranger, and I assure you I'm not too old to imagine stranger, then you need to decide very quickly whether you want me learning it from you or from a man who thinks courtesy is a warrant."

For one wild second Mira thought Celia knew about Lin. She did not think Celia had guessed. She thought Celia knew. She felt her own body make the decision it always made under pressure: not confession, not collapse, but detail.

"The route overrides aren't normal," she said. "That's all I know."

"That is not all you know."

"It's all I can prove."

Proof checked Celia mid-breath. Her thumb stilled on the pen. It was the only dialect she trusted, even when it made her jaw tighten.

"Can prove to whom?" Celia asked.

Mira said nothing.

Celia sat back slowly. "You should have come to me earlier."

"Would that have helped?"

"For me? Certainly."

The honesty in it was ugly enough to be useful. Celia did not mean help you. She meant help me. It almost made Mira laugh.

"Go home," Celia said after a long moment.

Mira blinked. "You just told me not to leave the building without clearing it."

"I'm clearing it. Your access is frozen for the evening. You will report directly to my office at eight tomorrow. You will not contact records. You will not contact disposal. You will not contact any external repair personnel if such people happen to exist in your social life. And, Ms. Alves" - Celia's voice lowered, roughened just enough to show the old neighborhood under the polish - "if you've done something that can still be contained, tonight is the hour for understanding that containment is a finite resource."

Mira stood.

"Is that advice?" she asked.

"No. It's class solidarity badly deformed by management."

That almost did make Mira laugh, which would have been a mistake. She nodded once instead and turned for the door.

"One more thing," Celia said.

Mira looked back.

"Security's checking bags, pockets, and badge clips on exit. That's audit protocol. If you were considering any romantic notions about saving yourself with a thumb drive, you are too late."

Mira's stomach dropped. She made herself shrug.

"I don't own anything that romantic."

"No," Celia said. "You don't."

The lobby had changed shifts. The young guard with glitter in her eyebrow was gone. In her place stood an older man with nicotine-stained fingers and a cuff of careful hand-stitched embroidery peeking from under his uniform sleeve, tiny orange flowers on black thread. He inspected bags the way a tired uncle might inspect spoiled food: thoroughly, without appetite. Mira set hers on the table, unclipped her badge when he asked, and turned out her pockets. Sandwich wrapper, work notes, a cracked lip balm, a tram card gone soft at one corner, the inquiry notice folded twice, the badge still warm from her blouse. He turned each thing over and handed it back.

"You can keep the balm," he said. "We're not monsters."

His voice held a rasp from old smoking and maybe choir practice. Mira almost thanked him for the joke. Then Yaret stepped through the side door from the administrative corridor and the lobby shrank around the sight of him.

He was alone now, coat over one arm, expression unchanged from the conference room. He saw Mira at the table, saw the open bag, saw the guard's hands in it. If he found any satisfaction in the scene, he kept it private.

"Ms. Alves," he said. "We may need another conversation tomorrow."

"I'm touched."

"Do not destroy anything tonight."

"You already made me sign the part where I don't."

"Signatures help some people remember."

He said it mildly. He could have been discussing weatherproofing.

The guard zipped Mira's bag and returned it. "All set."

Mira slung it over her shoulder and walked out without hurrying. Years of rent had trained her to look ordinary while bad news chewed through her.

Outside, the canal wind came hard around the annex corners and shoved filtered office air out of her lungs. "Alive for who."

"People who couldn't pay premium. Cleaners, dock crews, sex workers, anybody who needed a stronger back or younger knees for a weekend and didn't have twelve grand to burn." He rubbed hard at his chest like he could scrape the truth into cleaner shape. "And for the company. For profit. Both things were true. That's the part I can't pretty up for you."

Mira looked at him and saw two men occupying one outline. One had spent the last months stitching strangers because nobody else would touch them. The other had once stood in a filtered CommonFrame room and helped turn retained memory into a viable price point.

"So when bodies started coming back wrong," she said, "you already knew it wasn't an accident."

"I didn't know all of it. I didn't know the scale, or that people would wake up and name themselves." His voice roughened. "But I knew there was no version of the bargain line that was clean the way they sold it. I knew the anomalies weren't random. And I didn't tell you."

"No."

"Because I wanted Blue Hour to exist. Because I wanted you to keep looking at me the way you did before you knew what I was." He swallowed. "Because I was a coward."

Mira almost told him not to use that word like it solved anything. She did not. He had earned it if he wanted it. What he had not earned was forgiveness arriving quickly enough to make this feel manageable.

Out front, Jo banged the register drawer shut and shouted to no one in particular, "If either of you are dead back there, bleed quietly. I'm locking up."

The ordinary absurdity of it nearly split Mira in half. She pressed one hand to the table to steady herself. The towel beneath her palm was still warm where her body had been.

"You should've told me before," she said.

"I know."

"Before Lin. Before tonight. Before any of it."

"I know."

His eyes were on her now without defense. He was not asking her to absolve him. He was not even asking her to stay. That honesty was worse in some ways. It was easier to fight a lie than a man who had finally stopped using one.

Mira thought of Luz on the stool saying the body got wet before I did. She thought of the official terms for that: residual excitation, nonreportable arousal carry unless accompanied by visible mechanical failure. She thought of conference men and claim codes and all the ways a city taught poor people to live inside borrowed damage. Tavi had helped build one of those ways. Then he had spent years with blood on his hands trying to narrow the harm. Both facts sat there together. Neither canceled the other.

"Did you profit?" she asked.

He gave her a bleak look. "For a while."

"How much."

"Enough to move out of a room with mold in the walls. Not enough to keep it once I started talking back."

It was such a Tavi answer. Material, shameful, impossible to romanticize. Mira hated him a little for that too, for being specific where vagueness would have made anger easier.

"Don't tell me what I should do with this," she said.

"I wasn't going to."

"Good."

Jo's footsteps came to the back hall, practical shoes slapping tile. She stopped outside the curtain but did not enter. "I'm setting the alarm. If either of you is still in here in ten minutes, congratulations, you officially count as inventory."

"He already has experience with that," Mira said before she could stop herself.

Silence from the stool. Then Tavi let out one short breath that might have been pain or acceptance.

"Ten minutes," Jo repeated. Her steps went away.

Mira picked up her bra from under the table, shoved it into her bag, and finally met Tavi's eyes again. His next breath snagged, and his hands hung useless at his sides. Telling the truth had wrecked him in a different register than sex, and there was no taking it back.

She was still furious, still hot from him, still aware of the exact places his hands had been and the exact shape of the confession sitting between them like contaminated equipment nobody could throw out because they still needed it.

The worst part was the wanting, stubborn and intact. It stayed where he'd left it, alive in the same body that now had to carry what he'd told her.

Tavi shifted forward, saw her face, and stopped himself.

Mira slung her bag over one shoulder. "Tomorrow at eight," she said. "Mrs. Alvarado's knee."

Work was not pardon or dismissal. It was the only sentence she trusted not to lie.

"Yeah," he said. His voice caught and corrected. "Yeah. Eight."

She pushed through the curtain into the darkened laundromat. The machines were all dead now. Without them the room felt too large, the rows of glass doors like blank eyes. Jo was at the front gate with the padlock in one hand and her egg timer in the other, as if time itself might try to cheat her if she looked away.

"You all right?" Jo asked.

Mira almost gave the easy answer. Instead she said, "No."

Jo considered that, then nodded once and filed it away the way she filed unpaid tabs and useful gossip. "Good. Means you noticed something."

Outside, the canal wind had teeth in it. Mira stepped into it with Tavi's taste still in her mouth and the clean ugly fact of him lodged under her ribs: the man who had just held her like she was breakable had once helped CommonFrame calculate how much breakage a bargain body could carry and still turn a profit. By morning Jo would crank the gate back up, and the city would start knocking again.


# Chapter 8

Jo had cranked the gate up at eight; by ten-thirty the line bent around the dead change machine, doubled past the detergent wall, and kept growing. She'd torn the pink ticket roll off the hook she usually used for broken dryers. Blue Hour had run out of respectable ways to pretend it was anything except what it had become.

Mira stood behind a folding card table with a marker in one hand and a cup of coffee gone gray and bitter in the other and tried to sort urgency from shame before either one turned into a fight. Jo had taped butcher paper to the front of the table and written categories in thick black strokes.

`BLEEDING`

`PANIC`

`MOTION`

`MEMORY`

`I DON'T KNOW`

The last column was already longest.

"If you only came to wash your clothes," Jo said to the room at large, "I need you to decide whether your problem is laundry or God. I can only fix one of those in here."

A few thin laughs skated out, plus one offended sniff from a woman hugging a mesh bag of uniforms to her chest. More important, the line stopped trying to fold in on itself, which was what Jo actually cared about. She stood in practical black shoes planted wide on the tile, silver braid down her back, a pencil jammed through it like a skewer, and snapped tickets off the roll with the authority of somebody distributing medicine after a storm.

Mira took ticket twelve from a man who smelled of freezer brine and old aftershave. Noel Lima had a split thumbnail wrapped in athletic tape and a silver tooth that flashed every time the laundromat radio caught an organ chord. He flinched so hard his folding chair scraped backward.

"Not pain?" Mira asked.

"Not exactly." He pressed both fists against his thighs. The borrowed forearms twitched, wanting to rise and guard his face. "Every time I hear church music this body thinks somebody's about to ring a bell and beat the shit out of me. I do fish. I don't even box."

She wrote `PANIC / SOUND TRIGGER` on his ticket. "How long has it been doing that?"

"Since mass. My mother thinks I found religion. I found drywall."

Next was Damaris Vale, who had rented a silver-haired grandmother frame cheap because it was all she could afford for a school-grandparents breakfast. The frame came with soft knuckles and liver spots and a teenager's hands. Those hands kept trying to flick imaginary messages into the air, thumb-fast, restless, slutty with muscle memory. Damaris had stuffed them into oven mitts at some point out of desperation. One mitt was printed with cartoon peppers.

"I smacked my pastor on the ass," she told Mira without preamble. "Not on purpose. These hands got there before I did."

Jo looked up from the ticket roll. "Was he worth smacking?"

"That isn't helping."

"It helped me."

Mira hid a smile and wrote `MOTION / HAND REFLEX LOOP`. Damaris leaned in.

"Can you make them stop doing that little heart shape?" she whispered. "My granddaughter says that's how girls flirt now."

"That wasn't flirting," said a voice from the far end of the table. "That was typing."

Lin had been meant to hand out water and keep the waiting clients from drifting into the back hallway. Somewhere in the last forty minutes they had acquired Jo's spare apron, a legal pad, and half the room's trust. They stood with their hair pinned up under a faded scarf that hid the port at the nape, pencil tucked behind one ear, and listened with the kind of attention that made people hear themselves more clearly. They were not smiling. That was part of why it worked.

"How can you tell?" Damaris asked.

Lin took her hands very gently, oven mitts and all, and watched the thumbs twitch inside the quilted cloth. "Because they're trying to answer someone who isn't here. Flirting usually wants a witness. This is more like reaching for a cup that's not on the table anymore."

Damaris blinked, then barked out a laugh loud enough to startle herself. The restless hands slowed.

Mira had spent the past two weeks trying to keep Lin out of view, out of records, out of the category of notice. Now the room kept turning toward them on instinct. It should have tightened something in her chest. It did, but not enough to make her stop it.

The front windows had fogged from washer heat and bodies. Outside, two more people stood under the awning pretending they were only waiting out a passing mist off the canal. Everybody in the queue had that same look now: chin tucked down, face arranged into casual, eyes cutting sideways to see who else had come to an illegal place to ask what was wrong with the body they could not afford to return officially.

Blue Hour smelled like detergent, salt dragged in on shoes, fryer grease from the market uphill, and the sweeter, uglier edge of tissue solvent seeping under the back-room curtain every time Tavi opened it. The machines hammered and sighed. Somewhere, a toddler complained in steady outraged pulses while his mother fed quarters into a washer and refused to lose her place in line.

Mira turned sideways to let a basket of towels pass and caught sight of Tavi through the curtain gap. He was bent over the wetbench, long fingers inside a client wrist assembly, burn scar bright against his right forearm where he'd pushed his sleeves up. He looked up as if he had felt her looking. Last night came back to her with stupid bodily precision: his mouth under her jaw, the weight of him, the afterward, and then the confession laid over all of it like a second sheet she could not kick free.

He tilted his chin toward the queue. "How bad?"

"Worse than an audit. Better than a fire."

"That's not a category."

"It is today."

His grin showed for a second, loose and tired and almost enough to make him look uncomplicated. Then he saw the room more fully and the grin dropped. "You want first bench on Noel or oven-mitt lady?"

"Take Noel. If he hears another hymn in here and swings on the dryers, Jo will bill us."

"She already does."

He vanished behind the curtain again. Mira hated how easy their work voices still fit. She hated more that the fit helped.

Ticket by ticket the room tried to become a system. A bike courier in a stolen-looking office frame could not stop licking his own molars because the mouth remembered cigarettes he had never smoked. A market porter wearing a glamorous club body three sizes too elegant for his boots kept dropping to one knee every time anybody raised a voice, the frame apparently trained by some old proposal package or apology kink that had leaked through wash. A janitor with bleach-burned cuticles had a rental arm that saluted every time the tram shrieked downhill outside.

Each time the porter went down, he came up grinning too hard and brushing lint off elegant knees that had never been meant for Jo's tile. Across town the same glitch would have happened behind hotel blackout curtains, with a recovery aide pretending not to watch; here Jo nudged him upright with her shoe and told him if he was going to propose in her laundromat he could at least bring flowers.

What Lin did was not softness. Mira saw that almost at once. Lin did not soothe by lying. They sorted. They asked the question beneath the question.

"When your mouth starts tasting smoke," they asked the courier, "does it happen before or after you think about the package?"

"After."

"Then your fear probably grabbed the first door it saw. Sit by the fan. And stop checking your tongue every few seconds. That's like worrying a bad tooth. It tells your body the smoke matters."

The courier obeyed as if Lin had handed down code.

To a woman who had come in shaking because the borrowed legs under her office skirt had begun climbing stairs that were not there, Lin said, "Do the stairs arrive in your knees or in the room first?"

The woman frowned, surprised into honesty. "Knees."

"Good. That's smaller. Sit with your feet flat and let me know if the room lies afterward."

"Good, that's smaller" was exactly the kind of sentence frightened people needed, and Mira stole it immediately because it reduced the fear without insulting anyone.

By noon the butcher paper had new arrows, new notes, and coffee-ring maps of failure all over it. Jo had repurposed two overturned laundry baskets into seating. The woman with the mesh bag of uniforms had come back from the shop uphill with a sack of empanadas and was passing them around the queue without asking names. The actual laundry customers had either fled or surrendered and were now doing wash in companionable disgust beside people discussing alien hand habits and unauthorized grief.

Lin moved from chair to chair, taking symptoms in a neat cramped hand. They never rushed the humiliating part, which meant the humiliating part came out faster. People who had spent three days hiding from official claims clerks or spouses or bosses found themselves telling Lin the truth before they told Mira the billable version.

Mira watched Noel let Lin adjust the angle of his elbows so his borrowed arms would stop climbing into a guard. She watched Damaris hand over the oven mitts without being asked twice. She watched one man in a borrowed funeral suit, huge and sweating and near tears, admit to Lin that the body kept trying to kneel whenever anybody mentioned forgiveness.

Jo came to Mira's shoulder with a stack of torn tickets and said out of the corner of her mouth, "If your tall one leaves the front, they mutiny."

"They're not my tall one."

"Then whose miracle are they? Because I would like to invoice."

Mira looked down the room. Lin was crouched beside a woman old enough to be their mother, explaining something with both hands open as if demonstrating how to carry a bowl without spilling it. The woman's breathing had already slowed to match theirs.

"They're good with frightened people," Mira said.

Jo snorted. "Honey, that's because they're one of the only people in here who doesn't act embarrassed about fear."

The line shortened by three. It lengthened by four. A produce clerk in a green apron knocked on the window and held up a wrist wrapped in a dish towel already leaking through.

Mira took a breath that tasted like hot cotton and canal salt and solvent and knew with perfect financial clarity that Blue Hour had become the sort of place people gave directions to.

***

By two, the pink tickets were up to thirty-one and Jo had written `NO WALK-INS AFTER 4` on the butcher paper even though everybody in the room understood that they would take walk-ins after four if the walk-ins looked desperate enough.

The rain never quite started. It hung over Cordera as a wet thought, close enough to make the air tacky, not close enough to clear the block. Blue Hour's front door kept breathing in more bodies. The bodega owner sent over a plastic crate of bottled water in exchange for getting his sister moved up the list. A salon girl from halfway down the canal came to the window just to ask whether the rumors were true about the place behind the dryers where they fixed carryover cheap.

Mira was writing up aftercare instructions for a tram mechanic whose borrowed shoulder had learned to duck from blows that never came when the door opened and let in a woman with municipal credentials clipped to the breast pocket of a tray-green rain shell.

She had square navy nails chipped at the corners, damp trouser hems, and a stainless lunch thermos under one arm plastered with faded cartoon sardines. The thermos was so battered it had probably outlived marriages. Her badge read `ELSA PAREDES, MUNICIPAL HEALTH AND SANITATION`.

Everybody in line performed normal badly and all at once.

The room did not fall silent. That would have looked deliberate. Instead it grew louder in stupid places. A washer hit its spin cycle with the violence of an alibi. Damaris laughed too hard at something no one had said. One of the salon girls began folding a towel that did not belong to her into an ever tighter rectangle.

Paredes stood just inside the door letting her eyes move over the fogged windows, the extra chairs, the paper cup of tongue depressors Jo had forgotten to hide, the red biohazard sack half tucked under the card table.

"Afternoon," she said. Her voice was tired rather than sharp. "I got a complaint about crowding, bodily waste near public washers, and somebody doing wet work without a partition."

Jo was already moving. "You got a complaint from who, the woman upstairs who thinks steam is a moral problem?"

"Anonymous, which means probably yes."

"Then you know how much I care."

Paredes did not smile. "I still have to look."

Mira felt the inside of her cheek meet her teeth. Lying always made that spot raw by the end of the day.

"We're a laundry," she said, too fast, and corrected. "Obviously. One of the folding tables collapsed and cut my brother's hand. The crowd is because two machines are down and the rain's thinking about happening."

Paredes looked at the butcher paper categories.

"Do your broken machines usually produce a `MEMORY` column?"

Jo leaned over and slapped a new sheet right on top of it. "That's for my church ladies. They do a little grief thing after wash."

"At two in the afternoon on a Tuesday."

"Death doesn't keep office hours."

That, annoyingly, landed. Paredes's face shifted by half a millimeter, enough to show she had either buried somebody recently or knew somebody who had. Then her gaze slid toward the back curtain where solvent smell drifted around the edge in a sweet medicinal ribbon.

"I'd still like to see the rear."

Mira opened her mouth and never got to use whatever lie was next, because at that exact moment the inner dryer on the left clicked done and released a bright two-tone chime into the room. Coconut softener steam rolled out when the door popped.

The woman in the cheap ivory wedding shell made a sound Mira felt in her own teeth.

She had come in ten minutes earlier with a courthouse bouquet of baby's breath crushed in one fist and a veil already sliding off one side of her head. Her name, according to Lin's intake pad, was Paz Renteria, twenty-six, electrician apprentice, and she needed the body for wedding photos and the reception because the base line had a twelve-week wait she could not pay to skip. Her wife, Esme, stood beside her in a suit jacket gone damp at the shoulders, one bobby pin after another tucked into her breast pocket because she kept rescuing them from Paz's hair and did not know what else to do with her hands.

When Paz had sat down she was flushed and embarrassed and trying to joke about the bust seam biting under her arm. Now the softener steam hit her and the joke went clean out of her face.

"No," she whispered to nobody in the room. Then louder: "No, don't start with the balcony."

Her body folded forward so violently the bouquet stems snapped. Baby's breath sprayed over the tile. Esme lunged for her and got an elbow full of satin and borrowed panic.

"Paz, hey. Hey, look at me."

Paz wasn't with her anymore. She was staring into the humid space above the washers as if a hotel room had opened there, something salt-bright and terrible. Mascara dragged down one cheek. The borrowed mouth kept working around sobs that had too much practice in them.

"You said we'd go after breakfast," she said to the empty air. "You said if we walked down to the water first you'd stop being angry. Don't leave with the boat half there. Don't make me hold the stupid flowers by myself."

Every head in the laundromat turned, then tried not to turn, which looked worse.

Baby's breath skittered under the rack of lost socks. Damaris clapped both oven-mitted hands over her mouth hard enough to squeak the quilted peppers, and Noel bent over his knees as if the floor might excuse him from watching a bargain bride beg a ghost not to leave her alone on the water. Jo muttered, "Jesus, honey," and the pity in it came out sounding almost angry.

Paredes took one step toward the scene, officially rather than kindly. "Is she having a neurological event?"

Mira moved before she answered. "She's having a carryover episode."

It was too truthful. She heard that as soon as it left her mouth.

Paredes's eyes sharpened. "A what?"

"A grief thing," Jo said at the exact same time.

Paz tried to stand, snagged the hem of her dress under one heel, and nearly went down. Esme caught her around the ribs and got dragged sideways with her. The room recoiled to make space and found there was no space left to make. The tram mechanic swore. One of the salon girls knocked a laundry basket over with her shin. The toddler from earlier started crying in outraged sympathy.

Paz clutched at Esme's lapels and looked straight through her. "Mateo, please."

Esme's face changed. It wasn't anger. It was worse: the tiny clean cut of being replaced by a ghost in public.

Mira felt the whole mess trying to break open at once. The front room was packed, the back room was illegal, and a municipal official stood at the door while a bride sobbed through somebody else's heartbreak loud enough for the block to hear. Tavi was one curtain away with a client half numb on the bench and no clean way to surface.

She reached for Paz's wrist. The pulse there kicked fast and thin under rental skin. "Paz. Listen to me. You need to sit."

Paz yanked free with surprising strength. "Don't tell me to sit in that chair. He packed while I was in the shower."

Esme closed her eyes for one beat, then opened them and said to Mira in a voice gone flat with fresh married misery, "Can you fix this now? Please tell me you can fix this now."

Paredes took another step. "I need everyone away from her. Ms. Villanueva, if you're conducting unauthorized medical procedures in a public laundry, I'm not going to ignore it because somebody's wearing a veil. I can cite crowding, waste, and occupancy myself. If this is tissue work, I document it and refer it upstairs. I'm not starting a panic in a room this full unless I have to."

Jo planted herself broadside between the inspector and the curtain. "Then ignore it because it's my damn floor and I said wait."

Everything in Mira wanted to move fast and clean. She wanted to drag Paz back, have Tavi do a dampening pass, shut the room, and undo the evidence. But high pressure in Blue Hour never stayed private. It came with audience built in.

And Lin, from the far side of the table, saw that before Mira did.

They took one chair from the circle, set it in the middle of the room, and spoke in a voice pitched not for intimacy but for reach.

"Esme," they said. "Can she hear her own name right now?"

Esme looked at them, startled. "I don't know."

"Then sit with her anyway."

There was no hesitation in them and no apology either. That steadied the room almost by force.

"Noel," Lin said next, already turning. "Turn off the radio."

Noel obeyed instantly. The organ vanished mid-chord.

"Damaris, can you pick up the flowers?"

"With these whore hands? Absolutely."

The room let out the wrong laugh and bought itself one more second.

Lin faced Paredes. "She's not contagious. She's embarrassed."

The sentence hit Mira like cold water. Lin was not arguing facts. They were choosing a category the city knew how to leave alone for sixty more seconds. Shame was cheaper than contagion. Shame could still pass for private.

Paredes blinked, perhaps because nobody usually spoke to her that way in a laundromat. "Embarrassment is not a sanitation category."

"No," Lin said. "But grief's common enough I figured the city had paperwork for it somewhere."

Mira felt the move land a beat late and all at once. Lin was taking the worst thing in the room, public humiliation, and spreading it wide enough that Paz stopped being the single obvious piece of evidence. Crude. Brilliant. Mean in the useful way triage sometimes had to be.

The rest of the plan showed itself when Lin turned back to the room and said, clear as a bell, "All right. If your chair moves, make a circle. If it doesn't, turn where you are. We're doing grief in public for a minute."

For one stunned second nobody moved.

Then Jo, blessedly, understood before everyone else. "You heard them," she barked. "Circle up. If you've ever lost money, dignity, sleep, cartilage, or a man too stupid to stay, congratulations, you're qualified."

Chairs scraped. The line reshaped itself, not neatly, because nothing in Blue Hour ever did. But the room obeyed a new logic because a worse one had just arrived and this was the only available answer.

Paredes stood with her thermos tucked under one arm like a shield, watching poor people reconfigure themselves into something she could not cite without looking like a monster.

Mira caught Jo's eye. Jo jerked her chin toward the back, telling Mira as plainly as words to buy time and move.

Mira got her arm around Paz's waist on one side while Esme took the other. Paz's body fought them half-heartedly, still reaching for a balcony and a boat and a man neither of them knew. Lin stepped closer, close enough for Paz to focus on their face if she could focus on anything.

"Paz," they said. "Is the grief in your chest, or did it get all over the room?"

Paz sucked in a shuddering breath. "Room."

"Good. Then let's move rooms. Sometimes that helps first."

It was the smartest thing anybody had said all day.

They got Paz through the curtain while Jo filled the space behind them with command.

"All right, since we're already here. One thing you lost and one thing you need washed. Keep it short. I'm not your priest."

The first voice was Damaris's, because of course it was.

"I lost the good feeling in my knees around fifty-two, and I need somebody to wash this shame off my hands."

Noel, staring at the silent radio, said, "I lost three teeth and any desire to hear church organs, and I need my work apron cleaned before my mother asks why I'm crying."

The woman with the mesh bag laughed softly into her sleeve. The woman in the office skirt, the one whose knees kept inventing stairs, cried harder. Paredes did not leave. She did not come closer either.

Mira let the sound of the makeshift circle cover the back-room curtain falling shut behind her.

***

The back room held its own weather: warm damp air, hanging sheets to blunt noise, the wetbench lights throwing everything into surgical yellow. Tavi looked up from the client he had just bandaged and took in Paz's dress, Esme's face, and Mira's expression in one quick sweep.

"Jesus," he said. "Front-room bad?"

"Bride bad. Inspector bad. Both at once."

"Of course."

He stripped his gloves off with his teeth and moved to clear space on the side cot. Paz tried to twist away when they lowered her. Satin rasped against vinyl. The smell of coconut softener followed her in like a curse.

"Don't take the room key," she was sobbing. "I paid for the room."

Esme stood at the cot's shoulder with one hand pressed so hard over her own mouth Mira thought she might leave teeth marks in the skin. She still had the courthouse stamp on her wrist, smeared now. She had been married half a day and was already competing with a dead honeymoon.

"Hey," Tavi said to Paz, not unkindly. "Listen to me. I'm going to make the room smaller. That's all. Nobody's stealing anything."

He had that gift in a crisis, the ability to sound like technical instructions were an ordinary kindness. Mira hated him for helping make the systems that broke these people. She also watched his hands and trusted them anyway.

Paz's pupils were blown wide. Her whole body had gone into that ugly overlap rigidity that looked like panic and memory wrestling in the same skin. Mira loosened the beaded neckline enough to get at the pulse points. It was a cheap wedding shell, she thought automatically, with adhesive seams under the lace. It had been a resort package body originally, or some imitation of one. Somebody at CommonFrame had pulped the honeymoon and sold the dress twice.

"Esme," Mira said. "Did the episode start before the dryer opened?"

"No. She was just hot. She kept saying the dress was too tight. Then the smell hit."

"Coconut?"

"I guess. I don't know. Rich-people vacation smell."

"That helps."

Tavi was already drawing a cooling line across Paz's inner wrists with conductive gel. "Probably sensory carryover keyed to hospitality wash. Cheap lace lines get reused from destination packages all the time. They scrub the face, leave the ache."

He said it with enough disgust that Mira almost looked at him, almost let herself. Instead she got a cold pack under Paz's neck.

"Paz," she said. "I'm going to ask you something annoying. Is the water you're seeing daylight or night?"

Paz's breath hitched. "Orange. It was orange."

"Good. Sunset memory, not room memory. Easier to interrupt."

Tavi glanced at her, quick approval passing between them before either could stop it. Work made traitors of clean emotional positions. You could not keep hating somebody in a straight line while their hands helped you.

Outside, muffled through cloth and steam, the grief circle kept going.

"I lost my first apartment to mold and my second to rent," the salon girl from down the canal was saying. "I need this blouse not to smell like fear before my shift."

Jo's voice came after. "That's too long, but accepted."

Even Tavi gave a short unbelieving laugh.

"What's Lin doing?" he asked.

"Saving our asses."

"Good. They're better at front than both of us."

Mira did not argue because she had already learned that. She held Paz's jaw steady while Tavi clipped a small dampening lead behind one ear and watched the woman's face with that ugly precise patience of his.

Esme finally lowered her hand from her mouth. "Is she hearing me at all?"

Mira chose the answer carefully. "Some of her is. Talk to the part that came with you."

Esme stared at Paz for a second like she was deciding whether she could bear that instruction. Then she bent close, took Paz's hand, and said in a voice roughened by humiliation and stubborn love, "Baby, you still owe me half a tres leches cake and one dance where you don't look at a ghost. I'm not letting some dead resort asshole eat my reception."

Tavi barked a laugh before he could help it. Paz made a broken sound that was almost one too. It was good, or good enough. Mira saw the fight inside the borrowed muscles change shape.

"Again," Mira said.

Esme squeezed harder. "You married me in front of Judge Del Toro and your cousin Nia and one florist's bucket we stole from the bodega. You cried before this and those were your tears. Come back for your own tears."

Paz's breath stuttered. Her eyes found Esme's face for a second, lost it, found it again.

The carryover did not vanish. It never did that cleanly. But it loosened enough for her to whisper, hoarse and furious with herself, "I'm so sorry."

"Don't apologize for a body being cheap," Esme said, and that sentence sat in the room like the city itself.

Mira felt it in her spine. Cheap was a design condition, a legal class, and a whole level of acceptable damage.

Through the curtain came Paredes's voice, closer now. "Ms. Villanueva, I heard equipment."

Jo answered immediately. "You heard my old irons. If you want to confiscate those, be my guest, but you'll have to fight every woman on this block for crease rights."

The room outside laughed, not kindly but solidly, with the sound of a neighborhood choosing sides.

Tavi murmured, "She won't leave until she sees enough to call this stupid instead of criminal."

"Can you make it stupid?"

"I can make it less obvious."

He stripped the veil pins from Paz's hair with efficient fingers, handed the wilted veil to Mira, and said to Esme, "Get her out of the dress. Not because I'm charging by the satin, though I should. Because the compression's keeping the loop alive."

Esme stared at him. "In front of everyone?"

"No," Mira said. "Behind the screen."

There was no real privacy in Blue Hour, only kinds of mercy. Mira pulled the hanging cloth panel across and helped Esme work Paz out of the bargain bridal shell inch by inch. The beads scratched Mira's knuckles. Paz cried with quiet mortification now, which was an improvement. Under the dress she wore a plain nude slip, sweat-dark at the spine. Somebody somewhere had marketed the outer layer as romance and the underlayer as practical disposal.

By the time they got Paz into borrowed laundry sweats from Jo's lost-and-found bin, the front-room voices had acquired the rhythm of a real meeting, a bad one improvised out of panic and folding chairs, but real. Mira could pick out Noel's thick waterfront accent, Damaris's scandalized cackle, Jo's corrections, and Lin cutting through with their calm precise prompts whenever anyone spiraled into either too much detail or not enough truth.

"Say whether it's yours or borrowed," Lin was saying. "That matters."

Mira looked at the curtain as if she could see through it.

Tavi followed her gaze. "They're a natural."

"They're visible."

"Yeah." He wiped his hands and met her eyes for the first time since she'd dragged Paz in. "So are we."

There was too much in that sentence: last night, the queue, his confession, the fact that success and failure were now arriving in the same clothes. Mira let none of it open because opening it would take time the room had already spent.

"Help me get her upright," she said.

When they brought Paz back out, wearing gray Blue Hour sweats under her wife's suit jacket and clutching the crushed bouquet stems like evidence, the circle quieted on instinct. The room had changed while she'd been gone. What had started as a cover was now doing actual work. Damaris had taken her oven mitts off and was resting her disobedient hands in plain sight. Noel sat with his forearms loose, no longer braced for bells. Even Paredes, still standing near the door, had set her sardine thermos on top of a washer and stopped looking like she wanted to charge in guns first.

Lin rose from their chair and addressed Paz with the same seriousness they had given everybody else. "Do you want to sit back down or do you want to leave?"

Paz's face went scarlet. "Leave. Forever."

A cleaner laugh passed around the circle.

"Reasonable," Lin said. "Can you walk if Esme takes your left side?"

"Probably."

"Good enough."

Paredes watched the two women move toward the door. "She should still see a licensed neurologist."

Mira almost snapped something useless back. Esme beat her to it.

"With what money?" she said, not loudly. "The wedding body was the money."

Paredes's mouth flattened. She looked at the extra chairs, the folded butcher paper, the people still sitting in their accidental circle. Mira could see her doing the math: a sanitation write-up she could defend, a packed room she could not safely empty by herself, and one more problem obviously meant for a harder office with a worse reputation. Suspicion was still there, and so was recognition, though Mira did not mistake it for approval.

"You need a partition if you're treating bodily events in a public wash space," Paredes said to Jo. "You need separate disposal. You need occupancy posted. That's what I can write tonight. And if I come back and see sharps in a rinse sink, I'm not coming back alone."

"Heard," Jo said.

"And whatever this is," Paredes added, with a glance at the circle, "pick one version before the next complaint. Grief group or laundry. The city hates improvisation."

"The city can eat hot lint," Jo said.

Paredes picked up her thermos, looked like she wanted very badly to answer that in kind, and left instead.

The door shut. The room breathed.

No one applauded, thank God. Instead Noel said, "So, uh. Are we still taking numbers or did grief win?"

Jo rubbed both hands over her face. "Sit down, fish boy. Grief is intermission."

And because the city had no mercy and poor people learned to imitate that efficiency when necessary, the room laughed and kept going.

By the time the next three walk-ins appeared at the window asking whether this was the place that handled carryover without paperwork, there was no honest way left to call Blue Hour secret.

***

They locked the front door at nine-thirty and kept talking through the glass for another twenty minutes, pointing people toward tomorrow, next week, whenever they could plausibly promise bodies and labor they did not yet have.

The laundromat after a crush had the look of a field hospital pretending to be domestic: damp chairs, crumpled paper cups, a trail of baby's breath ground into the grout near the dryers, glitter caught in the drain trap, butcher paper folded and unfolded and written on again, and Jo's lost-and-found bin gutted from emergency wardrobe use. The tram mechanic had left behind a Tupperware container of rice and stewed beans in payment. The bride's aunt had paid cash, damp bills rubber-banded with a wedding favor ribbon.

Mira mopped the spot where Paz had dropped half her bouquet and watched the water go cloudy with lint, dust, and one floating pearl that was probably plastic but had looked real enough under crisis light.

My mother thinks I found religion. I found drywall.

The line from Noel came back to her and made her laugh once, short and unwilling. Exhaustion had a low sense of humor. So did Jo.

"If you start losing it on my floor," Jo said from the card table, where she was stacking the day's intake slips, "I'm charging you group rates."

"How much did we make?"
