Jo counted again, because counting calmed her. "Cash, eight hundred sixty-two. Plus the beans, plus one bottle of decent tequila from the bride's aunt, plus a promise of rewiring from somebody who may or may not actually know electrical work."

Tavi, cleaning the wetbench with solvent and a tenderness that almost looked obscene, whistled low. "That's not terrible."

"It is if it's evidence," Mira said.

"Everything's evidence if you piss off the right office."

He did not say Yaret's name. He did not need to. It sat behind half their decisions already, a quiet man in a clean collar somewhere down the weeks ahead.

Lin sat at the front table with the legal pad turned sideways and both hands around a mug of reheated tea Jo had forced on them. The scarf had slipped a little at the nape from the heat and haste of the day. They looked pale with fatigue, eyes too bright. Good with frightened people did not mean untouched by them.

"Did I make it worse?" they asked.

Mira stopped mopping. "Which part?"

"The part where everyone saw."

There was no use in pretending not to understand. Lin had done something brilliant and dangerous and could see enough to know both were true.

Jo answered first. "Honey, that room was already seeing. You just made them useful."

Tavi capped a solvent bottle with his wrist. "If we're assigning blame, start with the company that sold a honeymoon breakdown to a woman on her wedding day."

Lin looked at the legal pad. "That isn't what I meant."

Mira set the mop aside and came to the table. Up close she could see the pencil dent along the side of Lin's middle finger, the tiny lavender thread caught on the apron pocket, the fatigue gathering in the careful stillness of their mouth. They had spent hours being the calmest person in a room full of public shame. That cost.

"You made it visible," Mira said. "That is not the same thing as worse."

Lin absorbed that, unsatisfied.

"Visible can still get you raided," they said.

Tavi leaned one hip against the bench. "So can doing thirty-one off-book cases in a week and pretending the block hasn't noticed."

"Thirty-four," Jo said without looking up.

"What?"

"Three after the inspector left. I counted."

That shut the room up for a second.

Mira had known the line was bad. Thirty-four made it arithmetic: thirty-four people in a day, thirty-four bodies or borrowed bodies or frightened renters or something in between, thirty-four reasons Blue Hour no longer fit inside the back room in any meaningful sense.

She looked at the folded tickets, the cash, the wetbench light still warming the curtain from behind, and Lin's notes, neat and unsentimental.

`music trigger before panic`

`knees remember stairs`

`grief in room, not chest`

That last one sat in darker pencil as if Lin had pressed harder writing it.

Jo shoved the money into piles with detergent-dried hands. "You can all stop pretending caution is still on the menu the way you mean it."

Mira looked up. "You want to invite the city in?"

"The city was in here this afternoon, querida. She had sardines on her thermos."

That image stayed with Mira: chipped navy nails around a dented lunch thermos, rain dark at the cuffs, Paredes listening to the circle for half a beat before deciding what she could live with writing down. Mira could still feel that half beat in her jaw.

"I want," Jo went on, "for us to stop acting like this is still a side hustle between dryers. We need an actual front. Posted chairs. Posted rules. A better lie or a better truth."

"There isn't a better truth," Mira said.

"Then a better lie."

Tavi dragged a clean cloth over the bench in slow circles. "We also need hours. Real ones. Half the panic today was people not knowing whether they'd be seen before the body got worse. You put fear in a line with no clock and it breeds."

"That's rich coming from you," Mira said before she could stop herself.

He looked at her. There it was again, the thing between them with too many names and not enough time. He could have answered with a joke, an apology, or anger. He chose none.

"Yeah," he said. "It is."

The quiet after that was not comfortable. It was honest enough to work inside.

Jo broke it by sliding the legal pad toward Mira. "Read your tall one's notes."

"They're not my tall one."

"I said what I said."

Mira read. Lin had not just taken symptoms. They had built a triage logic Blue Hour had been fumbling toward for weeks.

`worse with witnesses`

`worse with closed heat`

`needs naming before touch`

`do not sit near radio`

`separate shame from injury`

She felt something ugly and almost hopeful move under her ribs. It wasn't relief; relief was too soft a word for a thing that came with more work attached. It was recognition, infrastructure in pencil.

"You did all this today?" she asked.

Lin nodded once. "People kept answering the same questions in different clothes."

Tavi laughed under his breath. "That might be the whole profession."

Mira turned a page.

At the bottom, after the last numbered ticket, Lin had written one line not about symptoms at all.

`Most clients calm down when somebody tells them the humiliating part is common.`

Mira read it twice. The words sat between a tea ring and a thumbprint of detergent gray, plain as spare parts. She thought of CommonFrame forms with their boxes for misuse, negligence, unauthorized stress. This was the opposite of that. Something people could repeat on a stoop, in a tram seat, outside a courthouse wedding in a dress that pinched.

The front window rattled under a knock.

All four of them looked up.

Outside stood a woman in a canal courier jacket, helmet under one arm, rain finally spotting dark across her shoulders. She had a grocery bag looped over her wrist and held the other hand flat against the glass. Even from inside Mira could see that the fingers were moving a half second ahead of whatever she meant them to do, tapping out some private impatient rhythm.

When Jo cracked the door, the woman did not try to come in. She just asked, "Are the numbers done for tonight?"

Mira could have said yes. She could have told the woman next week. She could have said the damp cash on Jo's table was enough evidence for one day, that Yaret was already nosing around missing disposal records, and that every extra client put Lin's face in one more stranger's memory while the wetbench and Brine Court rent both hung by scraps.

All of that sat ready in her throat.

Then she looked at Jo's cash piles banded with wedding ribbon, at Lin's notes gone soft at the corners, at the courier's bad hand drumming its half-second-ahead rhythm on the glass. The clean sheet under Mira's thumb did not feel like scrap anymore.

Mira took the pad, tore off a clean sheet, and started writing in block letters.

`TUES / WED / THURS`

`6 PM TO LATE`

`TAKE A NUMBER`

`NO CLAIMS PAPERWORK`

She looked at Jo. "You still have the extra folding chairs?"

"In the storage cage."

"Bring them out tomorrow."

To Tavi she said, "Can you build a second privacy screen?"

"Out of what?"

"Out of whatever you're always stealing from the world."

The ghost of his grin came back. "Yeah. I can."

She turned to Lin. "Front intake is yours if you want it. But we change the scarf. Too hot. We'll find something else."

Lin blinked. "You want me at the front?"

"You're already at the front."

The courier woman outside shifted from foot to foot in the rain. Mira walked to the door, handed her the fresh sheet so she could read the hours, and when the woman looked up with the desperate caution of somebody expecting to be turned away, Mira answered before prudence could rehearse its case.

"Tomorrow at six," she said. "Tell the others to come early."


# Chapter 9

Blue Hour after close smelled of boiled lint, cautery gel, and the sweet old leak from the steam line Jo kept meaning to bully the landlord into fixing. The courier woman's rainwater still marked the threshold where she'd stood reading tomorrow's hours off the fresh sheet Mira had handed her. Mira had wiped the wetbench twice and still there was pink under one fingernail from a courier's borrowed ear. Tavi had the anomaly wafer under the reader lamp and was lining stripped screws along the lip of the table from smallest to largest, one of his worse habits when he was scared enough to look busy.

Lin sat on an upturned detergent bucket with the intake cards stacked on their knee, reading every label even after they already knew what the bottle or bin was for. The printed words gave them something solid to grip. Jo's old green cardigan swallowed their wrists. A thread had come loose at the cuff and Lin kept winding it around one finger without noticing.

The wafer was not much to look at. It was a translucent sliver the size of two postage stamps, archive-sealed, with one corner chipped when Mira had pried it loose in the records room with her thumbnail and a prayer she had not believed in. She had taped it inside the lining of her bag for two weeks and still checked every morning to make sure it was there. Tonight Tavi had fitted an adapter from a retired wash console to a clinic reader that still thought it was processing skin-tax invoices.

"If this shorts the board, that's on you," Mira said.

Tavi did not look up. "You're the one who stole corporate memory candy."

"You told me to steal proof."

"I told you to steal proof you could use without burning my only decent reader." He gave the cable a small twist and the lamp stopped flickering. "There. If it catches fire now, it'll at least be elegant."

Mira leaned in despite herself. When he moved, the bench gave back the smell of soap and solder and the clean salt of his skin under a long shift's sweat. She hated that her body still knew the difference. Touching him had not made him easier to look at. It had only stripped away the distance that used to do some of the work for her.

The screen took its time. An old CommonFrame handshake icon blinked, went gray, blinked again. Beneath it, columns of code began to crawl up the display.

Mira knew claims strings the way other people knew neighborhood shortcuts. She read intake source, damage class, solvent charge, denature certification, and renter liability without thinking. She could read most of what passed on the screen before Tavi touched anything. What she could not read was why the disposal lines kept repeating a second code field after denature weight.

WH9

RSR

YLD

Another set of serials rose under it. They were bargain models from the cheap line. Some were flagged as carry instability, some as joint failure, and some had no damage class at all. The second field sat next to all of them.

"That's not pulp," she said.

Tavi's mouth flattened. "No."

"Then what is it."

He picked up one of the screws, turned it once between finger and thumb, and set it back in line. "Yield."

Mira looked at him. "Yield of what."

"Whatever doesn't wash clean." He tapped the screen. "Look at the fractions. If this was destruction, they'd log weight and solvent use. This is retention. How much they can strip before the shell gets sent to slurry."

Lin lifted their head. "Strip what."

Tavi glanced at them and dropped the technician dodge before it fully formed. "Memory trash. Reflex carry. Sensory loops. Whole leftover pieces if they're rich enough."

"Rich," Lin repeated. The word came out sour. "Rich like money."

"Rich like concentrated." Tavi rubbed the heel of his hand over his mouth. "Though, yes, also money."

More lines filled in. Mira saw route dates and pickup windows from claims, resort return docks, and hospital leasing partners. One code marker sat in the source column over and over again: D-POOL. She knew that one without help. It meant debt restructure pool, the clause she had spent years dressing up for clients who had missed three payments and signed away scan salvage in exchange for a smaller collection call.

She heard herself ask the question in the same voice she used with screaming renters. It was flat enough to keep the room standing. "Why is a debt pool in disposal routing."

Tavi let out a breath through his nose. "Because debt contracts buy you cheap pattern stock."

"You said aggregate lattice," Mira said. "You said shortcut. You didn't say this."

"I said what I knew."

"You knew enough to leave out the part where they harvest people who can't pay."

"Mira."

She had not raised her voice. The fact that he used her name anyway almost made it worse.

Tavi pushed back from the bench, then forward again. The room had shrunk on him; anywhere he put his body would look like retreat. "I built wash tolerances," he said. "I built carry thresholds so the bargain line wouldn't collapse every third run. I knew they were stretching donor residue. I knew they reused dirty leisure stock because rich clients pay extra to feel like nothing touched them and the company figures out how to sell the touch twice. I didn't know they were sorting debt pools at the back end. Not like this. Not with this much structure."

"Structure," Mira said.

"You want the blunt version? Fine. It's a recipe." He pointed at the screen harder than he needed to. "This says somebody isn't failing to clean the bodies. This says they're grading the leftovers and feeding them forward on purpose."

Lin had gone very still. Stress did that to them sometimes. They sat braced and listening, like somebody under a bad ceiling waiting to hear where the weight would fall. Their finger kept worrying the loose thread at the cardigan cuff until the skin at the knuckle went pale.

"Nine," they said softly. "I know that number."

Mira turned. "From where."

Lin shut their eyes. "Not know. It's in me. Cold air. A fan with something hitting it every turn. Lemon powder. Somebody saying hold the blue tags because Mirabel docks for mistakes." They opened their eyes again and looked at the screen, not at either of them. "It was in teeth. One of the pieces in teeth."

Tavi swore under his breath, then louder when Mira shot him a look. "Fuck. Warehouse Nine."

He shoved aside the screw line and pulled a canal map onto the second monitor. The building sat farther down the industrial water than CommonFrame claims, past the public salvage slips and the decommissioned municipal plant with the broken gull deterrent wires. Mira had passed the block on the tram and never looked twice because the city was full of dead buildings that kept paying taxes.

"It's listed as a mothballed denature site," Tavi said. "Used to be public before CommonFrame bought the easement. They keep it off the glossy maps because nothing good happens in facilities with the word disposal on the front. On paper it only handles overflow pulp and contaminated returns."

"On paper," Mira said.

"Right."

He zoomed in on the loading side. Service roads looped toward a canal gate and a small staff entrance. Mira felt the shape of the place settle in her head like a claims diagram, rooms implied by utilities, by truck lanes, by what kind of mess had to flow where.

"Can we get in," she asked.

Tavi scratched at the burn scar on his forearm, another habit he only had when he was trying not to pace. "Outer fence, probably. Inside, maybe. The disposal crews are contractors. Bad pay, bad masks, everybody thinking they only work with meat. Security is mostly drones because nobody expects anyone to break into a building everybody thinks is a dump."

"Do you have an old badge."

"Mine is too old and too linked to me. If my face shows up on an interior camera, somebody from vats might still know it." He looked at her. "Yours might work on the first door. They haven't fired you yet. They just want you nervous."

Mira stared at the map. "Then I go tonight."

"No," Tavi said immediately. "You go before dawn. Shift change. Less traffic, more blind spots."

"Fine."

"Also no to you going alone."

She almost laughed at that. "You just explained why you can't come."

"I can't. That doesn't mean alone is smart."

Lin stood up so abruptly the detergent bucket rocked. "I'm going."

Mira already had the refusal ready. It came up and stopped against the sight of Lin's face, which had gone pale in a way their bargain skin did badly, all the color dropping without the softness of human capillaries to disguise it.

"No," she said anyway. "Absolutely not."

"You're not going into a place that made me and calling that protection."

"I'm not calling it anything. I'm keeping you out of a warehouse full of body slurry and whoever's getting paid to sort it."

"Then keep me out after you know what it is." Lin took one step closer. Their voice did not rise, which made Mira listen harder. "Do not make me wait at home while you go count people I might be made from."

The deliberate full form hit the room like a snapped ruler. It was chosen. Lin did that when they wanted every word to stand upright on its own legs.

Tavi looked away. Mira wished he would not witness this. She wished, with the dull uselessness of wishing for things already spent, that none of them had become people who needed to have arguments like this in a laundromat after midnight.

"You might not be made from anything in there," she said.

"Then I will come back and be wrong in person."

Mira bit the inside of her cheek until the copper started. That was the trouble with Lin. They could sound so careful and still put the knife exactly where she had least room to move around it.

Tavi reached under the bench and pulled out two folded contractor ponchos, industrial orange gone dull with old use. "If you're both ignoring me, at least ignore me competently."

Mira took one. "You don't get to phrase it like permission."

"I don't think this is a permission situation." He handed the second to Lin, then dug in a drawer for two hair caps and a packet of cheap respirators. "Dock side gets a smoke break around four-fifteen. Wait till the inbound truck unlatches, then move with the blind corner on the left. If you hear the crane bell twice, the wash sluice is opening. Stay away from the floor drains when that happens because they'll pull a shoe clean off your foot."

"That's a weirdly specific thing to know," Lin said.

"I knew somebody who lost a shoe."

"Only a shoe," Mira said.

"Mostly a shoe."

That got half a breath of a laugh out of Lin. Tavi looked almost grateful for it. Then he turned the wafer screen back to Mira and froze the routing table.

"Take a photo of this in your head if not with your eyes," he said. "WH9. RSR. YLD. D-pool. If you find a terminal, look for seed lots, not damage archive. Damage archive will show you symptoms. Seed will show intent."

Mira had no interest in thanking him yet. What she said instead was, "If we don't come back by first light, burn the bench log and wake Jo."

Tavi's expression tightened, but he nodded. "You say that like I need coaching."

"You need reminding."

He accepted that too.

By the time Mira tucked the respirator into her pocket and slid the wafer back inside her bag lining, Warehouse Nine had stopped being theory. It had a route code, a shift change, and a place on the canal where CommonFrame said nothing lived except waste.

* * *

The walk down-canal took them through the part of Cordera that always smelled as if the city had turned its pockets out and left the worst things at the curb. It smelled of wet rope, hot metal, and kelp gone black in the grates. A vent from the rendering works coughed steam across the pavement and left Mira's ponytail damp under the hair cap. Lin stayed close without crowding her, their borrowed boots making a slightly different sound on the concrete, a hollower tap from soles not yet worn into them.

Warehouse Nine rose at the edge of the water in blocky dark sections, municipal bones under a CommonFrame skin. The original lettering had been sanded off the brick, but the ghost of SANITARY RECLAIM still sat under the newer metal plate that said simply W9 ANNEX. Decommissioned buildings were not supposed to have lit dock clocks. This one had three.

They waited behind a stack of cracked polymer bins while a truck backed into the loading bay. The vehicle carried no company logo, only a contractor plate and a mud-streaked sticker that said CLEAN HANDS, CLEAN CITY. When the rear doors opened, cold denature vapor rolled out and then the sweeter underneath smell followed it, protein gone warm again in the middle where the coolant had failed.

A woman at the dock scanner took the driver's sheet without looking up from the little phone propped beside her monitor. A child on the screen, missing front teeth, was trying to tape silver paper to a cardboard moon. The woman had lilac eyebrows under her cap and a strip of strawberry-print tape wrapped around the knuckle she kept cracking between scans.

"You left this one sweating in queue again," she said to the driver. "If Mirabel sees surface thaw, she bills a second rinse and I'm not eating that."

"I made up the time on the bridge," the driver said.

"Your bridge problems are your bridge problems."

She stamped the manifest, never taking her eyes off the child gluing craters onto the moon. The dock door cycled open wider. Mira touched Lin's wrist once and moved.

Inside, the air changed weight. It was thicker, humid under the cold. Every vent in the building seemed to share one wet lung. The respirator helped with the bleach and did nothing for the rest. Mira could taste old fat, saline, ammonia, and something sweet enough to feel indecent on the tongue. The floor under her boots had a permanent tack to it. Overhead fans pushed the stink around without lessening it.

The main sort hall wore the cheap face expensive crimes used when they wanted to pass as routine: temporary partitions, a frosted-panel office, a whiteboard for shift initials and lunch breaks, cases of nitrile gloves stacked next to a vending machine that had sold out of everything except salted peanuts and mint gum. Horror here came with a time clock and a microwave.

Lines moved through the center of the hall in parallel. On the nearest conveyor lay stripped bargain shells on denature cradles, some whole, some not, all with their ports uncapped and tagged. Hair had been netted down or shaved. Mouths were wedged open with rinse spacers. A foot twitched when the belt jolted, not from life but from bad balancing at the hip. Somebody had written RETURN SPOILAGE on one tag in thick marker and drawn a smiley face beside it.

Lin stopped for half a second. Mira felt the stop before she saw it.

"Keep moving," she murmured.

"I'm moving."

They moved along the shadow of stacked pallets toward the catwalk stairs. Mira read the signs as they passed and felt the shape of the building alter inside her head.

She had expected the categories claims used: joint failure, surface contamination, unauthorized modification, trauma. They were all the ways a company could pretend a body's trouble started after purchase. Instead the placards over each lane read:

G0 CLEAN

G1 AMBIENT

G2 CONTRACT

G3 LEISURE

G4 HOLD

Under each code sat threshold numbers for retention index, sensory density, and narrative persistence. That last phrase stopped Mira so abruptly Lin nearly walked into her.

On the wall beside the lane board hung a laminated pay notice:

RECLASSIFICATION MUST MATCH YIELD BAND OR PAYROLL WILL REJECT BONUS.

Below that, in smaller type:

CONTRACT MATERIAL INCLUDES D-POOL AND RECOVERY-CONSENT SOURCES.

There was no mention of defect or safety. The board cared only about how much somebody was still in the shell when it came off the line.

At the G2 lane, a worker with one sleeve rolled above an eczema patch ran a handheld over a headless torso and clicked his tongue. "You tagged this ambient."

The woman with the strawberry tape came over without putting down her scanner. Up close her apron was dotted with tiny sequined cherries, the kind sold cheap in market stalls for school performances. "Because the first pass said ambient."

"Second pass says contract and leisure carry."

"Then bump it." She glanced at the open torso port, bored as a cashier. "D-pool on the base, premium skin residue on top. That pays blue, not gray."

"Mirabel's going to say it's mixed."

"Mirabel can say whatever she wants after she does my station for an hour."

She slapped a new tag onto the shell and sent it down the blue-marked lane.

Mira felt something inside her go cold enough to harden. It was D-pool on the base and premium skin residue on top. It was not even hidden language if you knew where the bodies and the debt contracts touched. CommonFrame took scan salvage from people too poor to fight the fine print, took unwiped pleasure residue from clients rich enough to assume their weekends evaporated on command, and paid warehouse workers a bonus to sort the good leftovers from the plain ones.

Lin's hand closed on her sleeve, hard and deliberate, warning her before their voice had to.

Mira followed their gaze.

At the far lane, under G4 HOLD, stood wire rolling racks filled with shells wrapped in clear polymer for further processing. Somebody had shelved them for later, logged them, kept them whole. Some had ports sealed with yellow caps. Some had whole faces still attached, damp hair plastered to temples, eyes taped shut. Three carried the same pale rib scar, curving under the left side in a cheap neat line Mira knew too well from the nights Lin slept on their back. One of the wrapped shells had the same ear notch, or near enough that Mira's own skin prickled under her coat.

Lin did not speak. Their mouth had gone slightly open under the respirator, which made the mask pulse with each breath.

Mira stepped between them and the rack until she blocked the view. "Don't freeze here."

Lin swallowed. "They kept the mistakes."

"Because somebody can sell a mistake twice if they log it right."

It came out crueler than she intended. Lin nodded anyway, eyes still on her, not looking past, and that was good. Mira could work with that. She could not work with Lin unraveling in the middle of a warehouse that categorized personhood by bonus band.

They reached the catwalk stairs. A bell rang once somewhere overhead, then the squeal of an old pulley. Workers did not hurry. One bent to initial a clipboard. Another pulled off a glove to scratch the side of his nose and then put the same wet glove back on. Every ordinary movement in the room made the place worse. If the workers had looked monstrous, Mira could have filed them under danger and moved on. Instead they looked like everybody she saw at tram stops before dawn: tired, underpaid, protective of their breaks, and willing to grade whatever came down the line because the rent did not care where the bonus came from.

At the top of the stairs sat the office in frosted panels. A paper sign taped to the door read:

DO NOT LEAVE COFFEE IN SINK

Below it someone had added in marker:

THIS MEANS YOU, RAY

Mira tried her badge. For a second nothing happened. Then the lock clicked with the small humiliated sound of a system obeying someone it meant to punish later.

Inside, the office was warmer and somehow fouler, old coffee left to turn on a hot plate, printer toner, damp paper, the bodily stink caught in the vent filter and recirculated until it became part of the furniture. A plastic fern leaned gray with dust in one corner. An employee raffle flyer for half-price flu shots hung beside a compliance poster reminding staff to log all narrative-active incidents before meal break.

Lin shut the door softly behind them and stared through the frosted panel at the racks below. Mira went to the nearest terminal.

The screen woke on a warehouse dashboard tracking throughput, weight, yield bands, and seed hold counts. She did not know this interface, but she knew CommonFrame's logic. Every department renamed the same cruelty and called it workflow.

She plugged in the wafer adapter. The port fought her once, then took.

Menus opened to internal routing, hold lots, and blend tables. She clicked past two pages of clearance prompts and hit the first file that used the word seed instead of residue. The page loaded in neat gray columns.

LM BARGAIN AGGREGATE SERIES

BASE SOURCE: D-POOL RECOVERY CONSENT

ENRICHMENT: LEISURE CARRY, WASH WAIVER AUTHORIZED

TARGET RETENTION: .23-.31

RECOMPOSITION ROUTE: W9 / HOLD / RECIRCULATE

Mira's hand tightened on the mouse until the cheap plastic creaked. The file read like manufacturing instructions, complete with authorized waivers, target retention bands, and a recirculation route.

She opened the next lot.

The next lot listed the same ugly chain in neat gray columns: hospital rental spillover, debt-pool recovery, resort return, wash waiver authorized, then recirculate at the bottom as if the whole business were a housekeeping note.

The next lots changed sources but not logic. Hospital rental spillover fed one batch, resort return another, debt pools most of the rest. The inputs shifted; the route stayed the same. Whatever Mira had hoped to find on the floor below, the terminal made it plain that Warehouse Nine was working to spec.

Below the office window, a pallet jack beeped three times. Lin flinched at the sound and looked down again.

"Mira," they said, very quiet.

She turned.

On the floor beneath the G4 racks, workers were rolling out another cage. This one held heads in denature cradles, faces upward, ports cleaned for reuse. The features were not identical, but they belonged to the same cheap family, the same jaw economy, the same bargain symmetry. A few wore traces of the lives that had used them. Glitter caught in one eyebrow. A bruise shadowed along another throat. Somebody had left tiny silver stickers on a pair of nails that no rinse had fully taken off.

Lin stepped to the glass as if pulled by the sternum.

Mira saw the danger then, not as an alarm or a guard but as recognition, the way it could stop a person more completely than fear. To Lin, those wrapped shells were not just evidence. They were almost-relatives, near-selves, bodies that might have lent them a scar, a gait, a smell lodged at the back of the teeth. Warehouse Nine did not need chains if it could make a person wonder whether the room below was their family archive.

"Stay with me," Mira said.

Lin did not answer. Their hand had come up to their rib, fingers pressed over the scar through Jo's cardigan.

Nothing on the screen called it defect. Everything called it yield.

* * *

Mira jammed the wafer deeper into the port and hit export.

The terminal thought about it. An authorization bar crawled across the screen and then stalled at twenty-two percent.

ACCESS LIMITED

REQUEST SUPERVISOR KEY

"Of course," she muttered.

She did not have a supervisor key. What she had was a claims badge, an archive theft, and enough understanding of bad systems to know that the people who built them hated extra steps. She backed out, opened the print queue instead of the export menu, and selected summary tables, routing manifests, and whatever blend sheets the terminal would let her touch before it noticed her hand did not belong there.

For one beautiful second it worked.

Then the office printer behind her screamed awake.

Dot-matrix hammers began punching paper with a noise from a cheaper century. Mira spun around just in time to see the first sheet jerk into view, green-and-white striped margins, the heading SEED ROUTE SUMMARY rattling across the top.

"Shit."

Below the window, heads lifted. Workers looked up. The strawberry-tape woman pulled one side of her respirator loose.

"Who's printing up there?" somebody shouted.

The second page started. The terminal flashed amber.

Lin jerked back from the glass, breathing hard, still half inside whatever memory had grabbed them. Mira tore the first sheet free, grabbed the next two as they spat out, and yanked the wafer from the port so hard she bent the adapter pins.

A bell rang twice overhead.

The double bell meant the wash sluice.

Tavi's warning hit her half a second before the floor machinery answered. Somewhere below, valves opened with a deep mechanical cough. Water rushed through pipes inside the walls. The whole building seemed to inhale.

"Move," Mira said.

Lin was looking down again.

The double bell had done something to them. On the floor below, the G4 rack had been rolled closer to the rinse trench, wrapped bodies stacked shoulder to shoulder under clear polymer, faces fogged by condensation. One of the shells had its eyes open because the tape had slipped. Cheap irises stared at nothing, but from where Lin stood the line of bodies looked less like inventory than a queue waiting to be called by the right name.

"Lin."

"I know that bell."

"You can know it outside."

"That scar is mine."

"Maybe. Probably. It doesn't matter right now."

Lin turned, and the hurt on their face was quick and naked enough to cut her for saying it. Mira grabbed their hand anyway.

Below them the office door handle jerked once from the outside and missed the latch. Somebody swore. The terminal went from amber to red.

Mira pulled Lin into the hall. The catwalk was narrow, wet underfoot from condensation blown sideways by the vents. At the far end, a security drone came off its wall cradle with an insect whine and swung its camera toward them.

The drone had the bland body municipalities loved: a white flat shell the size of a serving tray, a red light at the center, plastic wiped clean. It looked like procedure made visible, force with the corners sanded off.

"Unauthorized access detected," it said in a voice almost polite. "Remain in place for site review."

"No," Mira said, and they ran.

The drone followed, not fast enough to catch them in open ground but fast enough to keep the alarm live. Red strobes began to pulse over the hall. Below, workers moved with more irritation than panic, dragging lanes to a stop, cursing whoever had made paperwork for them at this hour.

Mira hit the stair rail too hard and her palm slid on old disinfectant. She kept her grip. Lin stumbled behind her, shoulder thudding once against the wall. At the bottom of the stairs the wash sluice opened into the trench with a roaring gush of pink-gray water and solvent foam. The stink rose fresh and hot. Something soft struck Mira's boot, then rolled away in the current. She did not look down long enough to identify whether it had been part of a hand or a badly folded wipe.

"Left," she said. "Stay left."

They cut behind the halted G2 lane. Workers were shouting now.

"You from compliance?"

"Hey."

"Hey, you can't be in here."

The strawberry-tape woman had come around her station with the scanner still in one hand. Up close Mira saw the tired crease of concealer under her eyes and the moon-glue smudge on her wrist from the school project video she'd been watching at the dock. She looked human enough to have a child making paper craters before dawn and human enough to slap a blue tag on a body because it paid better.

"That print's not for floor staff," the woman snapped. "Give it here."

Mira kept moving. "Then tell your boss to stop leaving the queue open."

It was a stupid line, but it bought half a second because the woman looked offended before she looked alarmed.

Lin stopped dead again at the edge of the G4 rack.

The wrapped shells were closer here. Mira could see the cheap seams at the shoulders, the color mismatch where reused skin had been blended and printed over, the repeated rib scar shining under condensation film. One shell had a market wristband still stuck under the plastic. Another carried the faint purple shadow of somebody's church stamp on the heel. Lives clung at the edges even after the building had washed, sorted, wrapped, and reclassified them. Nothing in the warehouse had finished the job it pretended to do.

Lin lifted a hand toward the nearest face, fingertips stopping at the polymer.

"They look like they're waiting," they said.

Mira got both hands on Lin's shoulders and turned them bodily toward the exit lane. The orange contractor poncho crackled under her grip. "They're not waiting. They're tagged. That's different, and if you stand here they'll tag you too."

Lin stared at her for one stunned second, then nodded. The nod was tiny and furious and enough.

The drone dipped lower. "Remain in place for site review."

"Fuck your review," Lin said, and for one wild instant looked pleased with themself for choosing the word.

Mira almost laughed. The sound came out as breath.

They ran between stacked bins toward the loading dock. A worker grabbed for Mira's sleeve and caught only the edge of the poncho, tearing it straight down the side. Cold air hit her arm. Another alarm joined the first, not a siren exactly but a pulsing tone designed to annoy people into obedience. The dock door was half closed. Mira ducked under it and felt the metal skim her back. Lin dropped to hands and knees and came through after her, palms slapping wet concrete.

Outside, canal air hit like a slap of rotten pennies and rain. It was better than inside by miles.

They did not stop at the first stack of bins or the second. Mira dragged them along the seawall edge until the warehouse lights were partly blocked by rusted freight containers and the alarm had flattened into distance. Only then did she bend over with her hands on her thighs and pull the respirator down.

The first breath without it brought the warehouse back anyway: bleach, fat, hot ink. She spat into the canal and watched the spit string itself thin in the wind.

Lin braced both hands on the wall and retched dryly twice. Nothing came up. They had not eaten since midnight. After a moment they pressed the heel of one hand against their rib and looked at Mira with eyes made enormous by shock and anger and the effort of staying assembled.

"I'm sorry," Mira said.

Lin blinked. "For what part."

"The part where I said it didn't matter."

They nodded once. "It mattered."

"I know."

She took the crumpled print sheets from inside her jacket. The paper was damp with sweat and mist, green stripes blurring at the edges, but the text had held. Three pages. A fourth had torn in the printer when she ripped the stack free. A tribunal would call them anecdote. Mira looked down and saw a production method.

She crouched under the container's shadow and flattened the pages on her knee.

SEED ROUTE SUMMARY - W9 HOLD

LM SERIES / BARGAIN AGGREGATE

BASE SOURCE: D-POOL RECOVERY CONSENT / NQ CLUSTER / PORT AUTH 44

ENRICHMENT INPUT: LEISURE CARRY, PREMIUM WEEKEND / WASH WAIVER 3A

SECONDARY INPUT: AMBIENT LABOR REFLEX / HOSPITAL OVERFLOW

TARGET RETENTION BAND: .23-.31

RECIRCULATION STATUS: APPROVED

Below that sat partial lot numbers, shipment dates, and blend percentages. There was no executive signature and no top-level map of how many lines or how many years. The pages proved a method at one warehouse and one product series. They did not yet name the architect.

Mira turned to the next page. It showed more of the same, only with different source clusters and different premium routes. One line flagged narrative-active hold with an internal note:

DO NOT DENATURE UNTIL REBLEND DECISION.

That note was not an accident report or a sanitation lapse. It was a decision point written in plain language.

Rain stippled the paper. Mira shielded it with her body.

"They weren't sorting for damage," Lin said. Their voice had steadied into that careful register they used when the feeling underneath was too large to set loose all at once. "They were sorting for what stayed human enough to sell."

Mira looked back at the warehouse. One high window glowed over the canal, yellow through wire glass, as ordinary as a night office anywhere in the city. Somewhere inside, somebody was reprinting the pages she had taken and cursing the extra paperwork.

She had wanted, some stubborn stupid part of her, to find negligence because negligence had a shape she knew how to argue with. She could find the failed step, name the liable party, and push until somebody paid or a protocol got rewritten. This was a production method instead. It meant budgets, approvals, and enough people in agreement to make cruelty routine.

Lin crouched beside her and touched the corner of the first page with one fingertip, careful not to wrinkle the damp paper any further. "I thought maybe I was a leak," they said. "Something that happened because they were lazy."

"No."

It was all Mira trusted herself to say at first.

Then, because Lin deserved more than a clean denial, she forced the rest of it out. "You were built. Deliberately. So were the others. Whatever pieces of people they used, they used them on purpose."

Lin looked sick again, but not surprised. Surprise had been back in the office, when the racks came into view and recognition hit like a door opening in the wrong part of the mind. This was worse and steadier.

"Do we have enough?" they asked.

Mira folded the pages along the damp creases and slid them back inside her coat. The paper was already softening with weather. She would need plastic sleeves, copies, somewhere dry, somewhere safer than Brine Court and safer than the clinic. The wafer in her bag held more codes than before, but not the whole archive. The printout proved the recipe at W9 and the LM bargain series. It still did not show the sign-off chain, the full site list, or every hold lot waiting downstairs.

"We have enough to stop calling it contamination," she said. "We don't have enough yet to name who approved it, how far it runs, or who all got fed into it."

Lin watched her tuck the papers away. The canal wind pushed wet hair across their forehead. They did not brush it back.

"Then we go again?" they asked.

Mira thought of the office printer hammering alive, the repeated scar under plastic, the bonus notice on the wall, the child on the dock worker's phone gluing silver paper to a cardboard moon while bodies came off the truck behind her. She thought of Tavi saying seed, not damage. She thought of every renter she had ever told that an anomaly surcharge was unfortunate but standard.

"Not there like this," she said.

That answer did not satisfy either of them. It was the truest one she had.

When they finally started back toward the city, Mira kept one hand over the papers inside her coat against the wind. They were only fragments, three wet pages torn from a printer in a room that stank of coffee and rot, but they changed the work waiting for her at Blue Hour, and the warehouse bleach still rode her clothes when she finally caught a little sleep after dawn. Every overlap case on her bench was a lead now, maybe a donor trail, maybe a hold lot, maybe a person CommonFrame expected to disappear into yield bands and billing codes.

The map was still incomplete, and when she finally shut her eyes the corner she held was full of people.


# Chapter 10

Gabe was already at the stove when Mira came out to the kitchen, working yesterday's rice in a pan one-handed while the apartment held the wet-concrete smell of the hall. Warehouse bleach sat under it anyway, stubborn as a stain.

She had slept a little after dawn, or at least she had shut her eyes long enough to wake with the same pictures waiting behind them: hooks, drain channels, marker codes on salvage bins, a clerk at Warehouse Nine sorting whole bodies by how much other life they still carried. When the tram shrieked below Brine Court, the sound ran through the apartment's thin floorboards into Mira's teeth.

Yesterday's rice hissed in oil with an egg broken through it and the last of a scallion chopped fine. Gabe cooked with his hips held crooked, keeping pressure off the bad part of his back without ever making a performance of it. The white edge of his lumbar brace showed where his T-shirt had ridden up. On the table beside him lay a free tide sheet from the dock office, folded into quarters, softened at the creases from being handled too much. He still circled the high-water times in pencil even in weeks when nobody called him for legal work.

He glanced over when she came in. "You look like a government hallway."

Mira took two plates from the dish rack. "Good morning to you too."

"No, I mean it with love. Damp, fluorescent, something terrible happened there."

"You should be lying down."

"You should stop using my medical file as conversation." He scooped rice onto the plates and nodded toward the kettle. "Tea's hot. Lin went out with Jo an hour ago. They took the good umbrella and left us the one that leaks right over the handle, which I think counts as personality development."

There was a mug upside down by the sink, drying with careful precision. Lin had lined it exactly with the crack in the draining board, as if neatness could pay rent. Mira poured tea and sat.

Gabe dropped into the chair across from her with the small, involuntary breath he always tried to swallow before it became a sound. Then he grinned, because she had noticed. "See? Graceful. Gazelle stuff."

"You're thirty-one."

"And still young enough to be insulted by that tone."

The rice was too salty and exactly what she needed. He had thrown in one packet of soy too many and compensated with lemon from the heel of a fruit someone downstairs had handed him the day before. Gabe collected those little neighborhood economies the way other people collected favors, never enough to feel like he owed anyone, just enough to keep the week patched.

She ate faster than she meant to. He watched, not unkindly.

"Bad yesterday?" he asked.

Mira kept her eyes on the plate. "Work."

"That word is doing a lot."

"It usually does."

He tore open a pepper sauce packet with his teeth and lined the empty foil beside his plate in a neat silver row. He always did that. Even as a kid he had stacked wrappers, ticket stubs, bottle caps, anything that looked like it had a count built into it. "All right," he said. "Then here's my medical recommendation. Seawall. Wind's right. Food carts'll be open after the rain. You can sit there and pretend not to tell me things."

She looked up. "Since when do you prescribe fresh air?"

"Since my patient looks haunted and annoying." He shoveled in a bite and shrugged. "Also I need to walk before tomorrow. If I lock up in this chair all day, I'll move like a landlord."

That was closer to truth. The weather had turned in the night, a first early-autumn front rolling hard off the water, and changes in pressure worked their way into Gabe's spine like gossip into a building. Mira had heard him up twice in the dark, once in the bathroom, once just standing at the kitchen window waiting for the pain pills to become more than theory.

She should have gone to Blue Hour. She should have been with Tavi and Jo, should have been building an evidence chain from the partial manifest on the wafer before courage cooled into caution. Instead she thought of the bins at Warehouse Nine, of residual richness written in black marker on a body with one hand half pulped away, and the thought of another room full of damaged flesh made something in her close.

"Fine," she said.

Gabe pointed his fork at her. "You didn't have to sound so moved."

"I save my warmth for clients."

"Then I'm billing you."

They left an hour later with the leaking umbrella, though the rain had mostly finished. Cordera's air came salted and oily off the harbor, carrying fryer grease from the market, coolant from the tram line, a sweet rot from the canal that nobody admitted noticing anymore. The street below Brine Court shone with old rain. Delivery scooters hissed through puddles. A gull worked at a burst trash bag with the concentration of a surgeon.

Gabe wore his old navy dock jacket even though one zipper tooth was gone and the left cuff had gone shiny with age. He had patched the inside pocket with yellow sail tape. He kept his tide sheet folded in there like an official document. As they walked downhill he dragged his left foot only a little, disguising the hitch by setting his pace to the slope, but Mira knew the arithmetic. Each block cost him something. The city charged interest on every staircase.

He talked through the market in his public voice, the easy one that made strangers hand back an extra lime or save him the last cheap coffee without feeling hustled. He asked a fishmonger whether the storm had scared the mackerel into honesty. He told a flower seller her carnations looked like they had survived a knife fight. She laughed so hard she had to take the cigarette out of her mouth.

At the bottom of Canal Street the seawall opened up all at once: concrete steps slick with spray, gulls shouldering one another off the rail, ferry horns carrying low over the water. The sky had not settled on a single job. To the east it was a clean pale blue. Over the harbor the clouds still hung in bruised folds, dark underneath, bright at the edges where the sun worried at them.

The food carts had come back out fast. In Cordera weather, people started selling hot things the minute the rain dropped from impossible to rude.

Gabe steered them toward a fry cart with a striped awning and a handwritten sign for smelts, cassava cakes, and pepper broth. The woman running it wore a green visor even under cloud cover and had one thumbnail grown long and lacquered copper, useful for peeling foil lids and slapping rude hands away from the counter. She saw Gabe and rolled her eyes in recognition before he had even reached her.

"If you ask me for extra vinegar again," she said, "I'm charging by the splash."

"Sita, wound me if you want, but don't ration me."

"Hear him." She tipped her head toward Mira. "He says that every week like I'm the cruel state."

"You wish you had their consistency."

Sita snorted, set two paper trays down, and, after pretending not to, gave Gabe the little cup of pickled peppers she normally sold separate. He slipped the coins over with a care that told Mira he had counted them before leaving home.

They took their food to the lower steps where the concrete still held last night's rain in shallow dents. Gabe lowered himself carefully, one hand on the rail, jaw gone flat for three seconds with the effort. Then he sat, exhaled through his nose, and immediately ruined the dignity of the moment by shaking half the peppers over his fish.

"You going to eat that," he asked, looking at her untouched broth, "or are you just taking it to court?"

Mira lifted the paper cup. It was hotter than she expected. Pepper and fish stock and cilantro steam climbed into her face. Her body answered before the rest of her had time to stay suspicious.

For a while they let the seawall do the talking. Waves slapped the lower pilings. Gulls screamed over a dropped strip of batter. Behind them a pair of children in school sweats tried to kick a flattened bottle cap into the storm drain while their grandmother shouted corrections in Spanish from under a clear plastic hood. Out on the freight lanes, cranes along North Quay stood like thin black handwriting against the brighter patch of sky.

Gabe ate with quick efficiency and then slowed, settling into the weather. This was one of the few places where he stopped trying to look useful. He watched the water the way some people watched television, fully given over to it, reading mood and timing out of a surface that never kept one shape long.

"High water's late," he said after a while, tapping the folded tide sheet through his jacket pocket. "Storm nudged it."

"You still read those on your days off?"

"Tide notices, union boards, gig boards, menus, eviction threats. I'm a broad-minded man."

She smiled despite herself.

He caught it. "There she is."

"Don't get excited."

"Too late. I saw evidence."

The word snagged on her and turned into Warehouse Nine again, into marker on bins, half a serial visible through pulp, and Lin going very still beside her in that wet cold place because the body on the cart had shared the shape of one of Lin's remembered scars. Mira set the broth down before she spilled it.

Gabe looked out at the water when he asked the next thing, which was how she knew he meant it. "That bad?"

Mira could lie to clerks, supervisors, investigators. Gabe had a way of making lies feel not immoral exactly, just cheap. "Worse than I thought," she said.

He took that in with no theatrical change of face. "Worse how?"

She watched a harbor tug push against the side of a container barge, all blunt force and correction. "Not a wash problem. Not sloppiness. They're doing it on purpose."

Gabe's chewing stopped. "The overlaps."

She nodded.

"On purpose."

"Yeah."

Wind came hard across the water. It flattened his jacket against his chest and lifted a strand of her hair loose from its tie. Above them the ferry gave one deep horn blast that seemed to move the air in her ribs.

Gabe rubbed the heel of his hand over his mouth. "Jesus."

Mira had expected outrage, or disgust, or at least the clean relief of someone outside the thing saying yes, that's monstrous, now go break it. What she got instead was Gabe staring toward North Quay with the squint he used on loading rates and weather, already sorting the practical consequences.

His phone buzzed. He pulled it out, glanced at the screen, and let it buzz itself dead.

"Who was it?" she asked.

"North Quay Recovery. Or one of its nephews."

"About the old frame charge?"

"What else do men like that talk about? They don't call to ask after my spirit."

The debt had been hanging over him so long it had become a piece of furniture in the apartment, ugly and easy to bruise yourself on. Seven thousand eight hundred dollars because a heavy-lift rental had come back damaged after a salvage shift, a number large enough to be abstract until the moments it wasn't: the legal jobs he turned down, the collection threats, the way his shoulders changed when an unknown number lit the screen.

He slid the phone away and reached for more fish. His hand shook once before he got it steady.

Mira heard herself say it before she had decided whether she meant to. "I could put this somewhere. What I found. Enough people see it, enough of the right people, maybe the whole fucking line burns."

Gabe let out one quiet breath that was almost a laugh and had no humor in it. "There it is."

"What?"

"The face. I knew it was that face."

"I'm serious."

"I know." He looked at her then, not teasing, not soft. "Mir. If CommonFrame burns this week, what do you think happens Friday morning?"

"To them?"

"No. To me."

She opened her mouth and shut it again.

He rested his forearms on his knees. The wind pressed his hair flat, showing the little scar inside his right temple where he'd once gone into a bollard as a teenager and claimed it made him look strategic. "In this body?" he said. "I can stand maybe forty minutes if I lie about the pain, maybe an hour if the floor's kind. I can carry groceries up the stairs one bag at a time. I can wash dishes leaning on my left hip and then hate myself for the next two hours. The dock doesn't pay for any of that."

Mira said nothing.

"The dock app pays for lift class, cold tolerance, fast recovery, shoulder range. It pays for knees that don't blow out on wet metal. It pays for a spine that isn't mine." He kept his voice level, practical, almost bored, which made each word land harder. "If you blow up the cheap end of this market overnight, North Quay doesn't pause to admire the ethics. It fills those berths with people who can afford cleaner frames and leaves the rest of us onshore. If I sign on in my own body, I get maybe scanner work, gate checks, some miserable seat in a booth if somebody's cousin dropped dead. That's if Recovery doesn't garnish it before I touch it. If I go in through a gray broker with a heavy-lift frame already booked, I get cash. Three hundred on a bad shift. More if the load's ugly and the weather's worse. That's rent, groceries, pills, the electric staying on, the landlord staying theoretical for one more week."

Mira stared at the cranes across the water until they blurred and sharpened again. Warehouse Nine had given her something clean in one direction, at least: a factory full of proof, an evil with bins and paperwork. She had walked out of it wanting a single line from crime to exposure to consequence. She had not, until that moment, placed her brother's body on the other side of the line.

"I'm not saying leave them alone," Gabe said.

"It sounds a little like that."

"No." He shook his head. "I'm saying don't talk like the city's gonna pause and respectfully invent a better option because you showed everybody the blood under the floor."

His voice roughened on the last few words. Pain did that to him, scraped the polish off and left the grain.

Mira looked at him properly. The brace was digging into his ribs even sitting down. At the neck of his T-shirt there was a patch of adhesive shadow behind one ear where the transfer contacts had sat on his last shift, the skin still dull and pink. He had come to the seawall in the body he was born in, the one that hurt, the one the city priced low, and she had almost used him as a pure witness against the system that kept him fed.

"How often?" she asked.

"What."

"How often are you renting now?"

He gave her a sideways look. "That's the first question?"

"It's the useful one."

There was the smallest flicker of approval. Mira could still speak sibling, even from inside a bad realization.

"Two, three times a week if the board's decent. Four if rent's got teeth." He picked a pepper from the paper tray and held it by the stem without eating it. "And before you do the numbers, no, it doesn't pay clean. Broker takes a cut. Pod time takes a cut. If I bring the frame back with any scrape they can photograph under bright light, somebody takes another cut. Then Recovery circles whatever wage work still touched my name. That's why I stay gray when I can."

"And when you can't?"

"Then I smile nice and fill out forms and watch money I never held get taken by people who say it like weather."

He popped the pepper into his mouth and chewed, eyes watering immediately. "Shit."

Mira almost laughed. It hurt in her throat. "You did that to yourself."

"I contain multitudes and poor judgment."

The joke hung between them, thin but real. Then he put the empty paper tray aside and said, in a voice cleaner than the rest of the conversation had been, "I don't need CommonFrame innocent. I need somewhere to clock in on Thursday."

That sat in her harder than if he had shouted.

Behind them someone dropped a glass bottle. It broke in a bright ugly burst against the upper promenade, and half the gulls took flight at once. Their wings flashed white against the harbor and turned gray as soon as they crossed into cloud.

Mira rubbed her thumb against the seam of the broth cup until the paper softened. "I hate that this is the choice."

"It isn't a choice yet." Gabe leaned back on his hands and grimaced at the pull in his spine. "That's sort of the problem, isn't it. You thought you had one job. Turns out you've got two."

"Expose them and keep people alive."

"There you go. Very elegant. You should put it on a brochure."

"Fuck off."

"See, that was warmer."

They stayed until the broth went lukewarm and the wind worked through her jacket. On the way back up the steps Gabe paused once, one hand on the rail, not speaking until the pain wave passed. Mira waited beside him without looking directly at him. That was one of the old agreements between them, older than either of their adult disasters: witness without making a stage.

At the top, he said, too casually, "I need to stop at Harbor Shift before we head home."

She looked over.

"Need to lock tomorrow's frame before surge hits. Storm week always gets stupid."

"You're working tomorrow?"

"That was the 'walk before tomorrow' part of the invitation, yes."

"You left that out."

"You were busy being haunted."

He started toward the tram underpass, and after a beat she followed.

***

Harbor Shift occupied the ground floor of an old ticket office under the elevated tracks, three blocks in from the seawall. The sign out front still showed the ghost of whatever ferry service had used the building before biotech found better margins in flesh. Now the windows were pasted over with labor-body ads: heavy-lift, cold-room, ladder-flex, night-face hospitality, all smiling models with impossible shoulders and teeth too even to trust. In the corner of every poster sat the small square of the CommonFrame mark, discreet as guilt.

Inside, the air smelled of disinfectant, wet coats, and burned coffee from a machine older than either of them. A line had formed between chrome stanchions bolted to the floor, a little strip of order laid over the room's degradation. Everyone in it stood in the bodies they had been born in or lived long enough to become ordinary in: damaged knees, bad skin, tired mouths, wrists wrapped for repetitive strain, one man with oxygen tubing tucked under his collar, a woman in hotel housekeeping shoes rubbing the base of her thumb like she was trying to grind pain back out through the joint. Near the wall a teenager in school trousers held a courier helmet against his chest and kept mouthing arithmetic to himself, pricing the shift before he'd even been assigned one.

Mira had seen every kind of body returned to claims. She had handled rent damage after bachelor weekends, sewer jobs, offshore sex packages, stunt tourism, vanity bruising, cheap assaults. She knew in theory that the bargain lines serviced labor as much as pleasure. Theory had never looked this much like a Tuesday queue at the pharmacy.

Gabe's pace slowed. It was not shame exactly. It was preparation. He put on the expression she had watched him use with landlords, triage nurses, cops at transit checks, any counter where being easy to like might lower the fee by one small humiliating amount.

"You don't have to wait," he said.

"I'm here."

"Could be boring."

"I survived claims."

"Fair."

At the counter a clerk worked through reservations with the focus of somebody trying very hard not to take the room home. She had a sunflower pin crocheted onto her cardigan and a strip of silver tape wrapped around the bridge of her glasses. Every few minutes she turned her wedding ring backward, stone to palm, before calling the next person up. When the man with the oxygen tubing left, walking slower than when he'd come in, Gabe stepped forward.

"Afternoon, Nessa."

She glanced up, recognized him, and gave him the tired half smile of a woman who remembered everybody's worst paperwork. "Afternoon, Gabe. You here to confirm North Quay salvage?"

"If nobody richer stole it."

"Not yet."

Her fingers moved over the terminal. The screen light turned her taped glasses pale blue. Mira watched the clerk's face perform small arithmetic changes: routine, annoyance, sympathy, the professional blank that replaced both.

"Deposit bumped," Nessa said.

Gabe's charm held for one second and then tightened at the corners. "By how much?"

"One twenty."

"It was sixty yesterday."

"Yesterday the compliance patch hadn't finished syncing." She winced as soon as she heard herself, ashamed of the language and still unable to avoid it. "I'm sorry. North Quay flagged the old damage claim against your work profile. Any heavy-lift reservation cross-linked to that account pulls higher risk now."

"Higher risk for who?"

Nessa gave him the look of someone who had no power and enough self-respect not to pretend otherwise. "You know the answer."

Gabe set both palms on the counter. He had cleaned up his language automatically. Access did that. "Can you move me to a Class Two and keep the berth?"

"The berth needs a Three. It's a fifty-kilo minimum on the manifest."

"Can you split the deposit for me?"

"I can do half today and half before induction."

"And if I show up tomorrow with only half?"

"They can reassign the slot."

Behind them the line shifted. Nobody made a fuss. Everyone in the room knew the etiquette of other people's precarity. Do not look interested. Do not look relieved it isn't your turn yet. Do not look at all if you can help it.

Gabe pulled out his wallet. The leather had gone thin at the fold and been reinforced with clear packing tape. He counted once, stopped, counted again. Mira knew before he did that he was short. She could see it in the way he flattened the bills on the counter, trying to press more value into them.

"I've got ninety-two," he said.

Nessa swallowed. "I can hold the slot for fifteen minutes."

Gabe huffed one breath through his nose. "Generous empire."

Nessa's mouth twitched. "Don't flirt with me when I'm extorting you. It cheapens the craft."

That got a sound out of him, not quite a laugh. He rubbed his thumb over the edge of a bill until it curled. Mira reached into her coat pocket before either of them made it into pride.

"Here," she said.

He looked over, immediate refusal already there.

"Don't," he said quietly.

"You need twenty-eight."

"Mir."

"You need twenty-eight."

He held her eyes for a beat too long, anger and gratitude mixing into the expression that had haunted both of them since they were children and there had not been enough money for the thing that was plainly needed. Then he looked away.

"I'll pay you back tomorrow," he said.

"Sure."

Nessa took the bills without comment. Her nail polish had chipped down to half moons at the cuticles. She printed the confirmation slip on a machine that squealed and jammed once before surrendering a strip of thermal paper.

"Induction at five forty," she said. "Pod bank C. Bring your own contact gel if you've got it. House stock's watered."

"Always nice to hear that sentence about a medical process," Gabe said.

"Welcome to the future."

He folded the slip and tucked it into the yellow-taped pocket of his jacket with the tide sheet. For a second, watching the two pieces of paper disappear together, Mira thought that the whole city was in miniature there, weather and borrowed flesh, schedules and debt, survival filed beside survival until the pages wore thin.

As they turned from the counter the woman in housekeeping shoes stepped up. She was older than Mira had first thought, with a lipstick line gone feathery at the edges and a lunch sack printed with cartoon pears. "I need stair-flex if you still have one," she said before Nessa could ask. "West End tower. Twenty-six floors and the service lift's out again."

"I've got one until six," Nessa said. "After that it goes premium hospitality."

The woman closed her eyes briefly, doing whatever fast ugly math that required. "Six gets me three rooms short."

"I'm sorry."

"Yeah," the woman said, not mean, not forgiving either. "Everybody is."

Mira moved aside for the courier kid, the man with the oxygen, the next body in line, the next need. Around the room the posters kept smiling their smooth impossible smiles: better shoulders, better hands, better night skin, better work. None of the people beneath them believed the ads. They believed the shift. That was different.

Outside, the late light had started to thin. Water dripped from the underside of the tracks in a slow, regular tick. A tram thundered overhead, shaking rust dust down from the beams.

Gabe lit a cigarette and then seemed to remember who he was standing next to. "Don't."

"I wasn't going to say anything."

"Your eyebrows were."

He took one drag anyway, shallow, more ritual than intake, and crushed it under his heel after two breaths. The gesture made her sadder than the cigarette would have. Even his bad habits had to be temporary. In his body, anything that took up space had to justify the rent.

They walked half a block before he said, "Don't do that thing in your head where this turns me into your argument."

Mira looked at him. "I wasn't."

"Bullshit."

There was no heat in it. That made it harder to dodge.

"I was thinking," she said.

"Yes. Exactly. In bullet points." He adjusted the front of his jacket where the brace caught under the zipper. "I'm not your clean example, Mir. Not for them, not against them. I'm just a guy who needs a spine he doesn't own for about ten hours tomorrow."

The sentence landed so plain and so ugly there was nothing to improve in it.

They stopped at the crosswalk while a freight tram clanged through. On the far side of the street, down by the bus shelter, three workers in borrowed dock bodies waited with their hoods up against the spray. Even standing still they were different from the bodies underneath them: broader through the chest, harder in the knees, built for lift and repetition and cold. One of them was eating soup out of a deli container with hands too thick and clean to be born hands. When he laughed at something the smallest lag rippled through the face, that tiny half beat Mira knew from returns, the evidence of somebody occupying matter made for speed rather than ease.

She thought of Lin. She thought of the bargain shells at Warehouse Nine, sorted not as people, not as corpses, not even as damaged goods, but as useful residual density for future stock. She thought of the line inside Harbor Shift, of the housekeeping worker bargaining with the clock, of the courier boy pricing his body before he got to use it, of Gabe quietly taking watered contact gel because he could not afford standards.

The same machine that filled Warehouse Nine was standing under that bus shelter in borrowed shoulders, waiting to clock in.

"I know what they're doing," she said, mostly to herself.

"Yeah."

"I know what they deserve."

"So do I. That still doesn't tell me what I do Friday."

"I don't know what happens after."

Gabe looked at her then, really looked, and his face lost the last of the swagger it wore in public. What remained was younger and older at once: her little brother, the man who had learned to joke because jokes kept the room from smelling too strongly of failure.

"Well," he said, softer now. "Figure that part too."

There was no comfort in it. That made it useful.

They bought soup on the way uphill because the wind had turned sharper and because neither of them wanted to go home yet and say what the afternoon had become. The vendor ladling from the steel vat wore a denim apron covered in enamel pins from dead ferry lines and hummed old merengue under his breath between customers. Gabe, returning to form by inches, asked him whether the soup was medicinal. The man said, "Depends what you did," and gave them extra bread because he approved of the question.

By the time they reached Brine Court, the windows over the stairwell had gone mirror-dark. The tram screamed around the bend again, the same sound, the same metal complaint. Upstairs, their apartment held the warmth of shut rooms and the faint wet-fabric smell Lin always brought back from Blue Hour now, detergent threaded through city air.

Gabe toed off his shoes at the door and stood there one second too long, eyes closed. Then he took the confirmation slip from his pocket, set it on the table like a bill, and went to the bathroom to peel off the brace. Mira heard the muffled curse when the straps dragged over raw skin.

She unpacked the soup. Lin had left a note by the stove in careful block letters: JO STOLE ME FOR DINNER. DO NOT WAIT. Someone had added, in Jo's thicker handwriting beneath it: FEED YOURSELVES ANYWAY.

Mira almost smiled. Then Gabe came back out in his undershirt, moving slowly, and the smile went nowhere.

The brace had left red trenches around his waist. Above one hip the skin was mottled yellow and old plum where pressure had pooled from yesterday's shift. He saw her looking and reached automatically for his shirt.

"Don't," she said.

He stopped.

She did not touch the bruises. She wanted to and did not. There were kinds of care he could take and kinds he could not bear. Instead she slid the bowl of soup toward him. "Eat before the pills."

"Yes, ma'am."

He sat. The joke was dutiful this time, not lively. He ate half the bowl and most of the bread and fell asleep on top of the blanket before finishing the second pain pill, one arm thrown over his face against the kitchen light. Mira took the pill bottle from the mattress before it rolled off and put a folded towel under his knees the way the physical therapist had once shown him before the insurance visits ran out.

Then she stood at the sink with the Harbor Shift receipt in one hand and the soup spoon in the other, looking out at the dark window that gave her back only the room.

The thermal print had already started to gray at the fold: North Quay salvage, pod bank C, induction at five forty, deposit paid. It was tomorrow's spine, bought one humiliating bill at a time.

If CommonFrame caught even a whiff of what she had in her coat, this was the kind of paper that vanished first: Gabe's berth, the housekeeping woman's stair-flex, the courier kid's first decent shift, every cheap body the harbor treated as expendable right up until it needed one more crate moved.

A leak without somewhere for the fallout to land was just another bill shoved downhill.

Below the building the tram came through once more, metal shrieking over the curve. From the hill, faint but clear, the ferry horn answered from the dark water.

Mira set the spoon down, turned Jo's note over, and wrote four words on the blank side: Tavi. Lin. Gabe. Harbor Shift.

Blue Hour had to hear this before the whole thing came back to them as rumor. Tavi needed the Warehouse Nine proof while it still smelled like bleach and drain rot instead of theory. Lin had to know what a panic purge would do to the people still renting themselves by the shift, and Jo needed time to decide what the back room could hold when the first workers got cut loose. If there was a way to drag CommonFrame into daylight without feeding Gabe to the wreckage, it would have to start at Blue Hour before dawn.


# Chapter 11

Just before dawn, Mira found Tavi at the wetbench in Blue Hour's back room, with the late-night dryers thumping out front hard enough to make the laundromat sound, from the street, like one patient heart with too much laundry on it. The steam had thinned enough to show lint pasted to the pipes and the water marks climbing the cinderblock in old brown rungs. He had a pair of borrowed wrists opened under the lamp and was working grit out of a tendon channel with a dental pick; somebody from the shrimp sheds had dropped the frame before close. The room still carried fryer grease under the detergent, iodine under the steam, and that sweet tired tissue smell cheap stock got after a double shift in salt air.

He did not look up when Mira came in. “If that’s another ankle for tonight, tell them I’m dead and Jo already sold the corpse for parts.”

“It’s me.”

That made him glance over. He saw her face and put the pick down at once. “Jesus. What happened?”

Mira shut the door behind her and turned the lock. The click sounded louder than the dryers. “I need you to listen before you start fixing it.”

“That bad.”

“Worse.”

He reached for the paper cup by the sink, took half a swallow of coffee gone cold, and then peeled the sleeve off it in a slow spiral the way he did when he needed his hands occupied. The skin over the burn scar on his forearm looked waxy in the bench light. “All right. Talk.”

She had meant to do it clean. She had meant to lay out Warehouse Nine the way she laid out a damage claim: sequence, evidence, cost. Instead the words came out with the smell still on them. She described the loading bay slick with old mash and coolant, the bins tagged for residual richness instead of defect type, and the shells lined up under strip lights with administrative stickers on their heels. CommonFrame used the same little yellow bars for salvage credits, only these had numbers that matched debtor riders and expired lease contracts. The room held half-pulped faces that had been spared the grinder because someone farther up the canal had decided there was still enough life left in them to stretch across a cheaper product line. She told him that the sorter notes kept repeating recomposition yield, donor blend stability, wash variance acceptable in value tier. She told him about the rack of bargain models waiting for seed, clean on the outside and empty in the legal sense only.

Tavi did not interrupt. Once, when she said donor blend, his mouth opened and closed again. When she got to the part about debtor scans hidden in consent riders, he rubbed his thumb across his flattened nose hard enough to pink the skin.

When she finished, the dryers kept thumping. Somewhere out front a coin changer coughed and fell quiet.

Tavi said, “Fuck.”

“That’s all you’ve got?”

“No.” He dropped the coffee sleeve on the bench. Damp paper curled against the metal tray. “That’s the part I can say before I start saying things I can’t take back.”

Mira leaned against the sink because she had been standing on warehouse concrete since yesterday in some private part of herself and had not realized it until now. “I thought you already knew enough to be sick. You didn’t know this.”

“I knew the wash cheat was deliberate. I knew they were stretching residue because the margins on bargain stock were impossible otherwise. I didn’t know they were sorting live richness at the disposal end like meat grades.” He looked at the opened wrists under the lamp, not at her. “Lin saw it too.”

“Yes.”

He shut his eyes for a beat. “Fuck.”

Mira took the anomaly wafer copy out of her coat pocket and set it on the bench beside the ruined coffee sleeve. It looked pathetic there, a square of stolen proof no bigger than a communion wafer, all the rot of the company compressed into something you could lose in the couch cushions. “We can’t sit on it anymore.”

He gave a short laugh with no humor in it. “That sentence can mean six bad things.”

“It means we stop pretending this is a claims problem or a repair problem or one warehouse full of ugly paperwork. We tell people.”

Now he looked at her. The looseness had gone out of him. He straightened from the bench so fast the stool wheels clicked backward. “No.”

The answer landed hard enough that for a second she thought she had misheard him under the dryers. “No what.”

“No, not like that.”

“Like what. Words in public?”

“Like panic in public.” He pointed at the wafer without touching it. “You put that raw into the city and by breakfast the Bureau freezes every bargain line in circulation on safety grounds. They’ll call it temporary, like a clamp that’s only supposed to stay on for a minute, and then they’ll leave it there till the tissue dies. They start doing seizure checks in rooming houses and break bays and sex hotels. Every renter south of the canal who uses a cheap body because their own can’t climb stairs or lift freight or take a man twice their size gets told the stock is contaminated and their lease is void pending review. Pending review means no work, no cash, and a bill for unauthorized cognitive damage if they complain wrong.”

Mira said, “They’re printing people out of stolen scans.”

“I know what they’re doing.”

“Do you.”

“Yes.” The word cracked out sharper than he usually let himself sound. He dragged a hand over his face, then planted both palms on the bench and forced his voice back down. “Yes, I do. That’s why I’m telling you not to swing at them blind. You don’t cut into a body without somewhere for the fluid to go.”

He started moving as he talked, not pacing exactly, just setting a clamp beside the sealant gun and rolling a length of synth gauze tight enough to dent the cardboard core. He always needed a task under his hands. “You know Marisol from the hotel row, the one with the left knee that catches when she’s scared?
