You know the twins Jo sent us who split one dock frame between them because one shift each is the difference between rent and sleeping in their van? Your brother. Half the fucking quay. They’re on bargain stock because premium wash is for people who can afford to treat a body like luggage. If CommonFrame gets cornered too fast, they don’t save those people. They cut them loose. They send out a service suspension and a sorry for the inconvenience and a notice from debt resolution with cleaner fonts than the funeral home uses. We’d be standing here noble as saints while everybody we’ve been patching gets dropped back into the bodies they were trying to get out of for twelve hours at a time.”

The worst part was that he was not sounding like Celia. He was sounding like Gabe on the seawall, talking about what rent demanded from a damaged spine. Mira hated him a little for that. She hated the city for making everybody with working eyes arrive at the same rotten arithmetic.

“So what,” she said. “We protect access to the machine that’s grinding them.”

Tavi laughed again, angry now. “No. We cut the machine where it bleeds and not where it kicks.”

“That’s neat.”

“It’s true.” He stabbed a finger toward the back room wall, as if all the neighborhood bodies on the other side of it were listening. “You want the leak? Fine. Leak the debt riders. Leak the donor consent language buried in the contracts. Leak the disposal variance and the fake damage charges that keep people paying for contamination they didn’t cause. Get labor lawyers. Get one reporter who can read a yield sheet without turning it into a ghost story for rich people over brunch. Get the city looking at fraud first, because fraud makes paper move. If the first thing they hear is personhood, they’ll turn Lin into a freak exhibit and the rest of the living ones into defective inventory with better press.”

Mira folded her arms because otherwise she was going to reach for him and either shake him or hold on, and she did not trust either impulse. “You’re asking me to keep Lin hidden.”

“I’m asking you to keep Lin alive.”

“In a room.”

“For a little longer, maybe. Until we know how many there are and where. Until we’ve got maintenance keys copied and parts routes mapped and somewhere safer than your kitchen to put anybody who wakes up. You blow this open before that, CommonFrame starts pulping evidence and the Bureau helps them call it compliance.”

She could smell his skin under the steam, solder and soap and the stale clean cotton of the T-shirt he had slept in. The memory of his mouth last week came up mean and inconvenient. “And your part in it.”

He took the hit without blinking. “You think that’s what this is.”

“I think you’ve got reasons to prefer a careful story.”

“Of course I’ve got reasons.” He spread his hands. Grease lived in the lines of his knuckles no matter how hard he washed. “I helped build one of the shortcuts. I’ll wear that when it’s time. But if you think I’m hiding because I’m scared for my own neck, you’re not listening. They can hang me from the Claims Annex sign if they want. I’m talking about the woman cleaning legal offices in a rental pelvis because her own got cracked at nineteen and Medicaid gave her six months of pills and a pamphlet. I’m talking about kids doing freight in borrowed backs because their real ones won’t hold a crate. I’m talking about Lin and anyone like Lin getting swept into a warehouse with a nicer label on the door.”

He was breathing hard now, not loud, but enough to lift the scarred hollow above his collarbone. Mira noticed he had lined three scalpels up beside the sink by length, perfectly parallel, without knowing he had done it.

She said, “So your endgame is what. A better scandal.”

“My endgame is enough pressure to crack the contracts open without letting them crush the people underneath first.” His voice dropped. “Mira, if you say this wrong, the city will hear unsafe bargain stock. They won’t hear stolen lives. They’ll hear nuisance. They’ll hear liability. Rich people love an ethics talk right up until it threatens same-day service.”

The dryers stopped. The room lost its patient mechanical pulse and felt abruptly smaller, only the drip from the bench sink and the tram beginning its climb somewhere outside. She could hear the front of the laundromat now: a cart wheel bumping tile, Jo’s low voice to somebody headed home, the rattle of the metal grate.

Mira picked up the wafer and closed her hand around it. The corners bit her palm. She had come wanting reinforcement, maybe even absolution. She had imagined Tavi swearing, kissing her temple, saying yes, now, burn it down. Instead he had given her a plan full of sequence and casualties, another adult answer shaped like a delay.

“You should’ve been there,” she said. “You should’ve seen how they tagged them.”

Tavi’s face changed then, some hardness giving way into something worse because it was tenderness and grief at once. “I know. I should’ve been there instead of you.”

That almost undid her. She put the wafer back in her coat before it could. “I’m not promising anything.”

“I know.”

“And if you start acting like this is only a messaging problem, I’m gone.”

“It isn’t.” He scrubbed both hands over his face and came away looking older, bench-tired rather than flippant for once. “It’s a triage problem. That’s different.”

She unlocked the door. The front room was dark except for the soda machine glow and the strip of orange streetlight under the grate. Tavi did not try to stop her. When she stepped out into the damp detergent air of the laundromat, what she carried with her was not agreement, only the knowledge that the first person she had chosen to tell wanted a knife where she wanted a siren.

Brine Court’s hallway smelled like wet plaster, cumin, and the old radiator that hissed even when the heat was off. Somebody on the second floor had burned garlic. Somebody on the fourth was fighting soft enough to make the words worse. Mira let herself into the apartment and found Gabe asleep on the couch with the television muttering weather at low volume, one hand tucked under his shirt where the brace usually rubbed him raw. His actual brace hung over the kitchen chairback like a shed shell.

Lin sat at the table in one of Gabe’s old yellow rain shirts, sleeves rolled up six careful times. The free advertiser from the bodega lay in front of them, apartment listings cut into narrow columns and stacked by neighborhood. They had made three piles: studios, one-bedrooms, impossible fantasies with harbor views. Beside the piles sat a chipped mug full of pencil shavings and the little scissors Jo used on loose threads.

Mira stopped in the kitchen doorway.

Lin looked up at once. Their lighter iris caught the weak stove light and held it. “You’re late.”

“I know.”

“You ate?”

“At Jo’s.” It was a lie too small to matter. Lin knew it anyway by the look they gave her, but they let it pass. Mira hung her coat on the chair, careful not to knock Gabe’s brace down. “What’s this.”

Lin glanced at the piles as if seeing them from outside for the first time. “Addresses.”

“I can see that.”

“I wanted to know what people ask for a room with a door that closes.” They touched the top clipping with one finger. “This one says proof of income, government identification, and first month plus deposit. This one says no pets and no subletting and no exceptions. This one says professionals only, which I think means poor people with clean shoes.”

Despite herself, Mira smiled. It hurt. “That’s exactly what it means.”

Lin set the scissors down blade-closed. “Did Tavi agree with you.”

Mira pulled out the chair opposite them and sat. The vinyl was split under one thigh; foam pushed up through the crack. She could hear Gabe snore once and turn over in the other room. “Not exactly.”

Lin went still in the particular way they did when stress hit hard, thumb pressed against the mug until the pencil shavings inside stopped whispering against the ceramic. “Tell me.”

So Mira told them. She did not repeat every word from Blue Hour, but she told enough: Tavi’s fear of a blunt disclosure, the service suspensions, the seizure orders, and the way a safety review could become a starvation tool in less than a day. She even said the part she had most resisted hearing: that if the city moved fast, poor renters and living overlaps might take the first blow while executives found counsel and delay.

Lin listened with their hands folded around the mug of pencil shavings. The room filled with tram noise, then the faint rattle of dishes from the apartment below. When Mira finished, Lin looked down at the apartment clippings instead of at her.

“And you think he’s right,” they said.

“I think he’s right about what happens when institutions get frightened in public.”

“That is not what I asked.”

Mira rubbed a thumb over the table’s peeling laminate. Someone had once tried to paint it white. The old wood bled through at the corners. “I think parts of it are right.”

Lin nodded once. “So the plan is fraud first.”

“The plan might be contracts, debt riders, donor theft, disposal records. Enough to force scrutiny without giving them an excuse to disappear everybody into a safety process.”

“Everybody meaning me.”

“Lin.”

“No, say it correctly.” Their voice stayed quiet, which made Mira listen harder. “Everybody meaning me, and anybody else like me, if there is anybody else like me.”

The rain shirt cuffs around their wrists were rolled so evenly they almost looked measured. Lin had started doing that with sleeves, towels, and any paper they touched because order gave them one way not to dissolve into borrowed fragments. On the fridge behind them the rent notice had slipped half out from under the magnet, Brine Court Housing in block red letters above the number they already knew by heart. Twenty-four hundred dollars sat on the page with all the weight of a threat. It was only paper, but nothing about it felt abstract.

“I’m trying to keep you alive,” Mira said.

Lin’s mouth twitched, not quite a smile. “You and Tavi keep saying that like it solves the other part.”

“The other part is easier to fight if you’re breathing.”

“Breathing where.”

Mira opened her mouth and shut it again.

Lin reached for one of the clippings and flattened it. “This one is for a room above a butcher on Pell Street. The window faces a wall. It says the toilet is in the hall and the hot water works most mornings. I read it three times because it sounds terrible and also because somebody is allowed to live there under their own name.” They looked up. “You want me to stay grateful for a better hiding place.”

“That’s not fair.”

“No.” Lin’s finger stayed on the clipping. “Warehouse Nine was not fair. Waking up on your bench with somebody else’s stairwell in my head was not fair. Being kind about it does not make it a different shape.”

The television in the other room changed from weather to an ad. A cheerful voice promised instant approval on consumer credit. Gabe cursed in his sleep and rolled again.

Mira kept her own voice low. “If we do this wrong, they can take you as evidence. As property. As contaminated stock. I’m not being dramatic. I’ve read the statutes.”

“I know.” Lin’s eyes flicked to the brace on the chair, then to the rent notice, then back to Mira. “You and Gabe and Tavi are all counting bodies against money. I understand that. I’m not stupid.”

“I know you’re not.”

“Then don’t talk around me like I’m a package you’re deciding how to ship.”

The sentence slid under her guard because it was true. Mira had spent the whole evening laying other people’s strategies over Lin’s life like tracing paper: hide them, time them, use them later, protect them by deciding for them. She had called it caution because caution sounded less ugly than control.

Lin went on, still calm. “If you leak a warehouse and a set of contracts, CommonFrame says there were irregular practices in a value-tier disposal stream. They blame three managers. They improve the wash. They offer credits to renters who can prove distress. They keep owning whatever wakes up in the cheap line because nobody made them admit we exist.”

“That’s not all that would happen.”

“No.” Their voice roughened a little now, not much. “It is only the part that happens to me.”

She ran a thumb along the edge of the clipped apartments, over the impossible harbor-view stack Lin had built with embarrassing honesty, the one-bedrooms with tile floors and legal locks and monthly rents that might as well have been lunar distances. “Recognition doesn’t fix any of that overnight either.”

“I know.” Lin touched the side of their neck where the serial port sat hidden under hair that had grown just enough to soften it. “Recognition gets me on the form at all. It gets the next one on the form. It gets names where there are only numbers now.”

Mira thought of the warehouse tags, yellow bars on plastic heels. She thought of the sticky concrete and the way Lin had gone silent there, too silent even for fear. “Names are not enough if they still come for you.”

Lin’s laugh was brief and astonished. “Mira. They already come for me. The difference is that right now they can say they are collecting property.”

From the couch Gabe muttered, “Turn the damn boat around,” to whatever dream had him, then fell quiet again.

Lin lowered their voice further. “When I woke up, you asked me what I wanted to be called. You didn’t ask because it was sentimental. You asked because being called something mattered immediately. It still matters. Survival without being named is only a slower conveyor.”

There it was without rhetoric or tactical varnish: a life that could not stay borrowed forever without becoming another form of disposal.

Under Lin’s hand, the Pell Street clipping turned painfully concrete: a hall toilet, hot water most mornings, a window facing a wall. Under any normal measure it was miserable. Tonight it looked almost luxurious because somebody could rent it by saying I’m here and forcing the city, however reluctantly, to answer back.

“If the moment comes,” Lin said, “I’m not staying in the kitchen while you explain me in safer words.”

Mira heard Tavi in that too, not in tone but in certainty. Everybody had an endgame now. Everybody could make a case from pain that was real. She felt suddenly, absurdly, like the least prepared person in her own conspiracy.

“I don’t want safer words,” she said.

“Then don’t choose them.”

Lin gathered the clippings into one pile and squared the edges on the table. The motion was domestic, fussy, and devastating. Mira watched their long careful fingers align rooms they could not legally rent and understood that the argument had shifted underneath her while she was busy deciding what to reveal. It was no longer only about proof. It was about whose future counted as too dangerous to insist on.

The meeting request from Celia arrived at 9:12 the next morning with the subject line off-site reconciliation, which was the kind of language CommonFrame used when it wanted something both deniable and important. The message contained an address in South Basin and nothing else.

Mira stared at it in the break room while a vending machine clattered out stale peanuts for somebody in returns. Through the Annex glass she could see the canal traffic moving thick and gray below, tugboats and rental ferries and one barge carrying vat substrate under a tarp that snapped in the harbor wind and would not fool anyone who had ever smelled it. She thought of deleting the message. Instead she memorized the address and went.

South Basin was three tram stops and a financial class away from Brine Court. The lobby smelled like lemon polish and sealed air, with no mildew and no fryer grease wandering in from the street. A desk clerk in a slate jacket nodded her toward the lift without asking for a name, which told Mira the instructions had been left in advance. Unit 4C opened on the first knock.

Celia Rusk had taken off her shoes and was standing in stocking feet on cream carpet that probably cost more than a month at Blue Hour. Her blouse was as immaculate as ever, pale gray silk, collar exact. The incongruity was in the socks: thick brown wool, the kind older women bought for pain and never meant anyone to see. An electric kettle clicked on the counter behind her.

“Come in,” Celia said. “You look cold.”

“It’s not even winter yet.”

“In Cordera. That still counts.” Celia stepped back. “Close the door.”

Mira did, then hated how quietly it sealed. The apartment was not large, but every surface in it advertised money the way Brine Court advertised lack. It had dry walls, a couch without exposed springs, windows thick enough to keep the gulls out, and a galley kitchen with four matching plates and a bowl of clementines that had not been bought one at a time. A folded throw lay at the end of the sofa, and a bathroom visible down the hall held actual white towels. Mira’s whole body reacted before her principles could catch up. The heat settled on her skin and began taking the bite of the morning out of her wrists. There was no mold smell anywhere. With a flash of shame so sharp it felt almost like desire, she realized Gabe could sleep here without the damp climbing into his back.

Celia watched her notice it all. “Tea?”

“No.”

“I’m making some anyway.” She moved to the kettle with her usual measured efficiency, but when she tore the sugar packet she had to use both hands and brace one thumb against the counter. It was arthritis, unmistakable now that Mira saw it outside the office polish, outside the expensive pens and the little flexes between meetings. The pain sat in the room unvarnished. “Sit, if you like.”

Mira stayed standing.

“All right.” Celia poured water over the tea bag and let it darken. “This unit belongs to CommonFrame. We keep a few for visiting auditors, board members, people too important to commute with everybody else. It could be reassigned very easily.”

Mira did not pretend not to understand. “To me.”

“To you.”

“Why.”

Celia lifted the mug carefully, both hands around the heat. “Because I’d prefer not to lose one of the only competent adjusters in the building.”

“That line might work on somebody else.”

“It might.” Celia met her eyes over the rim. “You’re not somebody else, which is why I asked you here and not into conference room B.”

She crossed to the dining counter and slid a slim folder toward Mira. Inside was a one-page internal transfer memo already half-completed, blank only where Mira’s signature would go. It named her senior anomaly review specialist, effective immediately, with a salary adjustment to fourteen hundred and fifty per week before deductions, a temporary housing allotment for six months, family occupancy permitted for one additional resident, and an administrative hold on the current disciplinary referral pending internal reassignment. The whole thing was written in the clipped merciful language institutions used when they wanted obedience to feel like rescue.

Mira kept reading because stopping would have meant admitting how hard the numbers were hitting. Fourteen fifty a week before deductions, family occupancy, and six months of dry walls shoved Brine Court’s red notice back into her head alongside Gabe’s brace and the landlord’s wet cough on the phone every first of the month. She could feel the apartment around her like a hand at the base of her neck.

“There’s also room,” Celia said, “for a retention bonus if the transition is smooth.”

Mira looked up. “How generous.”

“How practical.”

“In exchange for what.”

“For staying employable and letting some matters remain inside a system capable of correcting them.”

Mira laughed once. “Correcting.”

“Yes.” Celia did not flinch from it. “Not perfectly or cleanly, but more usefully than a public detonation that hands Tissue Safety a pretext to seize half the city.”

It was Tavi’s logic wearing silk and corporate housing, and Mira hated how immediately she recognized the overlap.

“You know, then,” she said.

Celia set the tea down. “I know enough.”

“That’s a management phrase.”

“Fine.” A flicker of impatience cracked the polish. “The aggregate line was never a wash failure. It was a margin decision. The donor language in the debt riders should’ve been stripped years ago. Disposal variance at Warehouse Nine was supposed to stay a bookkeeping problem, not become visible to anyone who could read a body. Is that plain enough for you.”

Mira went very still, not from shock but because movement would have spilled something. Celia knew, not by rumor or suspicion or anomaly rates and bad instincts, but well enough to name the lie correctly.

“How long,” Mira said.

“Long enough that the question no longer interests me.”

“It interests me.”

“Of course it does. You’re still young enough to think chronology is the same thing as remedy.”

Mira shut the folder. “You knew and you left it running.”

“I knew pieces. I learned more. I stayed because staying let me keep some of the ugliest proposals off the table.” Celia’s mouth tightened. “Don’t make the mistake of believing refusal is purity. Refusal mostly changes who gets eaten first.”

The line was ugly enough to feel true in her mouth. That was what made Celia dangerous. She could say the rotten thing plainly and make it sound like adulthood.

Mira gestured at the apartment. “So this is what staying gets me.”

“This is what it can get you: warm walls, a salary you can actually plan against, distance from the Bureau, and a chance to move into internal review where you can do some good instead of some theater.” Celia’s voice softened, not sweeter but more direct, worker to worker under the executive skin. “I know what Brine Court costs when it stops pretending to be affordable. I know what two missed pay cycles do to a family. I know exactly how righteous people sound when they haven’t had to choose between telling the truth and keeping the bathroom mold out of their brother’s lungs.”

The offer bit because it was not abstract. Mira saw, with furious clarity, the shape of the temptation Celia had built: not status but dry towels, rent paid on time, Gabe able to walk from bed to kitchen without damp biting his spine, and Mira herself not flinching every time the tram card balance dipped below ten dollars. An apartment door that shut was enough to make the humiliation of wanting it immediate and total.

She said, “And in return I help you call people product more elegantly.”

“In return you keep your access and use it.” Celia folded and unfolded her aching fingers around the mug. “You think public revelation will produce recognition because you’re still thinking like an honest employee in a dishonest company. It will produce containment. Hear me carefully. If you force this into daylight before anyone with power has prepared the ground, the first official category attached to your hidden people will be hazardous material.”

“My hidden people.”

“You know what I mean.”

“I know exactly what you mean.”

Celia looked away toward the sealed window. From four floors up the canal was a gray ribbon between new glass and old brick, all of Cordera pretending money had ever cleaned anything instead of moving it out of sight. “I’m not offering absolution,” she said. “I’m offering survivability.”

The word sat between them. It was almost kind. It was also a bid.

Mira put her hand on the transfer memo again. The paper was heavy, expensive company stock, enough that even silence had nicer texture on it. For one obscene second she imagined taking it home and setting it on the table in front of Gabe, which would mean no more notices and no more bargaining with laundromat cash and late fees and whether they could stretch her salary through another month. She imagined telling Tavi they would do it slower, smarter, from inside. She imagined telling Lin to wait in a room with better heat.

The future Celia was selling did not feel impossible. That was the most dangerous part. It felt humiliatingly, seductively usable.

Mira slid the memo back across the counter. “You didn’t bring me here because I’m competent.”

“No?”

“You brought me here because you think I’ve seen enough to become expensive.”

Celia’s mouth curved, almost approving. “That too.”

“And because whatever you know, you know it’s real.”

This time Celia did not answer at all. Her silence was more precise than agreement.

The folder, the clementines, the wool socks on cream carpet: the whole apartment made an upholstered case for surrender. “If I sign this, my silence becomes part of payroll.”

“Most useful silences do.”

“Maybe.” Mira stepped back from the counter. “Mine doesn’t.”

Celia exhaled through her nose, not dramatic, just disappointed the way a manager might be disappointed by a smart employee who had chosen sentiment over process. “You are making an error.”

“Probably.”

“Inspector Sanz is less patient than I’ve been.”

“I know.”

“There are consequences to preferring spectacle over leverage.”

Mira thought of Tavi at the bench, arguing for a scalpel. She thought of Lin sorting impossible apartments into neat piles and insisting on a name that could stand on a form. Spectacle was not the choice in front of her. The choice was which damage she was willing to schedule and in whose body.

“This isn’t leverage either,” she said. “It’s a buyout.”

For the first time that morning Celia’s face lost all managerial softness. What remained underneath was older, harsher, and far more working class than the silk blouse. “Everything is a buyout when you have less money than the thing across from you.”

Mira believed that too. She believed it enough that refusing hurt.

She opened the apartment door. Cooler hallway air slid in around her ankles. “Then this one’s still no.”

Celia did not rise to follow her. “If you change your mind, do it before the month turns. South Basin units are not held forever.”

Mira almost laughed at that, the pure corporate pettiness of the line. Instead she stepped into the hall and pulled the door shut on the warm dry room.

The elevator mirrors showed her a woman who looked more temptable than she wanted to admit. On the street the city hit her all at once again: salt, tram brake sparks, fryer oil from a corner cart, the canal’s chemical breath. She stood there a second with her hands in her coat pockets until feeling came back into them.

She had refused the apartment, the salary, and the soft administrative version of disappearing. None of it put anything back together. Tavi still wanted a leak thin enough to slide past the law before it noticed. Lin wanted names, daylight, and a future that could survive paperwork. Celia wanted her bought and useful. Mira started uphill toward Brine Court knowing only that she had turned down one endgame and lost the fiction that the others could still be made into the same one. Her CommonFrame badge tapped once against her keys inside her pocket as she climbed, an ugly little reminder that whatever came next would still have to move through official rooms with the badge clipped on and her name attached.


# Chapter 12

The next official room was Municipal Arbitration Hall, which always ran too hot for the kind of business it did. The lobby radiators clanged behind grilles painted the color of old teeth, and the heat came up through the tile in damp waves that turned thrift-store blazers shiny under the arms. Everyone waiting carried paper or plastic or both. In Cordera, the people with money kept their disasters in private portals and sealed conference rooms. The people without it took a number, sweated under civic lighting, and watched their trouble crawl toward them in red digits over a service desk.

Mira stood by Courtroom C with her CommonFrame badge clipped where it belonged and felt the heat gathering under her bra band. The filtered air in the hall carried floor wax, old coffee, and, through the revolving doors each time they opened, the city's regular mix of salt, fryer grease, and machine coolant. A clerk behind a plexi window kept calling names in a voice practiced enough to strip the names clean of any family attached to them.

Across from the courtroom, a woman in a church-blue cardigan kept taking off her reading glasses and polishing them with the hem of her sleeve as if the problem might be on the lenses. One hinge had been repaired with copper wire twisted so neatly it looked like jewelry until you saw the green stain on the acetate. She had a bakery visor folded flat in her tote, the plastic brim split and repaired with transparent tape. When she leaned toward the desk to ask a question, Mira saw the flour ground into the seam of her thumbnail.

"Excuse me," the woman said to the clerk. "Will this be done before three? My grandson gets out at three-fifteen. The school starts charging after four."

The clerk did not look up. "All matters proceed in docket order, Ms. Paredes."

Yolanda Paredes nodded as if she had been given something useful. She sat down again and pressed her lips together over bad dentures that clicked once before settling. Mira knew the file under her arm better than the woman did. It described a bargain labor body, a B-line janitorial lease, and six overnight shifts a month cleaning offices in the civic core because Yolanda's own body could not handle stair towers anymore. It listed old pelvic hardware, one bad lung, and a documented standing limit of eleven minutes before spasm. She rented bodies because rent did not care what her hip could do in the morning. CommonFrame was seeking twelve thousand eight hundred forty dollars in equipment loss, contamination control, and unauthorized modification surcharges, plus a thirty-six-month suspension from future bargain leases. That was the neat way of saying: no more work route, no way to keep her landlord sweet, no way to get upstairs in buildings willing to pay cash for overnight labor.

The frame in question had split a seam in the right forearm on shift, then frozen in a freight elevator and started saying, in a voice the security footage did not match to Yolanda, Please do not put me back in the water. The next day it convulsed hard enough to smash two wall sconces in a law office lobby and throw a bucket cart into polished stone. Nobody had died. CommonFrame was still billing the event like a small riot.

Mira had opened the file three days ago and known before she reached the second page what the room was for. Someone had scrubbed the early anomaly flags out of the intake record. The yellow notice bar she had seen on the first version was gone, and the maintenance note showing the frame had reported phantom smoke odor and involuntary speech before it ever reached any outside hands had gone with it. In its place sat a clean little story about unauthorized tissue intervention leading to user-induced cognitive destabilization. She could see Celia's touch in the polish, legal's timing in the rewrite, and the poor woman on the bench being turned into a cautionary pamphlet.

Celia arrived without hurrying. She never hurried in public. Her silvering hair was still neat despite the mist outside, and she had one of her expensive pens tucked in the inside pocket of a coat that probably cost more than two months at Brine Court. Her hands worried at each other once before settling, the arthritic knuckles making a brief hard knot under the skin.

"You're early," she said.

"The docket said eleven-thirty."

"It says many things." Celia glanced at Yolanda, at the crowded benches, at the clerk. "There's still time to keep this within the file."

Mira almost laughed. It would have come out ugly. "The file is the problem."

"The file is what exists in this room." Celia's voice stayed low and polished, but the heat had taken a little of the lacquer off it. "I told you yesterday what can happen after this, and I wasn't threatening you. I was trying to describe gravity."

Mira looked through the courtroom window at the empty witness chair. "Gravity mostly seems to happen to the people without offices."

"Do not be theatrical."

"I'm at work. I don't know how."

Celia let that sit. For a second Mira saw the older worker inside her, the one who had probably once stood in hot municipal buildings with somebody else's wages in her mouth like a stone. Then it was gone. "If you confine yourself to what this claim can support, Ms. Paredes may leave with a payment schedule instead of an enforcement referral. If you decide to improvise, there will be consequences that don't stop at you."

The threat slid in under Mira's badge clip like a cold coin despite the hall's heat. Yesterday's offer had shed its polite wrapping. It put salary and license on the table, along with the little raft of respectability Mira had spent years building out of ugly work and clean shoes. Her job meant the landlord still answered her emails. It meant Gabe's prescriptions could sit on an auto-refill for a few more months. It meant she could walk into CommonFrame in the morning without being searched.

It also meant Yolanda Paredes was about to be billed for a defect that had been printed into her shift.

The courtroom door opened. A bailiff with a pink birthmark climbing out of his collarbone called the next matter and held the door with two fingers, careful of the paper cup of mint tea balanced in his other hand. "Paredes versus CommonFrame Holdings, claims subrogation review."

Yolanda stood too fast and had to catch herself on the bench. Mira saw the hitch in her left hip, the quick private wince she tried not to show strangers. Celia moved first, as if the room belonged to her. Mira followed because, for another few minutes, it still technically did.

Courtroom C was small enough that shame had nowhere to sit but inside the body. The table surfaces were laminated to look like wood. The city seal behind the arbiter's chair had been polished so often the gull in the emblem looked rubbed thin. Arbiter Selene Pike was already in place, a woman in her sixties with a strip of green athletic tape wrapped around the middle finger she used to drag the microphone closer. On the bench beside her sat a tin of sugar-free peppermints, its lid dented in the shape of a thumb.

CommonFrame's counsel stood when Mira came in. Elias Braddock was lean, carefully bearded, and wearing a navy tie with tiny silver anchors printed all over it so he could pretend to belong to a port city without ever smelling like the docks. He smiled at Mira with the kind of courtesy designed to make refusal look unprofessional.

At the back, one row behind the public seats, Yaret Sanz sat with a slim black folio on his knee. He had crossed one ankle over the other and looked as if he had been arranged there by an architect trying to suggest calm. His eyes met Mira's once, then moved away. That was worse than a stare.

Yolanda lowered herself into the respondent chair and put both hands flat on the table as if she were waiting for blood pressure. Mira sat at CommonFrame's side beside Celia, who set her pen down exactly parallel to the edge of her pad. The clerk swore everyone in. The words were old, municipal, and useless. Truth was not what rooms like this were built to recognize. They were built to assign cost.

Braddock began in a tone soft enough that the microphone had to do some work. "This is a straightforward claim arising from prohibited third-party tissue intervention in a licensed bargain labor frame, followed by a predictable cognitive instability event causing material damage, loss of serviceability, and sanitation response costs."

Mira had written enough claims notes to know that straightforward and predictable were blades sharpened on repetition. Braddock clicked his screen and the itemization came up on the monitor facing the bench:

Loss of frame serviceability: $7,200.

Biohazard remediation and transport: $1,460.

Unauthorized modification surcharge: $2,800.

Commercial property damage reserve: $1,380.

Administrative recovery fee: $1,000.

Total pending liability: $12,840.

Next line down: Lease privilege review recommended.

Yolanda made the smallest sound in the back of her throat. It was not a protest, just the body's little leak when it recognizes a number it cannot survive.

Braddock kept going. "CommonFrame's records show no cognitive irregularity of this frame prior to off-book intervention by an unlicensed tissue operator. The respondent acknowledges seeking such intervention. We therefore request affirmation of liability and temporary suspension of future lease access pending full compliance review."

Temporary was only thirty-six months written as a throat clear.

Arbiter Pike looked over the rim of her glasses. "Ms. Paredes, do you dispute the factual basis of the claim?"

Yolanda swallowed. "I dispute all of it."

"Then tell me what you dispute."

Yolanda's fingers curled over the table edge. "It was already wrong when I had it. The arm opened while I was mopping. Not all the way, but enough I could see wet under it. And it kept smelling smoke. Burned hair, like that. There wasn't any fire."

Braddock lifted one hand. "To be clear, odor complaint alone is not evidence of manufacturing defect."

Yolanda turned toward him, then back to the arbiter because poor people in such rooms learn fast where eye contact counts. "It talked."

Braddock's face did not change. "Meaning what, precisely?"

"Meaning it said things. Not me. Somebody else." She tugged the cardigan closed over her chest. "In the elevator it said, Please don't put me back in the water. I don't talk like that. I don't know what water."

Braddock let a beat pass, the generous beat of a man letting someone sound foolish at full length. "And after this alleged speech event, you did not return the frame through licensed service."

"Licensed service told me six days." Yolanda's voice roughened. "I had two shifts before rent day."

"So you chose to bring company property to an illegal repair site."

"I chose to keep working."

He inclined his head as if she had made his point for him. "How much did you pay the unlicensed operator?"

Yolanda looked down. "Eighty. Cash, folded in his hand before he even touched the arm."

"Cash?"

"Yeah."

"And did this operator provide any written release, contamination waiver, or tissue chain documentation?"

"No. He wrapped the seam and told me not to lift with it till morning."

"Did you understand, when you signed your lease agreement, that unauthorized modification voided coverage?"

Yolanda hesitated long enough for the answer to become dangerous. "I signed what they put in front of me. Rent day doesn't wait for the fine print."

"That was not my question."

Mira watched the woman shrink sentence by sentence. Yolanda's shoulders came in first, tugging the blue cardigan tight across her chest. Then the words got shorter. Then shame did the last part, and the room helped by pretending shame was evidence. Braddock never raised his voice. He did not need to. He had a chart, a contract, and a woman who had missed wages to be told that survival counted as consent.

Arbiter Pike asked Yolanda about her work schedule, her service call, the clinic. The questions were cleaner than Braddock's and did almost the same damage. Mira heard herself in the cadence. Claims had trained her to strip a life down until it fit a box. Ms. Paredes, when precisely did you first observe forearm seam compromise. Ms. Paredes, were you at any point informed that odor experiences may arise from cross-environmental contamination. Ms. Paredes, please answer yes or no where possible.

Yes or no where possible was another way of saying leave your life outside.

Yolanda said, "I called because it was saying things. The girl on the line told me to power down and return it after my lease block. I said I couldn't, I had two buildings left. She said that was my choice." Her eyes moved to the monitor and then away. "I know what all that money means. You don't have to read it slow."

For the first time, something twitched in Celia's jaw.

Braddock clicked to the maintenance log. The timestamps were clean, the notation was clean, and the whole thing was too clean. Mira had spent enough years in archives to know when a record had been ironed. The original first-call note had come in at 02:14, flagged by an overnight service rep who hated her job enough to type honestly: reports involuntary vocalization, smoke odor without source, probable mnemonic bleed. Now the entry read 11:32, after the visit to the back-room clinic, and the note had become respondent reports post-modification instability.

Someone had not only changed the story. Someone had changed the clock.

Mira heard the blood in her ears, not as a pulse but as a pipe sound, water forced through somewhere too narrow. She could see the exact terminal where the alteration had likely happened. She could see the compliance dropdown. She could see how easy it had been.

Arbiter Pike held up her taped finger before Braddock could speak. "Five minutes. I want the service-call chain loaded before we proceed."

The hall felt even hotter after the courtroom, as if the building had been storing other people's fear in the walls all morning and was ready to give some back. Mira stood beside a dead ficus in a brass planter while a clerk hustled past with a stack of files hugged to her chest. Through the narrow window in the courtroom door she could see Yolanda still sitting at the table, one hand over her mouth, the other resting on the bakery tote as if she meant to keep it from being taken too.

Celia came up beside her and caught the fabric at Mira's elbow between two fingers. She did not squeeze hard; harder would have been simpler.

"If you speak past the log," she said, "you're done. They'll take the license before lunch."

Mira looked through the wire glass at Yolanda. "They're already taking hers."

Celia let go. "And they'll keep taking after they're done with you."

***

When they went back in, Braddock turned toward her at last. "CommonFrame would like to call Ms. Alves as records custodian and claims adjuster familiar with the service history."

Celia's pen moved a fraction of an inch toward Mira's hand. It did not touch her, but it came close enough to feel like instruction.

Mira stood when her name was called. The witness chair was still warm from the last body in it. When she sat, the vinyl stuck to the back of her thighs through her skirt. The clerk asked if she would swear to tell the truth. Mira said yes. Her own voice sounded dry, filed down, useful.

Braddock smiled his anchor-tie smile. "Ms. Alves, in the ordinary course of your employment, did you review the Paredes claim file?"

"Yes."

"And based on the reviewed materials, did the frame present to CommonFrame with evidence consistent with unauthorized tissue intervention?"

Mira looked at the monitor again, at the clean timestamps and the neat lie.

"Not the first time I saw it," she said.

A hush tightened across the room, thin and municipal as carbon paper. It was the ordinary kind that came when everyone decided at once that the script had slipped.

Braddock did not look at Celia. He was too disciplined for that. "I'm sorry?"

"The first version of the file didn't say that." Mira kept her hands flat in her lap so nobody could see what wanted to happen to them. "It showed a service complaint before any outside repair. Odor event, involuntary speech, forearm seam compromise, possible mnemonic bleed. Those notes are gone from the version you entered today."

Braddock's courtesy hardened by a degree. "Ms. Alves, if you have concerns about internal notation revision, those are not the subject of this hearing."

"They are if you're using the revision to bill her."

"Answer the question asked."

Arbiter Pike leaned forward, tape flashing green around her finger. "Ms. Alves, do you have a foundation for what you are asserting?"

Mira could feel Celia beside her like a heat source. She knew exactly what happened next if she kept going, and she knew it in bills, in lost access, in Gabe asking how bad it was and trying to make a joke too early, and in Brine Court Housing's auto-email on the first of the month arriving to a salary that no longer existed.

Yolanda was looking at her with the stripped face people wore at clinic doors and collection counters: not hope, not exactly, because hope cost too much by then. Just attention so total it felt like debt.

Mira said, "Yes."

Braddock cut in. "On what basis?"

"On the basis that I handled repeated bargain-line anomalies with the same pre-repair pattern and that this file originally matched them." Mira heard her own training taking over, the precise narrowing she used when everything inside her wanted to break shape. "B-line aggregate labor frames, lots four-C through four-H. Phantom odor. Unsourced speech. Residual image intrusion. Seam stress in tissue that tested within ordinary wear tolerance. The events presented before off-book intervention, not after it."

Braddock laughed once without sound. "You're offering speculative pattern language from unrelated files."

"No. I'm offering company knowledge."

"Ms. Alves," Celia said, finally speaking. Her voice was immaculate again, which meant it had cost her something. "Stay within the Paredes record."

Mira turned just enough to look at her. "This is the Paredes record. It's just not the polished one."

Arbiter Pike's eyes moved between them. "Ms. Alves, if you are alleging evidence alteration, say so plainly."

So Mira did, because there was a point at which you either laid the blade on the table or kept pretending it was silverware.

"CommonFrame altered the service log and removed early anomaly flags to make this look like contamination from illegal repair," she said. "The frame was already unstable when Ms. Paredes leased it. The carryover was in production."

The word carryover landed badly. It was too soft and too familiar, and she saw that at once.

"No. Not carryover. Overlap. The bargain line is seeded with retained mnemonic material. We keep calling it contamination on the customer side because that's cheaper than admitting it's manufacturing."

Braddock was on his feet. "I object to this entire line as unsupported, irrelevant, and plainly retaliatory. This witness is under separate internal review."

"For what?" Arbiter Pike asked.

Celia answered before he could. "Improper file access and suspected contact with unlicensed repair actors."

Celia laid the accusation into the room with the same neat care she used on her pens and cuffs, as if she were handing over a coat.

Mira heard herself say, "Not suspected."

Celia's head turned.

Mira kept going. If she stopped now the room would rush back over her and set. "I've been sending people to off-book repair because your licensed service windows bury poor renters in downtime, penalties, and replacement fees. I've also performed intake on those repairs. I know the difference between a dirty stitch and a seeded memory event. This wasn't caused by an eighty-dollar patch in a laundry back room."

Yolanda inhaled so sharply it clicked in her dentures.

Braddock said, "So the witness admits participation in illegal tissue handling."

"I admit participation in keeping people from getting ruined for defects you printed into them." Mira looked at the arbiter, not at him. "CommonFrame's bargain line uses aggregate lattice and salvage recomposition. Returned shells with high residual load are not always pulped. They're sorted, reseeded, and sent back out. I saw the anomaly rates. I saw the service histories. I saw shells in Warehouse Nine tagged by residual richness instead of defect type."

The last part changed the room. Braddock's face stayed composed, but the composition now required labor. Beside him, Celia's pen stopped halfway to the pad, then lowered with deliberate precision. The line of her mouth tightened as she recalculated what leverage remained now that Warehouse Nine had been said aloud.

Arbiter Pike said, very carefully, "Did you just reference an off-record facility?"

"Yes."

"Were you authorized to access it?"

"No."

"Do you have documents supporting what you have said?"

Mira thought of the wafer in her bag, warm from being carried close to her body all morning. She thought of Lin in Brine Court, hidden in a life stitched from everybody else's leftovers and their own fierce will, and of how much she was not saying, even now.

"I've copied data and contemporaneous notes," she said. "Not with me at this table."

Braddock spread his hands. "So we are to suspend a valid claim on the basis of admitted theft, unlawful repair activity, and a disgruntled employee's conspiracy theory."

"I'm not disgruntled," Mira said. "I'm late."

That almost made Yolanda laugh. It made Arbiter Pike's mouth twitch, then flatten.

"Ms. Alves," Pike said, "did the original Paredes service complaint contain a reference to involuntary vocalization before any unlicensed intervention?"

"Yes. Before anybody off-book laid a hand on the frame."

"Did it contain a reference to odor anomaly?"

"Yes. Smoke odor without source, same entry block."

"Did you personally view those entries?"

"I did."

"Can you state when?"

"Three days ago, before litigation prep locked the file."

Braddock said, "Again, unsupported."

Pike ignored him. "And in your professional assessment, would an exterior forearm patch produce involuntary speech in a frame absent preexisting cognitive instability?"

This was the question that mattered because it let the room pretend it only wanted technical truth. Mira gave it the kind that would hurt.

"No," she said. "Not unless the patch tech somehow sewed an entire second nervous event into the tissue, which didn't happen here. An arm seam patch can introduce infection, poor adhesion, ugly scarring, electrical mismatch if the work is garbage. It doesn't manufacture a woman begging not to be put back in the water. What it can do is give the company something cheap to point at after the fact."

Yaret stood without being asked. He did it so smoothly the chair scarcely moved.

"Arbiter," he said, "the Municipal Tissue Safety Bureau requests leave to note the witness's admissions for enforcement purposes."

His voice was formal enough to iron itself. Mira hated that she almost found it soothing.

Pike said, "You may note what you have heard. You may not convert this hearing into a seizure event."

"That is not my intention."

No, Mira thought. His intentions were always prettier than his results.

Braddock was already regrouping. "Even if one indulged this witness, the respondent still knowingly voided her coverage by seeking illicit intervention. Contract terms are clear."

Yolanda found her voice again, ragged but present. "Contract terms don't clean a thirty-two-story building at two in the morning."

Braddock turned to her with professional sadness. "Ms. Paredes, your circumstances don't alter the agreement."

"No," Mira said. "But your production fraud does."

Celia shut her eyes once. When she opened them, she looked tired enough to be honest but chose otherwise. "Mira. Stop."

It was the first time all morning she had used Mira instead of Ms. Alves.

Cold opened under Mira's ribs despite the boiler heat. Celia had finally placed the last exit door where everyone could see it. She could shut up now, lose a little dignity, and keep the paycheck. People did it every day. She had done it every day. That was the whole machine.

She looked at Yolanda's copper-wired glasses, at the bakery visor in the tote, at the hand flattened over a number that would eat a life. Then she looked back at the arbiter.

"CommonFrame knows the anomaly rate isn't accidental," she said. "They know because the rate is useful. Poor donors sign scan rights in debt paperwork. Returned bargain shells are recomposed instead of destroyed. Residual-rich stock gets pushed back into circulation. When the overlap breaks surface, you call it customer damage, illegal repair contamination, cognitive instability, anything that invoices clean. That's what this hearing is. It's not a neutral review. It's cost recovery for deliberate manufacture."

Nobody applauded. Nobody gasped. This was not that kind of room. The clerk kept typing. Somewhere out in the hall a printer started up. The only visible sign that anything had changed was Yolanda lifting her head, slowly, as if it had been resting under somebody else's hand.

Arbiter Pike removed her glasses and polished them with a square of cloth pulled from her sleeve. She took enough time doing it that the silence became procedural instead of dramatic. "The court is not prepared to adjudicate allegations of production fraud on oral testimony alone," she said. "However, it is also not prepared to assign liability on a record whose chain and content are now under direct dispute by a sworn company employee with relevant technical experience."

Braddock said, "Arbiter, this is an abuse invitation."

"Counsel," Pike said, without looking at him, "you will survive a continuance."

She set the glasses back on. "Pending referral to special review, the Paredes liability claim is stayed. No interim charges will issue to the respondent. Lease suspension is likewise stayed until the record is examined." She looked at the clerk. "Preserve the transcript and service file access history. Full chain."

Yolanda closed her eyes. Her mouth opened a little on the exhale, and Mira could see the exact effort it took not to mistake delay for salvation. This was the bill held above her face for a few more days, nothing kinder than that. In rooms like this, even time cost.

Braddock sat down with the careful economy of a man already planning the next room. Celia did not sit. She was looking at Mira with a flat, exhausted fury that had no space for surprise because surprise required innocence and they had both spent that years ago.

Yaret stepped forward only when the clerk said adjourned. He waited until the microphone went dark and the arbiter had risen. Then he opened the black folio and removed two forms plus a thin polymer card.

"Ms. Alves," he said, as if they were meeting in a lobby and not at the edge of her wage. "Based on your admissions today, your tissue-handling license and claims access credentials are suspended effective immediately pending formal investigation. You are ordered not to enter licensed repair premises, alter or remove biological stock, or access protected service records."

He held out the polymer card that marked her temporary suspension. The lettering looked cheap for something that could close so much.

"In addition," he went on, "the Bureau is issuing a civil penalty for unlicensed repair participation in the amount of five thousand dollars. Payment instructions are enclosed."

Five thousand dollars landed harder than the big number on Yolanda's monitor because this one had her name on it. Five thousand dollars was rent and Gabe's medication and the grocery money Jo sometimes pretended was change. It was every stupid decent reason Celia had known how to weaponize.

Mira took the papers because making him repeat himself would not make them lighter.

Braddock began gathering his case tablet. "CommonFrame will also, of course, be terminating Ms. Alves for cause."

For cause was another blade.

Celia finally spoke, and now the polish cracked enough to show the old hunger underneath. "Your payroll ends today," she said. "Your health coverage runs to the end of the month if legal approves it. Security will escort you for collection of personal items at the annex under supervision. You will surrender your badge now."

Mira unclipped it. The skin beneath the clip had sweated a pale half-moon into her blouse. She put the badge on the table. It made a thinner sound than she expected.

Yolanda half rose from her chair. "Ma'am," she said to Mira. "I didn't ask you to lose your job."

Mira turned. Yolanda's hand was still on the tote with the broken visor inside it. She looked sick with gratitude, which was one of the worst looks a person could be forced to wear in public.

"I know," Mira said.

That was all there was room for. A guard had already appeared at the door, this one broad-shouldered and embarrassed, with acne scars silvering one cheek under the courtroom lights. He did not touch her. He just lifted an arm toward the hall in that careful way institutions used when they wanted force to look optional.

Mira picked up her bag. The wafer was inside, along with her keys, a half packet of painkillers, and the lunch she had not eaten. The room felt hotter now, though maybe it was only that everyone in it had permission to look at her differently. She was no longer a colleague or an adjuster or anything respectable in office terms. She was a contaminant in office shoes.

Celia capped her pen with a hard little click. "You've made this much harder than it needed to be."

Mira met her eyes. "For who?"

Celia did not answer. That was answer enough.

The guard walked her out through the hall where the next set of poor people waited with folders on their knees. Some glanced up, saw the papers in her hand, the dead badge, Sanz behind her, and looked back down fast. They knew an ejection when they saw one. Bureaucracy had choreography. The clerk at the window did not pause her work. Overhead, the red numbers rolled to the next case.

At the security desk a woman with chipped bronze nail polish returned Mira's pocketknife after logging it out of the morning tray, then hesitated and kept the badge on her side of the glass. "Company property," she said, apologetic because apology was free.

Outside, the civic-core steps shone with old rain. The afternoon had turned bright in that mean autumn way that made every puddle look metallic. Ferry horns moved somewhere beyond the buildings. A tram bell clanged on the avenue. The heat of the hall left her all at once, replaced by wind off the water pushing through her blouse where the badge clip had been.

The forms in her hand were already softening at the corners from sweat. They named a suspension, a fine, and termination pending final processing, and each word did its own small separate harm. She could picture the payroll deposit that would not land on Friday. She could picture Gabe saying he was fine too quickly. She could picture Brine Court Housing's subject line in her inbox and the way the auto-generated courtesy always sounded most insulting right before eviction.

Behind the glass doors, Yaret was already turning back toward the interior as if she had never required more than a signature. Celia was nowhere visible, and that suited Mira. She did not want to see whether Celia had stayed to work or to watch.

She folded the forms once, then separated the Bureau fine from the rest and worked it into thirds until the number made a white seam in the paper. That went into her coat pocket, where the folded fine pressed against her thigh. The rest went in her bag with the lunch and the wafer and the painkillers, all of it the same weight now. Then she went down the steps before the guard inside decided that even standing there counted as trespass.


# Chapter 13

The front glass sweated under Jo's forearm as she changed the hours with a red laundry marker fat as a thumb so nobody could pretend not to read it. It was the next morning, still before dawn.

PUBLIC WASH 6-10  
DROP-OFF ONLY 10-4  
PICKUP 4-8

The blank middle of the day was not blank at all. It was the clinic, hidden in plain sight behind steam and the rattle of old dryers, and now that Mira had no salary to leave for and no legal cover left to lose, it might as well have been her real address.

The marker had bled in the condensation, giving every letter a small wound. Jo stood on the milk crate by the door with the cap in her teeth and looked down at the sidewalk traffic with the satisfaction of someone who had just changed a border.

"If anybody asks," she said around the cap, "dryer three is eating belts, dryer six is eating quarters, and my staff is tragically incompetent."

Gabe, hunched on the folding stool by the counter with his brace under a thermal shirt and a clipboard on his knee, lifted two fingers. "I object to one-third of that."

"Object seated," Jo said. "You're on the stool because your back still thinks it owns the schedule."

Mira locked the front till and slipped the key into the pocket that already held the fine notice from the Bureau. It was five thousand dollars on heavy municipal paper. She had folded it twice, then once more, until the number lived in a white seam against her thigh. The paper had the same dead, confident feel as the hearing room. It still surprised her that a city could throw a price onto your life with one clean stamp and expect you to walk home carrying it.

The first knock at the back came before Jo climbed off the crate.

By eight-thirty the laundromat smelled like hot detergent, fryer oil from the empanada cart outside, and the metallic sweetness of opened tissue sealant. Jo kept the front half noisy and respectable. She ran three empty dryers with damp towels inside so the back-room wetbench could hum and click under cover. She took real laundry from two old men who liked her enough not to notice anything and not enough to ask. Lin worked the aisle between washers in a brown delivery cap pulled low and a cardigan that hid the serial port at the nape. Anybody glancing in from the street would have seen a shy helper sorting tagged shirts. Anybody who stopped longer would have seen the line of people pretending to wait for wash.

Mira had spent years training her body to move fastest under fluorescent contempt. The hearing had not taken that out of her. It had only changed who was standing on the other side of the table.

The woman on the repair chair introduced herself as Leonie Pardo in a voice that made the name sound borrowed. She was maybe twenty-eight, with lemon bleach dry on the cuffs of her janitor pants and a plastic strawberry barrette clipped into hair so short it had nothing to hold. She worked one cough drop from cheek to cheek, training her mouth into a new habit.

"It happens when the spin kicks up," Leonie said. She kept looking toward the dryers even though the door to the back room was shut. "The sound gets in my elbows. Then my hands start this thing."

She lifted them. They pinched and released in the air, thumb to forefinger, thumb to forefinger, in neat little bites. It was not random twitching or a seizure. It was a practiced motion.

"You sew?" Mira asked.

"No. I tear things, mostly." Leonie gave a quick embarrassed smile. "Office trash, bad mop heads, my own pockets. I don't sew."

"How long?"

"Three days. Since Saturday night." Leonie swallowed the cough drop, winced, then went on. "And I keep hearing somebody say blue line first. Not in my head like a thought. More like my mouth already knows it and I'm late catching up."

Mira wrote it on the paper intake slip. `blue line first`. She wrote `pinch motion bilateral, triggered by spin cycle`. She wrote `client denies sewing history`. Her handwriting had gone hard and narrow since the hearing, the letters packed shoulder to shoulder, every inch of paper feeling bought with blood.

Tavi leaned over from the wetbench with a probe light clipped to his ear. He had already changed into the apron he used for sealant work, the one with old burns silvered into the chest. "Let me see the left forearm."

Leonie rolled the sleeve. The bargain frame underneath was hotel-clean on the surface and failing in all the places money hid. The seam at the inner wrist had gone pink and damp. Cheap skin, overused flexion, too many nights scrubbing somebody else's sink with leverage the model had not been printed to repeat this often. There was also a faint line of puckered scar tissue over the radial tendon that did not belong on any factory-fresh wrist.

"That scar yours?" Mira asked.

Leonie frowned at it, affronted by the idea that her own arm could surprise her. "No."

"You ever rent for the docks? Freight? Rigging?"

"I clean legal offices in Civic West." She glanced toward the front room. "You need me to say yes to something? Because I don't."

"No." Mira touched the scar with a swab and Leonie's fingers immediately made the pinching motion again, faster now, stitching invisible cloth in the steam. "I need you to keep saying exactly what happened."

From the front, Jo called, "Gabe, if that man with the laundry bag asks whether I can rush his sheets, tell him yes if he has cash and no if he has a poem."

"What if he has both?"

"Then charge him twice."

The room laughed, including Leonie, and Mira felt the laugh move through the wall like borrowed weather. That was how Blue Hour stayed open now. It was not secrecy or even caution. It stayed open because Jo had turned panic into shift management and because the neighborhood had started protecting the desk with the automatic ferocity people gave a working boiler in winter.

Mira cleaned the wrist, checked the seam depth, then moved to Leonie's neck to test response. The skin there went colder under her glove. It was a residual surge. The dryers thudded into a faster cycle and Leonie's whole body tensed, not with fear but with a readiness that had not begun in her.

"Blue line first," Leonie said softly. "Orange page is a trap."

Tavi's head came up.

Mira wrote that down too.

"You heard that exact phrase before?" he asked.

Leonie blinked. "I heard it just now."

"No," Tavi said. "I mean in the carryover. Has it repeated?"

"When I was cleaning the arbitrators' bathrooms yesterday. A faucet rattled and there it was again. I nearly stitched the hem of a judge's robe to the sink towel." She laughed once, then put a hand over her mouth, embarrassed by the sound, the reflex of somebody who had spent too long in offices where even laughter felt billable. "Sorry."

"Don't be sorry," Mira said. "Just stay still."

She slid the scanner over the wrist and watched the cheap display sketch stress brightening under the skin. She hated relying on the scanner now. It logged more than it needed to, and every device in the room had started to look like something that could testify for the wrong side if seized. Tavi still needed it to work fast. They all knew that. They also all knew he was going to burn half his system one day before he let the Bureau take it whole. The thought came to Mira as a bodily certainty, not a prediction, and she pushed it away.

"Twenty for the seam and the wash," she said. "If we have to do a memory flush past local residue, that's extra. Cash only."

Leonie looked at the floor. "I've got thirteen, a transit card with maybe two rides, and meal vouchers from the court building because they keep ordering too much lunch and pretending it's dignity."

Mira felt the fine notice against her leg again. It was thirteen dollars. She could calculate what that meant faster than mercy. It meant two bags of broth if Gabe's pain spiked and he couldn't cook. It meant part of the light bill Jo pretended not to mention. It meant one quarter of the painkillers Tavi bought in singles because buying a bottle drew attention. It also meant Leonie had already come here after deciding against some other necessity.

"Ten and the vouchers," Mira said.

Leonie exhaled so hard the cough drop smell came back out, medicinal cherry over bleach. "I can do that."

"I know you can."

Tavi gloved up and took the arm with the reverent impatience he got around damaged tissue. "You're going to feel pressure, then warmth, then me swearing because these bargain cuffs were printed by sadists with a quarterly bonus."

"That part I believe," Leonie said.

He flashed her a grin and bent over the seam. Public Tavi sounded loose enough to slide out of any room. His hands never lied. He stabilized the wrist, opened the inflamed seam with a microblade, and exposed the inner lattice in a fan of wet pearl. The old scar sat threaded through it like a private signature. Somebody else's healing laid into factory stock. Mira watched Leonie's face as Tavi irrigated. There was always the body in front of her and the absent body pressing through it. Blue Hour had stopped being strange when it got too common to afford wonder.

Lin came in with a stack of paper slips and a pencil sharpened down to insult. "Three more waiting. One can stand. One says he can't hear brass instruments without vomiting. One is only here because his girlfriend thinks the hand isn't his."

"The hand isn't his?" Tavi asked.

Lin held out the slip. "He says it signs his ex-wife's name whenever he gets drunk."

"He waits," Mira said.

Lin nodded. Their face stayed careful in public, but in the soft wet heat of the back room a small, mean delight crossed it. "I told him drinking first and then visiting us was disrespectful to the science."

Tavi barked a laugh. "See? Natural clinician."

Lin's mouth twitched at the praise, then settled. Under pressure they sometimes went very still, like stillness was a rail they could grab with both hands. Mira had learned to watch for that. Stillness meant the fragments were crowded.

"What else?" she asked.

Lin checked the slips. "A grandmother rental from Market South. Teenager hand reflexes, mostly percussion. She brought fritters in a paper bag because she thinks that changes the price."

"It changes Jo's mood," Mira said.

"Nothing changes Jo's mood," Gabe called from the counter. "It rotates on fixed machinery."

Jo opened the back door with her hip, set down a mug of coffee by Mira's elbow, and took Leonie's vouchers without counting them. "Front room says two people saw you in the Hall yesterday," she told Mira. "One thinks you're brave, one thinks you're stupid, and both need help with their borrowed tendons. Congratulations on your new celebrity."

Mira kept her eyes on Leonie's arm. "Did either one bring money?"

"One brought cash. The other brought canned peaches and a prayer card."

"Cash first."

"See?" Jo said to nobody and everybody. "Institutional training. They could not beat it out of her with a whole hearing."

The words landed softer than the joke asked. Mira knew Jo meant to keep the room moving, but the hearing still sat on her skin like chemical residue. She had testified. They had fired her before the microphones cooled. She had walked out of Arbitration Hall with a Bureau inspector serving her the fine on the steps while the poor renter she had saved cried from relief and shame at the same time. There was no applause and no clean turn of history. There was just the city using paper to remind her what counted as legal speech.

Leonie watched her with the quick side-eye of people who had spent too much of life in offices. "You really said it there?"

"Enough of it."

"And now you're here."

"Now I'm here."

Leonie considered that, then nodded, filing Mira's answer where she'd file a correct intake response.

By noon the back room had filled to its workable limit. Steam hung in the fabric stacked along the walls. Tavi was tying off Leonie's stitch line when Lin brought in the man with the faithless hand and the girlfriend who looked angrier than he did.

The man smelled of anise liquor, fryer grease, and fresh blood. A bar napkin had been wrapped around his right hand so long it had pasted itself to the split knuckle underneath. His name, once Mira got it out of him, was Omar Becerra. The girlfriend planted herself by the wetbench and said hers was Yasmin Soto and she was only here because his hand had embarrassed them both.

"It keeps signing some woman's name," Yasmin said. "Not mine. Not his. He was closing tabs at the bar and wrote it on three receipts like he meant to marry the paper."

Omar glared at the floor. "It's my ex-wife's name."

"Then why'd you punch the dryer?" Tavi asked.

"Because the dryer laughed at me."

Tavi looked at Mira. "He's drunk."

"Obviously," Yasmin said. "He's also bleeding on Jo's towels."

From the front room, Jo called, "If he bleeds on my towels, he's paying linen rates."

Omar made the mistake of trying to flex the hand. The napkin darkened. Mira caught his wrist before he could hide it and peeled the paper free with saline. The split sat across the second knuckle, shallow but ugly, skin curled back like wet label stock. His fingers twitched under her hold, not pain alone. A writing cramp. The tendons at the base of the thumb kept loading and releasing around an absent pen.

"How much have you had?" she asked.

"Three shots and one argument."

"No memory flush," Tavi said at once. "Not with ethanol onboard. I'll clean the knuckle, maybe tape it. That's it."

Yasmin threw both hands up. "He came because you fix overlap."

"We do," Mira said. "We don't do guesswork with a soaked brain and call it medicine."

Omar muttered, "Told you."

Lin had already found a pencil. They set a detergent invoice on the stool beside him. "Write your name anyway."

"Why?"

"Because if it's nonsense, nonsense will keep the same shape."

That made Omar look at them for the first time. He sat because pain sat him. Mira steadied the invoice against his knee. He took the pencil in his right hand and printed `O`. Then `m`. Then the fingers changed their mind. The wrist softened. The thumb slid higher on the shaft. A looping capital `E` flowed onto the paper in a hand too graceful to belong to the rest of him, followed by three quick letters and the beginning of a surname before Omar swore and flung the pencil.

Yasmin did not say I told you. She did not need to. The look on her face had done that four times already somewhere else.

Tavi picked the pencil off the floor. "That could still be disinhibition. Booze turns grooves loud. Doesn't mean donor trace yet."

"It means something," Yasmin said.

"It means his hand remembers a script he doesn't," Lin said. "That isn't nothing."

Mira folded the invoice over the half-written name before Omar could stare himself sick at it. "Have you ever rented service frames? Banquet, hostess, anything that lives with a pen in the fingers?"

"Barback. Wedding cleanup. One holiday season in a hotel shell." He swallowed. "I print. Always have. My ex used to laugh because my block letters looked like a ransom note."

That mattered. It wasn't proof. It was a route.

"Here's the triage," Mira said. "Tavi closes the knuckle and splints the hand. I log the script event and the trigger. You come back sober tomorrow or the day after and do the test again. If it repeats clean, it graduates from drunk trick to evidence."

Yasmin looked like she wanted faster justice than the room could sell. Omar looked relieved and ashamed in equal measure.

"And if I don't come back?" he asked.

"Then the hand keeps writing without you," Mira said. "Your choice."

He let out one hard breath through his nose. "Tape it."

Tavi irrigated the split knuckle, swore when Omar jerked, and wrapped two fingers together with white splint tape. Mira wrote on the intake slip while the hand still trembled on Omar's thigh:

`OMAR BECERRA`

`right-hand motor script episode under ethanol; partial unfamiliar cursive signature`

`partner witness: repeats during drinking / no deep wash performed`

`return sober for replication before donor trace assigned`

She underlined the last line once. That was the care outcome. It wasn't a cure or certainty. It was a boundary that might keep the evidence from turning to sludge.

After Yasmin paid in six damp bills and a roll of quarters, the queue kept moving: the freight temp whose knees locked at gull cries, the busboy whose bargain jaw bit down on somebody else's wedding vows, the grandmother rental from Market South whose fingers drummed teenage rhythms on tabletops when she got bored. Lin kept the queue from curdling. Gabe handled payment, gossip, and the back buzzer, flattering people just enough that they forgot he was also watching for inspectors. Jo ran the laundromat like a border crossing.

Work replaced clock time. Mira trusted work more.

When she finally straightened, her lower back a strip of hot ache and her gloves dusted with dried sealant, Leonie was shrugging into her coat. The pinching motion in her hands had gone.

"If it comes back?" Mira asked.

"I write it down." Leonie touched the folded intake slip Mira had given her. "On paper."

"Good."

Leonie hesitated. "That thing about the orange page. You want me to remember more of it?"

"If it comes, yes."

"Feels ugly, remembering somebody else's trap."

Mira could still feel the raised seam of the scar through the swab in her glove. "It probably was somebody else's trap. That's why I want it."

Leonie nodded once and went out through the detergent aisle, shoulders set. Lin was already at the back door before she reached it, holding the latch with the practiced invisibility of a helper. At the front glass, Jo flipped the sign to CLOSED for one hour though voices still pressed from the waiting line and laundry kept spinning. The hours had been rearranged and the faces had shifted. It was the same work with less cover and more witnesses. Blue Hour looked less like a side job every day and more like a place the neighborhood would notice if it disappeared.

Mira pulled Leonie's paper slip back from the completed stack and copied two phrases onto a cleaner page.

`blue line first`

`orange page is a trap`

The letters looked less like notes than the beginning of something that would have to survive them.

* * *

Rain came in sideways that night and freckled the front window with canal grit. Jo sent the last real laundry out with a free scolding and locked the door. Gabe dragged the folding table closer to the back-room light, his left foot catching twice on the mat when he got tired enough to stop hiding the drag. Lin brought over the day's intake slips in clipped stacks. Tavi stripped his gloves and flexed his long fingers one by one, trying to unknot the hours out of them.

The room had the wrung-out intimacy of after-service kitchens. Damp heat clung to everything, the coffee had gone bitter on the burner, and everybody was past hungry and not yet stopped.

Mira spread the slips across the table. Leonie's phrases sat beside three other fragments from the past ten days, all written by different hands because Blue Hour had always jotted what mattered wherever paper existed: a receipt roll, the back of a detergent invoice, Jo's account notebook, even a food carton lid once, until the grease made the ink surrender.

`blue line first`

`don't sign the orange page`

`Stitch can fix a sling with no light`

`parking lot saints stealing fries`

Lin touched the last one with a fingernail. "That one is mine." They said it without claiming ownership exactly. More like they were pointing to a pressure bruise. "Or part of mine."

Mira lifted her head from the slips. Lin had taken off the delivery cap. Their hair at the temples was damp and flattened, and the notch in the left ear showed white under the light. "When did you get it?"

"This afternoon when the dryers changed cycle." Lin closed their eyes for a second, not dramatically, just to hold the thread. "I could smell old fryer grease and wet rope. Somebody was laughing with a mouth full of fries. Not rich-people weekend laughing. Tired laughing. The kind that spends itself on the way out."

Gabe leaned over the table. "Say the line again."

"Parking lot saints stealing fries."

"No, the other one."

Lin frowned in concentration. "Stitch can fix a sling with no light."

Gabe opened his mouth like he had a joke coming, then caught on something harder. He hooked two fingers under the edge of his brace and hauled himself a little straighter with a hiss.

"Jesus," he said. "No. I know that. Stitch wasn't a phrase. Stitch was a person."
