The company will preserve manufacturing records, donor-source maps, maintenance protocols, and all related service data. An independent forensic team will be appointed."

Voren wrote something on a yellow pad without visible reaction. Yaret's face became even more polite, which was how Mira knew he had not gotten the narrower order he wanted.

The chair continued. "The tribunal further finds that living overlap-bearing bodies presented in the record possess direct factual interests that cannot be adequately handled under ordinary defect language. However" - and every public bench tightened on that word - "this panel does not have statutory authority to declare full legal personhood or ownership transfer today. Until a higher determination is made, such individuals will be entered into the civic record as protected disputed claimants. They may not be destroyed, sold, or removed from review jurisdiction without leave of this tribunal."

Protected disputed claimants: that was the phrase. It hit Mira with the force of a door half-opened onto a room still on fire.

Relief went through the public benches first. Then confusion. Then anger as the category finished landing. It meant not pulp and not sale, but it still did not mean person. It meant being held in a better cage.

Lin sat beside her with both hands flat on the bench.

Chairwoman Beal kept reading. "CommonFrame is ordered to produce, within twenty-four hours, a continuity plan for maintenance obligations affecting currently leased and medically dependent bargain-body users. The Bureau will refrain from seizure of the specific witness known as Lin absent further order. Existing criminal and civil questions are reserved."

It was a partial shield, and it came with a crack already running through it.

Voren rose immediately. "CommonFrame objects to the characterization of claimant status and reserves all rights to seek emergency relief."

"Of course you do," Jo muttered.

Yaret stood a beat later. "The Bureau requests clarification that protected disputed status does not impede public-safety supervision."

Chairwoman Beal did not look impressed. "The order is plain. Supervision is not custody. We are done for today."

The gavel tap was light. Administrative power never needed theater if it believed paper would finish the job.

Noise broke loose at once. Reporters surged. The plant workers in the back started talking over each other. One of the clinic women shouted, "Not personhood?" and got shushed by three different people who were already calculating appeals. The officers at the door straightened but did not advance.

Mira turned to Lin. "Are you all right."

Lin's mouth moved, then settled. "I am in the record."

"Yes."

"I am also apparently disputed."

There was no graceful answer to that. Mira put a hand on the back of Lin's coat between the shoulder blades. Under the wool she could feel the living heat of them, and under that the body the city still thought it might sort correctly if given enough forms.

Tavi leaned over from the aisle. "Hey. Protected is not nothing."

"No," Lin said. "It's not."

The disappointment in the sentence was so exact it made Mira look away.

Celia was already collecting her papers when a reporter tried to corner her. She batted the microphone aside with the gloved hand and kept moving. For one second Mira considered letting her go without another word. Then she thought of the service portal, the maintenance maps, the people whose jobs and mobility sat inside bargain shells right this minute.

"Celia."

Celia stopped.

"If they trigger insolvency," Mira said, "what happens first."

"Access locks. Parts holdbacks. Deferred updates. They will call it review stabilization." Celia's voice was quiet enough that only their cluster could hear. "You need the maintenance maps before close of business."

"Do you have them."

"Not here."

"Can you get them."

Celia hesitated. Not from reluctance. From arithmetic. Mira could almost see her counting who still answered her calls, which passwords had been killed, how much of her own remaining shelter she was about to spend.

"Yes," Celia said at last. "If I move now."

Jo looked at her hard. "Then move."

Celia gave a brief nod and left through the side corridor without waiting to be thanked.

That was when Mira's pocket started vibrating.

It was not her phone but Gabe's old backup handset, the one Blue Hour clients still had the number for because nobody trusted central systems anymore. She pulled it out. The screen showed missed call after missed call, then a new voicemail notification and three texts arriving on top of one another.

`Portal says account unavailable. What does freeze mean?`

`My labor frame is due for tendon service tonight. No one answering.`

`They told me my body lease is under review and I can't clock in tomorrow. Please call.`

Tavi saw the screen over her shoulder and said, very softly, "Fuck."

Jo's own phone started going off in her tote. Then the donor-rights clinic woman's. Then one of the plant workers answered his and began swearing with the frightened concentration of a man hearing a machine fail while still standing in front of it.

The room that had come to witness a ruling was turning, in real time, into triage.

Lin flinched at the chorus of ringtones and fixed on Mira. "This is because of the freeze."

"This is because of the company," Mira said.

"The company is using the freeze."

"Yes."

The distinction mattered. It did not help.

By the time they pushed out through the lobby, the crowd on the steps had doubled. Someone shouted a question about personhood. Someone else shouted about jobs. Rain had thinned to cold mist, turning every coat dark. The camera crews surged toward Lin and then checked themselves at the expression on Mira's face. Over the noise, she could hear the harbor ferries blowing farther off, long flat notes moving through the wet air like warnings nobody had budgeted for.

Yaret came down the side stairs with two officers trailing him, his posture as immaculate as ever. He paused only long enough to say, "Protected disputed status is unstable. Keep your witness available."

Mira looked at him. "They aren't evidence you get to store."

"The order does not say otherwise."

"It says you don't take them."

"Today," Yaret said. "We will see what the next forum says."

He moved on before anger could become argument.

Lin watched him go. "He says forum the way some people say weather."

"Because he thinks weather is inevitable," Mira said.

Jo was already on the phone, voice sharpened into command. "No, listen to me. Don't let them tell you to pulp anything. I don't care what the portal says. Bring the paperwork and bring the body if it can travel. Yes, I know that's not an easy sentence. Move."

Tavi had his own phone out now, scrolling, swearing, dialing. "Service boards are down. Whole bargain portal is stuttering. Mira, if this cascades, people are going to start showing up wherever they think repair still exists."

Blue Hour was gone. The back room had been smashed, the wetbench burned down to salvage metal. There was no wherever left, only whoever.

Mira stood on the arbitration steps with the tribunal order dampening in her hand and understood the shape of the victory. They had forced the city to hear Lin as a living claim on the public record. They had put deliberate creation where CommonFrame wanted defect language. They had stopped the factory for a moment.

And now every cheap body built to keep poor people moving through other people's economies was about to be left in the rain with its maintenance withheld, its legal status contested, and its users told to wait for process.

Lin stepped close enough that their shoulder touched Mira's arm under the coat. "What do we do."

Down in the street, a tram bell clanged and somebody cursed and the cameras kept rolling because civic catastrophe was always good footage if it arrived with enough faces.

Mira looked at the missed calls again, at the order, at the crowd already starting to sort itself into the people asking questions and the people asking where to go. Blue Hour was a burned shell. Jo still had the laundry. For the next few hours, that would have to be enough. Then she folded the damp paper once and put it in the evidence sleeve with the ledger.

"We answer," she said.

It was the only sentence in her mouth that did not feel like a lie.


# Chapter 17

The tribunal win lasted until six-thirty that evening. After that, answering meant hearing the same recorded message from three phones at once: one on Jo's counter, one on speaker in Tavi's hand, one tinny and furious from somewhere in the line already thickening outside the laundry. Thank you for contacting CommonFrame client support. Due to insolvency proceedings and court-ordered asset preservation, all bargain-line service centers were temporarily unavailable. Maintenance obligations were suspended pending administrator review. Please monitor your account portal for updates.

Suspended, unavailable, review: they were the exact kind of clean little lies Mira had once been paid to hear and repeat with a level face. She knew where the bodies went inside those phrases. She knew what got renamed nonessential when a company was trying to make itself look dead in a way the court would find respectable. Flush kits. Skin sealant. Joint sleeves. Wash dampers. Every cheap ugly thing that kept bargain stock from swelling at the ports, shedding collagen at the knees, hearing three lives at once in the middle of a bus transfer. The parts were now assets. The people wearing them were not.

Jo had hauled the front shutter halfway up and jammed it there with a mop handle. Cold canal air dragged detergent smell and old extinguisher powder through the shop. The back room was still a blackened cavity, one wall bubbled from the raid fire, the floor uneven where the wetbench had died. Nobody sane could run repairs in there. Nobody sane would have tried. The line outside kept growing anyway, wrapping past the laundromat windows and into the produce stand next door where someone had started handing out bruised tangerines because waiting people would pass out faster on empty stomachs.

"If your problem is billing, I can't help you," Jo called, standing on an upside-down milk crate in her house cardigan and a church blouse printed with little blue saints she denied owning whenever anyone noticed it. Her silver braid swung against her back when she pointed. "If your leg is leaking, if your memory is cutting in and out, if your arm is stuck around somebody's throat, that is us. Pick one honest emergency and get in the right line."

That moved them better than kindness would have. Mira had a notebook open on the washer lid nearest the door. Her handwriting had gone back to claims-script neatness without asking her permission. Name, serial if known, symptom onset, user contract if any, last maintenance date if they remembered. She had stopped writing warranty status because there was no longer any point in recording a dead god.

The first man in front of her gave his name as Dario Velasquez. He smelled like fish scales and machine oil, and two fingernails on his right hand still carried a coat of gold glitter, chipped but stubborn, from his daughter's birthday the night before.

"Dock frame," he said. "Cheap one. I know. Don't look at me like that."

"I'm not looking at you like anything," Mira said.

"Good, because my wife already did." He swallowed and glanced back at the line as if shame might spread by eye contact. "My elbow locked on shift. Then it unlocked all at once and threw three crates into the water. Supervisor said get it serviced before I come back or don't come back. Portal ate my deposit and now it says account unavailable."

Mira touched the arm where the synthetic biceps narrowed toward the elbow seam. Heat had pooled under the skin. The flex sleeve clicked under her fingers like damp paper being folded wrong.

"How long since it started slipping?"

"Week, maybe. I worked around it."

Of course he had. People always worked around it until the machine made them too expensive to keep pretending. Mira wrote urgent. Tavi leaned over her shoulder, smelling of cold metal and the stale coffee he drank when sleep had lost the argument.

"Batch 8K bargain stevedore," he said. "Sleeve delamination. We can strap it for now, but if the tendon puckers he's dropping the whole forearm."

Dario went gray under the dock tan. "Dropping where."

"Off," Tavi said. "Not right this second if you keep your hands to yourself."

"Tavi."

"What? He asked."

Mira put her hand flat on the notebook to stop herself from tearing the page out. More people pressed at the half-open shutter. A woman with one borrowed knee braced both hands on the windowsill and tried not to scream as the joint buzzed. A teenage courier in a bargain sprint frame kept blinking hard, then turning his head at voices nobody else heard. On the sidewalk, somebody vomited pink wash buffer into the gutter. It ran around a cigarette butt and thinned toward the storm drain like watered paint.

Lin moved through them with a paper cup full of tongue depressors and a box of old transit pencils. They had borrowed Gabe's yellow rain shell, the one with a missing snap at the throat, and the hood kept slipping over one of their mismatched eyes. Every time they pushed it back, they looked a little more annoyed at clothing in general.

"Write your name if your hands work," they told the courier, steady and careful. "If they don't, tell me and I'll write it. Don't make up the date of your last wash. We need the real one."

"I don't know the real one."

"Then say that. It helps."

The kid stared at them, breathing too fast. "Are you a nurse?"

Lin considered the question with irritating seriousness. "No. I'm good at not making people lie when they're scared."

That, apparently, was enough. He handed over his wristband.

Mira's own phone started vibrating across the washer. She looked down and saw a number from the plaintiff coalition, the legal team that had spent the last forty-eight hours explaining to every camera in Cordera why the tribunal mattered and why CommonFrame could not be allowed to bury the ruling in delay. She let it buzz. The city screens over the tram stop across the street were already carrying the bankruptcy headline under footage of the courthouse steps.

COMMONFRAME FILES FOR EMERGENCY INSOLVENCY PROTECTION.

SERVICE OBLIGATIONS UNDER REVIEW.

EXECUTIVES DENY RETALIATORY SHUTDOWN.

The line outside the laundromat read the screens in ugly little waves. Some people cursed. Some looked relieved for half a second because bankruptcy sounded, if you did not know the contracts, like the company might finally be in trouble. Then the relief curdled as the practical meaning worked through the crowd. A bargain body was not a sweater. You could not hang it in the closet until the courts sorted themselves out. People were inside these things now. People were making rent with them, crossing the city in them, fucking in them, lifting with them, hiding in them, surviving in them.

One of Jo's regulars from the corner bodega, Suri Nwoko, limped in on a caregiving frame with her pink salon apron still tied over her coat. She had been arguing about coffee prices with Jo for three years and remained convinced every dark roast in the district was a personal insult.

"Tell me you have seal gel," she said without greeting. "The ankle split on the bus and now my sock is wet in a way that feels expensive."

She hiked the hem. A seam above the borrowed ankle had opened like a bad cut in soft fruit. Clear fluid slicked down into her shoe.

Jo looked at it once. "Sit before I make you sit."

Suri sat. "I hate when you're right."

"That must make your days long."

They got a laugh out of her, which was useful because the laughter kept her from looking too hard at the fluid pooling in the tongue of her sneaker. Mira added her to the list. Tavi was already shaking his head.

"We're out of the cheap gel," he said. "I used the last tube on a shoulder port at dawn."

"We had six tubes yesterday."

"Yesterday the company still wanted these people billable."

Mira looked up at him. "What do you mean, still wanted."

He scrubbed a hand over his mouth, leaving a gray streak of machine dust along his cheek. Two unmatched gloves hung out of his back pocket like dead animals by the wrists. "I mean those parts are behind serialized locks now. Service depots will close first, inventory freeze second. They'll say they're preserving assets for creditors. What they're preserving is resale. Gel, sleeves, dampers, all of it."

"Can they do that with active users still in line?"

Tavi gave her a look that was half pity, half accusation for having once worn a badge. "Mira. You know they can."

She did. She knew the clause numbers too, knew how often insolvency language sat buried under user responsibility, knew the phrases that turned catastrophe into accepted commercial risk. She had spent years telling poorer clients that what had happened to them fell under exclusions. She could feel those old scripts in her mouth now, rancid and ready.

Outside, a man started shouting that the CommonFrame service center on Harrow had chain on the doors and a paper notice behind glass. Somebody else shouted back that the South Quay center had private security and no staff. The shouting spread, then thinned into the rougher noise of panic trying not to use the word panic.

Jo came down from the crate. "Enough." Her nicotine laugh snapped once, not because anything was funny but because her throat did that when she was angry. "We are not fixing a district from the front of my washers."

"We don't have anywhere else," Mira said.

"Wrong. We have somewhere else that's cold and filthy and belongs to a city that already abandoned it, which makes it spiritually perfect."

She was looking past Mira now, out toward the canal road and the slice of gray water beyond the roofs.

Tavi followed the look first. "Jo."

"Don't Jo me. The old ferry terminal has drains, benches, a loading ramp, and enough square footage that people can panic without breathing on my neck."

"It's locked."

"So are rich people's wine cabinets. Keys are one category of obstacle."

Mira felt the answer land before she had the energy to argue with it. The terminal was bigger, colder, closer to open water. Wind came off the harbor there with salt in it and nowhere to hide. It also had old ticket counters, loading bays, public toilets, concrete floors you could bleach, and ceiling hooks left from a time when the city still pretended transit for workers counted as infrastructure. It was twenty minutes on foot from Brine Court, downhill enough for carts, close enough that people knew the route.

Her phone kept vibrating. Legal team again. Then an unknown number. Then Gabe.

She answered him first.

"Tell me you already heard," he said. Pain made his voice brighter than usual, as if it had been polished against a rough edge. "Dock boards are lighting up. People are stuck in lift frames with no support and everyone's acting surprised the company would pull the plug on poor people first."

"We're moving."

"Good. Because there are about forty people on the neighborhood channel asking where to go, and if I answer 'the sidewalk outside Jo's' one more time my thumb is going to detach."

She could picture him in the Brine Court kitchen chair, brace under his sweatshirt, left foot dragging every time he got up, still pretending he was more useful if nobody saw him wince. "Can you get to the terminal?"

"I can get to a chair at the terminal."

"That's enough."

Jo was already barking assignments.

"You," she told Dario, pointing at his good arm. "Carry those folding tables."

"My elbow."

"Use the other one. Congratulations on your surviving elbow."

To Suri: "Take every clean sheet you see and stop choosing by color."

To Lin: "Bring the notebooks, the registry, and the kettle. People tell the truth easier with hot cups in hand."

Lin nodded once and vanished into the hallway.

Mira tore three pages from the notebook, rewrote the urgent list, and shoved the rest under her coat. Victory had lasted one night, maybe less than that. It had lasted long enough for headlines, not long enough for maintenance. She looked at the line, at the wet shoes, the swollen ports, the hands people kept flexing as if obedience could come back by command.

"Everybody who can walk, we're moving to the ferry terminal," she shouted. "If you can't walk, tell us now. If you're carrying a body that won't hold upright, tell us now. We are not leaving anyone here to wait for a company that already left."

The shouting broke into movement. Dario hooked the folding tables under his good arm. The courier with the stray voices grabbed a cart handle before Lin could ask. Panic was still there, but it had its face turned downhill now.

The first cart hit the slope with a squeal of bad wheels. Somewhere in Mira's coat pocket the legal team reached message number four.

Mira did not listen.

***

The old ferry terminal smelled of brine, pigeon shit, rust, and the damp mineral cold of concrete that had not decided whether it belonged to weather or building. Wind came in under the warped doors and traveled the whole length of the waiting hall. It carried gull noise with it and worried the old route maps still bolted above the ticket windows, islands and schedules from a public future that had died so gradually nobody had marked the date.

Jo got them inside with bolt cutters borrowed from the produce vendor's nephew and an expression that suggested municipal property was mostly a rumor rich people told one another. Mira came through the loading hall behind the second laundry cart and found the chain in two pieces on the ground, Jo directing traffic like she had owned the terminal since birth.

"Triage by the ticket windows," she said. "Repairs in the old waiting room. Anyone who faints goes flat against the east wall because that's where the draft is least murderous. If you piss in a corner when there's a functioning toilet fifty feet away, I will remember your face until I die."

People were already moving before Jo finished. A woman in a grocery visor wrestled a crate toward the ticket windows. Somebody dragged a fan across the terrazzo by its cord. Jo pointed, and the room found its next job.

They built the place out of whatever the neighborhood could carry fast. Folding tables from the bodega. Plastic bins of gauze and old tubing from three closed nail salons and one veterinary office that asked no questions as long as Jo promised a future favor. Extension cords from a church basement. Two industrial fans, which nobody wanted until Tavi pointed out the reek of open port infections would turn the hall unworkable by afternoon. Someone dragged in a coffee urn the size of a toddler. Someone else brought blankets that smelled of cedar closet and cat.

Mira moved through it with her notebook and the stupid, steady clarity that came when terror had too many tasks attached to it to be called terror. She taped paper signs over the ticket windows. INTAKE. URGENT. PORT CLEANING. MEMORY BREAKS. The last one had been Lin's suggestion, precise and bleak. Not everyone showing up was leaking or splitting. Some were hearing borrowed fathers call them by wrong names in the middle of crossing streets. Some had woken in bargain stock with other people's church songs trapped in their teeth. Some were overlaps who had watched the tribunal and then watched the company abandon them anyway.

Gabe arrived in a cab paid for by somebody he refused to name, swearing at the driver, the curb, and his own left leg with equal sincerity. He lowered himself into a folding chair like a man being folded by someone else, one hand pressed against the lumbar brace under his sweatshirt. He had brought a thermos of burnt coffee, a bundle of charging cables, and a sandwich bag full of hotel pens he had apparently been stealing for years in preparation for exactly this kind of civic emergency.

"Dispatch desk," he said through his teeth. "Don't argue."

Mira opened her mouth.

"Don't. I can sit and yell. It's one of my better skills."

So she put him by the entrance with two notebooks, a power strip, and the neighborhood channel pulled up on his cracked tablet. Within ten minutes he had built a crude routing system for who needed immediate hands-on repair, who could wait for strapping and instructions, who needed bed space, and who had a working vehicle large enough to transport somebody whose bargain frame had gone rigid at the spine. He flirted with old ladies, cursed at dock foremen, and kept slipping one cough drop after another into his mouth because pain made him want sugar and he was trying to save the coffee for later.

Lin took the middle ticket window. They had a stack of index cards, the registry ledger open to one side, and a ceramic sugar bowl from Jo's apartment full of paper clips. Every frightened person who came to the window got the same unnerving attention, as if Lin could not imagine wasting even one of them.

"Tell me what your body did first," they asked.

Lin was not asking what had happened, what contract line had failed, or whether the client had used the frame according to terms. Lin was asking what your body did first. The question pulled people down into sequence and sensation. Mira watched it work again and again. A woman in a labor-assist frame said, "My left hand forgot the broom and kept reaching for a rosary that wasn't there." A man in a courier shell said, "My knees started hearing applause." A sex worker with bitten-off black polish and a wool cap over one ear said, "The port at my neck burns every time somebody calls me honey, and I know how stupid that sounds." Lin wrote all of it. None of them laughed.

Tavi claimed the old waiting room at the back and turned it into a repair bay out of insult, wire, and habit. He swore at the terminal's dead overhead lights until a former ferry electrician named Remy Santiago, who had a little cloud tattooed behind one ear and glitter worked into the cracks of his knuckles, got half the room powered through an exterior maintenance box with a knife and a prayer so profane Mira almost wanted to write it down. Tables lined up under the revived strips of light. Old ferry benches became instrument trays. Someone found a wheeled luggage rack and it became transport for clients who could not bear weight.

The woman in the wool cap came through to repair before Mira had finished taping the last sign straight. Her card said Nessa Ordonez, Pearl Rooms, cash nightly. Up close the burn at her neck was not a metaphor. Someone had hidden the bargain port under a strip of black lace glued down to make the shell look less medical, more expensive. The lace had fused into the skin where cheap heat built all morning. Every swallow clicked.

"It starts when they call me honey," Nessa said. Her voice had gone low and flayed. "Not sweetheart. Not baby. Honey. Then the back of my tongue goes numb and I throw up in my mouth."

Mira cut the lace away. Adhesive took the top layer of skin with it. Underneath, the port lip bulged purple and wet around an aftermarket throat-smoother no bigger than a thumbnail.

Tavi leaned in with the penlight between his teeth. "Some bastard stacked a comfort-response patch over a nightlife larynx rig because the house wanted you sweeter for less money. How many shifts did you work through this?"

"Three."

"Of course you did."

They got her flat on a bench before her knees gave. One of the university clinic volunteers held the light and tried very hard not to breathe through their mouth. Tavi slit the swollen seam. A clot of amber gel and stringy pink wash slid down Nessa's clavicle and into the towel Mira had bunched under her shoulder. The smell was perfume, salt, and the first sweet turn of rot. Nessa bit her fist, then apologized into it because people trained to sell access did not always stop performing just because somebody was draining pus out of their neck.

"I've got rent by dawn," she said when she could speak again. "I know how this looks."

"It looks infected," Mira said. "Don't waste breath making it noble."

Tavi threaded a child-size feed tube through the inflamed channel, rigged a drain bag from IV scrap, and taped the whole ugly assembly to Nessa's shoulder with waterproof strips. "This buys you twelve hours and maybe your airway," he said. "It doesn't buy you another shift."

Nessa gave a short laugh that turned into a cough. When she tried the word honey under her breath, her voice still snagged low and wrong, but the port stopped sparking under the skin. Gabe found her a cot at the union hall, a charger, and a booker willing to move two of her clients instead of blacklisting her for vanishing. She cried harder over the canceled bookings than she had over the knife.

Nessa had barely cleared the back hall before the line outside doubled and the city started noticing in the only language cities use when poor people solve an official failure in public. Two local feeds sent camera crews. A council aide arrived, looked at the crowd, promised to "escalate resource conversations," and left before Jo could assign him mop duty. Three volunteers from the university clinic came in with masks and the stiff, eager terror of people whose tuition had not prepared them to smell opened port rot. Mira put them on sterilizing tools and saying comforting things with accuracy.

Then Celia Rusk walked in carrying CommonFrame's afterlife in a rolling suitcase with a broken handle.

Mira saw her first as a disturbance in the line, the small involuntary widening people made around expensive wool and managerial posture even after both had plainly had a bad morning. Celia's coat was good cashmere under harbor grit, one cuff dark with sleet where it had dragged. Her silver hair had flattened on one side from sleep or something close to it. In one arthritic hand she carried a leather folio fat with printouts and old-school storage wafers. In the other she gripped the useless half-extended handle of the suitcase like she would rather sprain her wrist than admit it no longer telescoped.

Jo looked up from assigning blankets. "Well. The mausoleum has sent flowers."

Celia took in the room before answering: the intake cards, the blankets, the smell, the way people had already started trusting the ticket windows more than any official desk. Her face changed only at the edges. Truth always showed there first.

"CommonFrame terminated corporate housing at nine this morning," she said. Moderate voice, complete sentences, as if the terminal were a conference room and she refused to hand panic the chair. "My access survived long enough to pull archived maintenance maps. Not executive systems. The old service schematics. Batch weaknesses, manual override sites, substitutions if proprietary parts are unavailable."

Tavi laughed once, with no humor in it. "How generous of the sinking ship to cough up a diagram."

"I didn't bring it for your gratitude."

"Then why are you here."

The answer came a beat too fast for her usual poise. "Because the company card that paid for my apartment no longer works, because I know what those bodies will do without support, and because you can't improvise your way through a district-scale maintenance collapse on spite alone."

Tavi's mouth closed for exactly two seconds.

"Watch me."

Mira stepped between them before the room could decide it wanted a fight more than a triage site. Up close Celia smelled faintly of wool, printer toner, and the ginger candies she kept in her coat pocket for a stomach she never publicly admitted went bad under stress. The folio in her hand was damp at the edge where rain had reached it.

"What kind of maps," Mira said.

Celia set the folio on the ticket counter and opened it with hands that stiffened when she spread them. Inside were printed service trees, serial range tables, notes from discontinued maintenance bulletins, hand-marked annotations in dark green ink. Mira recognized one of Celia's expensive pens from the smear on the margin. She had written these herself or corrected somebody else's lies into usefulness.

"Bargain lines only," Celia said. "The premium clients have lawyers and concierge medicine. They will land on their feet or on someone else's. These are the bodies that won't."

Jo snorted. "A revelation."

"No," Celia said, and for a moment the polish cracked enough for old hunger to show through. "An accounting."

A stretcher improvised from a door slab came through the hall before anybody could say more. Suri was on it now, jaw clenched so hard Mira could see the muscles flutter. The split at her ankle had widened. Clear fluid had turned pink at the edges.

"She took three steps and the seal went," Lin called from intake. "She said her calf feels full of bees."

Tavi bent to look. Celia moved to his side without asking permission. He almost told her to fuck off. Mira saw the words gather. Then Celia leaned closer to the ankle seam and said, "Do not flush from the lateral port if this is serial group K-12 through K-19. They reversed the collars after a lawsuit in East Ferry. You will drive gel directly into the sheath and lose the foot."

Tavi froze, then swore anyway because she was right and he knew she was right before he checked. He wiped the seam, found the serial cluster stamped half under the skin fold, and shot Celia a look so hostile it counted as acceptance.

"Fine," he said. "Hold the light."

Celia held the light. Her arthritic fingers trembled around the handle only once. Suri, flat on the door slab, looked up at Mira and said through gritted teeth, "If that woman is who I think she is, and if she saves my ankle, I'm going to be extremely annoyed on principle."

"Stay annoyed," Mira said. "It keeps blood pressure up."

Tavi cinched the manual seal and color came back to Suri's toes. Celia stood over him reading from an old service bulletin, flat as office dictation, while Mira caught one note in the margin about East Ferry payouts and felt her teeth set. Pink fluid slicked her gloves. Suri bit a towel and kicked once hard enough to jar the slab. When it was over, she started crying from the release of it and apologized three times in a row because class shame was often louder than pain.

Jo handed her a cup of coffee thick with sugar. "You can cry in my terminal. It's one of the permitted fluids."

After that, nobody told Celia to get out. Tavi shoved the flashlight back into her hand when he needed it. Jo did not thank her. Mira did not either. But Celia stayed by the repair bay, useful as a clamp.

Once Lin's intake cards had outgrown the sugar bowl and the drains by the bathrooms were running pink, the terminal had its own ugly order. Urgent repairs in the back. Intake at the windows. Port cleanings near the bathrooms because the drains worked. Gabe at dispatch sending people to borrowed couches, church basements, one union hall whose steward hated CommonFrame more than he feared liability. Remy kept the lights alive with a face of concentrated insult. Lin's pile of intake cards rose higher and higher, clipped into categories nobody in city government had yet imagined would need names.

Outside, the queue bent along the seawall. Inside, the old departure board carried no routes at all, only taped signs and Mira's increasingly blunt handwriting.

NO OFFICIAL MAINTENANCE AVAILABLE HERE EITHER.

YES, WE KNOW THAT IS THE PROBLEM.

If you brought blankets, leave them by the east wall.

If you brought reporters, take them back out.

***

Darkness blackened the clerestory windows, and the camera crews learned to frame the room tight: Jo at the ticket windows, Lin's index cards, the cracked CommonFrame logo on the van, just enough ruin behind everybody to make the footage feel civic. The rest kept spilling out of shot. Bleach lifted old piss from the concrete every time somebody sloshed a bucket. In bay three a woman kept reciting bus stops that belonged to somebody else's childhood. Black-market tubing hung from a municipal handrail beside Celia's service manuals and a coffee urn the size of a toddler. Television could manage villains, witnesses, verdicts. It had nowhere to put a room like this.

Mira had not sat down in six hours when the coalition lawyer found her.

Asha Belen wore a white raincoat she never fully buttoned and had a habit of chewing one thumbnail flat while she read. Tonight the coat was speckled with terminal grime and camera dust. She stepped around two sleeping clients on the floor and caught Mira near the old snack kiosk where they were storing sterile wraps. A woman in a green fishery parka stood half a pace behind her with a grocery tote looped around one wrist so tight the handles had bitten red grooves into the skin.

"I've been calling you all day," she said.

"I noticed."

"There is a bankruptcy hearing at nine tomorrow. We need you, Lin, and at least one of the donor families visible. Tomas Vale's sister finally agreed to come. If LM-304C ties cleanly enough to her brother's file and the company doesn't get to define this as unfortunate market disruption instead of retaliatory abandonment, I can walk a blood relative into court instead of another abstraction. Otherwise the administrator liquidates parts stock by creditor class and we spend six months filing motions while people disappear."

Mira kept counting packs as Asha spoke, thumb sweeping the paper wrappers into stacks of ten because if she stopped her hands would start listening too hard to the coughing, the gulls, the metal bowls. "I know."

"Do you? Because the feeds are already shifting. They love the terminal, by the way. They love poor people helping each other in a picturesque ruin. It lets every official in the city act like community resilience is a substitute for duty."

Mira looked at her then. Asha's lipstick had worn off hours ago. There was a coffee stain on her sleeve and one earring missing, which made her look younger and more dangerous than the courthouse version of herself.

"You want me on a dais while this place fills up."

"I want the stock unfrozen and the maintenance depots opened under court supervision."

"You want a story a judge can process."

"Yes," Asha said, too smart to deny it. "Because judges don't rule on smell and suffering. They rule on records."

The woman in the green parka still had not offered her name. She looked past Asha toward the camera lights by the seawall and said, "I said I'd come talk. I didn't say I'd let the city turn my brother into harbor footage."

From the intake window Lin called Mira's name, not loudly, just once, and Mira was already moving through the hall.

A shuttle van had backed up to the loading ramp. The logo on its side was a CommonFrame service mark half-scraped away in a hurry. Two frightened attendants in disposable coats were unloading bodies and trying not to meet anyone's eyes. One of them, a young man with a silver ring through one eyebrow and a cartoon dinosaur pin on his collar, said, "South Quay closure. They told us to transfer unresolved cases to municipal review. Municipal review said there isn't one."

"How many?" Mira asked.

"Nine in the van, maybe twelve more if we go back." He swallowed. "They shut the doors while people were still in chairs."

This was what the tribunal order bought by sundown: a half-scraped service van, attendants dumped on a loading ramp, bodies still warm from chairs the company had abandoned. Mira could feel the folded order in her coat pocket, light as receipt paper and just as useless against the stretchers coming down.

Tavi and Celia were already at the van. Lin stood by the ramp with the registry ledger in both hands. Their face had gone very still. Asha came in on Mira's heels. Tomas Vale's sister stopped at the edge of the loading bay, close enough to see and still one step from bolting.

"This one," Lin said.

On the second stretcher lay an overlap body Mira recognized from the registry sketches, not because she had met him before but because one of the living donor traces had been oddly specific. The left ear had a nick through the upper ridge, healed wrong. The rib scar curved pale under the gown. The registry note from chapter thirteen had read: probable donor chain to Tomas Vale, fishery debt resale, no informed consent, living. The overlap on the stretcher looked about thirty and no age at all. His eyes were open but not tracking. Every few seconds his right hand tapped twice against the rail as if finishing a sentence somewhere else.

The woman in the parka made a low sound that was almost swallowed by the gulls. "He tore that ear on a crab pot when he was twelve," she said. "My mother made him wear a shower cap to bed for a week because the pillow kept sticking to it."

"Name?" Mira asked the attendant.

"In system he's listed as salvage cognitive instability, batch LM-304C. No active user. He started seizing when they cut maintenance. We gave him one emergency dampener from the crash cart but that's done."

Tavi had his fingers at the nape port already. "Port's cooking. Lattice temp is up. He needs a deep flush and a manual reset."

Celia's mouth tightened. "If you deep flush on this series, you're going to shear whatever unstable overlap structure is still readable. The maintenance bulletins were explicit. It saves the frame and compromises mnemonic trace."

Asha was beside Mira now. Mira heard her inhale before she spoke. "Wait. This is one of the traceable bodies?"

Lin nodded once.

"Then we need documentation before intervention. Photos, serial verification, maybe a court monitor if I can reach one. If we preserve this condition and tie it to the donor file, it strengthens the argument that these are people being abandoned as property."

Tavi looked up from the port. He had reached the point in fatigue where his humor burned off and left only the blunt metal underneath. "If we preserve this condition, he's dead or blank in twenty minutes."

"Not dead," Celia said automatically, then stopped because the distinction sounded monstrous even to her.

Lin looked from Asha to the stretcher. Their fingers tightened on the ledger hard enough to whiten the skin over the knuckles.

"Will he know us after," they asked Celia.

Celia did not perform false comfort. "I don't know."

"Will he know anything if we wait?"

"Probably less."

Tomas Vale's sister had gone paper-white. She kept staring at the overlap body and then at the camera crews beyond the open doors as if both were weather she might still outrun. "If he is him, I can say that in court," she said. "But if my face lands on the feeds beside you cutting him open in an unlicensed terminal, the packing plant walks me out before lunch. I've already got a garnishment taking half my check."

Asha blew out a breath. "I understand the urgency. I'm not asking you to be cruel for optics. I'm asking you to think one move ahead. If the evidence degrades, the company says contamination. They say rogue repair. They say your terminal is where the facts got ruined."

"They were always going to say that," Tavi said.

"Yes, and I'm trying to stop them from winning with it."

The overlap on the stretcher made a sound then, small and wet and frightened. His eyes focused for half a second on the terminal ceiling.

"Boat's late," he whispered. "Tell my mother the blue bucket's under the sink."

Tomas Vale's sister put her free hand over her mouth. "That was under her kitchen sink for twenty years," she said, words leaking through her fingers. "He kept bait shrimp in it even after she told him not to."

For one useless second Mira saw a blue plastic bucket shoved under a kitchen sink, wedged beside a rusted pipe and a bag of onions. That stupid domestic image hit harder than any tribunal slogan. It had come through all the grinding intact. The hand tapped the rail twice again.

Yaret Sanz arrived in the middle of that silence, escorted only by the weather and his own capacity to appear exactly where official trouble thickened. His dark coat was beaded with harbor mist. Damp marked the cuffs of his trousers. He carried a waterproof document sleeve and no visible backup, which was either restraint or theater.

People noticed him fast. The room's noise changed shape around his presence. It was not quieter. It was sharper.

"Inspector," Jo said from three yards away. "If you brought disinfectant, you can stay. If you brought paper, line the bird cages."

Yaret ignored the invitation and the insult with equal care. His gaze settled on the van, the stretchers, Celia, the open folio of maintenance notes on a bench. When he spoke, his voice was soft enough that everyone nearby leaned closer instead of away.

"I am here to inform you that these premises are not licensed for tissue repair," he said. "Any invasive intervention performed here may compromise standing evidence in the bankruptcy and tribunal continuations. Ms. Alves, you understand that."

Mira looked at him over the stretcher. "Do you have a licensed alternative?"

"The city is assessing emergency capacity."

"That means no."

"It means the proper channels are being established."

Jo laughed in his face, a bright ugly smoker's bark. "The proper channels are in a van outside drooling on themselves."

Yaret's eyes shifted to the overlap body. "That case in particular should be preserved pending review."

"Preserved how?" Mira said. "On ice."

"Stabilized without alteration if possible."

Tavi straightened. "If possible left fifteen minutes ago."

Asha stepped in before the argument could turn into a crowd sport. "Inspector, if the city wants preservation, then the city can place a monitor here now and release emergency stock."

"That authority is not mine."

"Funny how the authority to stop people is always somebody's and the authority to keep them alive belongs to a committee."

Mira barely heard the rest. The skin around the nape port had gone from pink to boiled-meat red. The fingers kept tapping the rail. Lin's face had gone blank with concentration. Celia's charts lay open on the bench, dampening at the corners, full of override sites and substitutions that would turn tomorrow's hearing into a swamp of rogue-repair allegations. CommonFrame would say unregulated salvage. The judge might nod along. The body on the stretcher would still cook if they waited for cleaner facts.

Lin answered before anyone else could.

"If you keep him unreadable on purpose," they said to Asha, to Yaret, to the room, "then you're still using him."

No one answered. Even the loading-ramp noise seemed to pull back for a beat.

Mira felt something settle inside her, not peace, nothing that pretty. It felt more like the click of a latch when a door finally gives up pretending it might swing both ways.

"Tavi," she said. "Do the flush."

Asha shut her eyes for one second. When she opened them she was angry, but not at Mira alone. Maybe Mira was not even most of what she was angry at. "Then I need everything documented as it happens."

"Fine," Mira said. "Document while helping."

She turned to Yaret. "You can witness or leave. If you want standing evidence preserved, start by preserving the person."

For the first time since Mira had known him, something almost like irritation passed visibly over his face. It vanished fast.

"This choice will complicate your case."

"This is my case."

Then there was no time for him at all.

They moved fast because fast was what the body required. Tavi opened the nape port. The smell came first, hot plastic under skin, then the slick shine of overheating gel. Celia read the override sequence from the maintenance chart, every number clean, every instruction stripped of corporate varnish now that it served actual hands. Mira held the shoulders and watched the monitor spike. Lin took the body's hand when the tremor started. Asha, to her credit, started recording serials, times, symptoms, every detail she had just begged them not to alter.

"Flush line," Tavi said.

Mira passed it.

"Not that one," Celia snapped. "Blue collar, not red. Red feeds sheath."

They switched. Gel clouded through the line, first pink, then a grayish milk threaded with black particulate where the cooked residue came loose. The overlap body arched once so hard Mira felt the force of it in her own wrists. His mouth opened on a sound with no room for language in it. Lin did not let go.

"You're at the terminal," they said, bent close. "You're at the water. Stay where the sound is."

In claims, Mira used to get clear the moment a body turned into a code. She could feel that old habit reaching for him now, wanting salvage cognitive instability, batch LM-304C, anything but the hand Lin was holding. Salt wind kept lifting the drape against her wrist. Asha called out timestamps. Yaret stood five feet away and contributed nothing. The old trick would have made the record cleaner. Mira let it die there.

"Temp's down," Tavi said.

"Again," Celia said. "Short pulse. Do not full-clear him unless you want a breathing blank."

Mira braced harder. Her gloves were slick. Hot gel slid over her thumb and cooled in the draft. Around them the terminal kept running. Someone laughed by intake. Someone else vomited in a bucket. Gabe was arguing with a driver about wheelchair space. Jo ordered three volunteers to boil more water and stop gawking if they were not going to become useful.

The second pulse took. The body on the stretcher jerked, coughed, and then sucked in a raw, surprised breath that sounded like somebody arriving late to his own life.

His eyes opened. For a second there was nothing organized in them. Then they found Lin's face.

"Blue bucket," he said, weaker now. "Under the sink."

"Okay," Lin said. Their own voice shook once and steadied. "We'll write it down."

Celia exhaled through her nose. Tavi sagged at the shoulders without fully stopping. Asha recorded the recovery time with the flattened stub of her chewed thumbnail pressed against the pen barrel, fury and relief fighting to a draw on her face.

Yaret slid the document sleeve back under his arm, then turned just enough that the nearest camera could hear him without his ever having to acknowledge it directly.

"For the record," he said, "the Bureau cannot authenticate tissue evidence altered through unauthorized intervention at this site."

At that, Tomas Vale's sister flinched hard enough to knock her tote against the rail. She looked at Yaret, at the open port, at the bucket of gray wash on the floor, and then at the camera light whitening the ramp.

"I can't do this on a clip they're going to call salvage," she said.

Asha caught at her sleeve. "If you leave now, I lose the cleanest witness line I've got."

"Then lose it," the woman said, already backing away. Fear had stripped her voice down to the bone. "I can't lose the job too."

Asha let go too late. The green parka was already halfway down the loading ramp, one hand tight around the grocery tote, the other dragging the hood up against the camera lights. She never looked back.

The loss landed with the clean force of arithmetic. No sister. No neat donor line. No one a bankruptcy judge could mistake for a respectable victim and therefore briefly decide to care about.

Mira stripped off one glove and reached for the next intake card.

"Note whatever you need," she said. "Then tell your city where to find us."

Outside, reporters were still clustered by the seawall hoping for a sentence that could fit under footage. Mira could hear the faint mechanical chirp when cameras reset focus. She imagined the version of herself Asha wanted on those steps tomorrow: composed, wounded, legible to power. Maybe that version would still be useful. Maybe she would even appear, if there was an hour to spare and someone else to hold pressure on the next opened seam.

But the terminal had made its demand, and now the price of answering it was walking down the ramp in a borrowed parka. Not purity. Not symbolism. Maintenance. The ugly, repetitive labor of keeping people from being abandoned by the systems that had already profited from them.

She wrote a new heading across the top of the intake page in letters large enough to be read from three feet away.

CARE FIRST.

Then she called for the next body.


# Chapter 18

The cameras had gone by then. The line was still there. By midwinter the old ferry terminal smelled less like abandonment than broth, saline, wet wool, and the hot-metal tang of space heaters working above their pay grade. Salt had eaten lacework into the ticket grilles. The terrazzo floor stayed cold no matter how many rugs Jo scavenged and taped down. But the place no longer sounded empty. It clicked and coughed and argued. Gabe had coaxed one strip of the dead departure board back to life, and every few minutes the plastic letters flipped from INTAKE to SUTURES or BEDS or PORT CLEANING with the same hard clatter that used to send commuters toward the bay. Mira liked the noise more than she trusted herself to admit. Every clatter of the board sounded like somebody planning to come back tomorrow.

She stood behind the old information counter with a clipboard, triage stamps hanging from a shoelace at her wrist, and watched the morning line organize itself into something almost civilized. Wet coats steamed by the doorway. A man in a rented loading frame tried not to limp on a knee that belonged to three different shifts of other people. A student in a bargain nightlife shell kept pressing two fingers to her throat because every time Jo laughed the borrowed larynx answered in a baritone she swore was not hers. Somebody had left a sack of turnips by the fee jar. Somebody else had left twelve dollars, two tram cards, and a zip bag of sterile clamps. The money tin and the produce crate sat side by side now, cash for gauze pressed up against turnips for soup, both of them a little short.

Gabe occupied the stool inside the glass dispatch booth like a man who had been born to gossip through cracked municipal equipment. His lumbar brace made a blocky shelf under his sweatshirt, and the orange knit cap one of the night volunteers had made him sat crooked over one ear. He had a harbor-radio earpiece hooked to his collar even though half the terminal could hear him without electronics. A grease pencil rode behind his ear beside a hotel matchbook he kept for luck and never used. When he saw Mira checking the board, he raised his coffee in salute.

"Morning, claims girl. You're behind."

"I'm on time."

"The room isn't. Port leak in coat four. Memory carryover in coat six. One ankle split. One panic attack pretending to be heart trouble. Also a man at the back who says his borrowed jaw won't stop humming church music, and I told him that's triage for God, not us."

Mira glanced past him. "Did you give God a cot number?"

"I gave God blanket C and told him to wait his turn."

Jo ran the soup station out of the shuttered snack bar with the severity of a dock foreman and the timing of a comic who refused stage lighting. Her silver braid was looped up with a clothespin because she had lost the good clip again, and her detergent-dried hands moved between broth tubs, pill organizers, and the donation ledger without ever looking rushed. She wore the same slate shoes built for standing twelve hours at a time and still found energy to insult anyone who shuffled.

"If you're bleeding, say bleeding," she called toward the line. "If you're not bleeding, stop leaning on my table like you're auditioning for widowhood. Mira, your brother gave soup to the man with the active leak again."

"It was one cup," Gabe said.

"It was one cup into my blanket inventory."

That got a laugh out of three people who had come in too tense to look at each other when they hit the door. Jo took the laugh as payment and kept ladling.

At the table nearest the windows, Celia Rusk sat under two blankets and a good wool coat that had once been expensive enough to resent rain. The cuffs were shiny now. She had cut the fingers off a pair of gloves so she could still hold a pen properly, and she was annotating one of the salvaged maintenance maps in blue-black ink with the same fastidious little strokes she used to reserve for executive briefing packets. Her arthritic hands hated the cold. She flexed them against a heat mug between pages, then went back to translating corporate part numbers into things the cooperative could actually find or barter for.

"Rear knee actuator, model B-14," she said without looking up. "If Sloan uses the harbor surplus batch, he needs a two-millimeter shim or the joint will grind itself stupid in a week."

From behind the privacy tarps, Tavi answered, "See, this is why we keep you, Rusk. Not the charm. The other thing."

Celia put a neat line through one number and replaced it with another. "I'm touched by the clarity of your performance metrics."

Jo snorted. "Look at that. She lived with us three months and finally learned how to make a joke."

Tavi pushed through the tarp a moment later with his sleeves rolled and a smear of sealing foam dried white across the heel of one hand. Midwinter had carved the stoop deeper into him, but it had not made him slower. He moved like somebody who had learned to husband every bend and reach after losing the equipment that once did half the work for him. The burn scar high on his right forearm shone pale where the hair never grew back. He had a strip of cartoon-frog tape stuck to his sweater from patching a kid's splint and had not noticed it yet.

"Mira," he said, low enough that only she heard the change in register, "you got another clean basin? This one came in with salt in the port and an opinion about iodine."

She handed him the basin from under the counter. Their fingers bumped. He tipped his chin in thanks, private as a touch.

Then louder, for the room: "If anyone's waiting on lower-back work, eat first. I'm not listening to stomachs complain over a tissue printer again."

"You are absolutely listening," Jo said.

"Fine. I won't enjoy it."

Mira stamped two intake slips, rerouted a case with tendon memory into wash instead of repair, and let the rhythm of the room settle her into the day. Blue Hour had never managed this part. Blue Hour had been steam and secrecy and the animal jump in her chest every time the back door opened. The terminal was colder, louder, less forgiving to bodies, and somehow easier to breathe in. Here the work had spilled out where people could see it. The city still had not decided whether that made them useful or criminal. Men in clean municipal coats still slowed on the curb outside and counted heads. Clients still came in looking first for exits, then for uniforms, then for the nearest lie they could tell about why they were really here. But fear had lost its monopoly on the room. Schedules, broth, clipboards, and Jo's voice had taken back some ground.

The first urgent case was a courier named Lenna whose bargain sprint shell had split along the calf seam after three illegal double shifts. Lenna was maybe nineteen, smelled of fryer oil and rain, and kept apologizing to the body as if it had done her a favor by giving out late instead of on the overpass. Mira cleaned the seam, felt the heat around it, checked for internal contamination, and wrote BED TWO / TAVI / POSSIBLE NERVE CARRY on the card.

"Can you make it stop kicking when I try to sleep?" Lenna asked. "It keeps doing this twitch like it's trying to catch up to somebody."

"Maybe," Mira said. "Can you tell me if it's your somebody or the body's?"

Lenna thought about that longer than most people did. "Mine doesn't run that fast."

That was enough answer for now. Mira circled MEMORY too and sent her through.

The man with the humming jaw turned out to be a bus mechanic in a leased date-night face, embarrassed enough to blush through foundation. Every time he closed his mouth hard, a fragment of hymn rose in his throat, not words, just the tune. He hated hymns. His mother had taken him to church until he was sixteen and dead set against joy. Mira watched the muscles bunch under the borrowed skin and wrote WASH / OBSERVE / NO CHARGE because sometimes the cooperative stayed open by deciding shame was not billable. He looked like he might cry at that and hid it by asking if the soup had celery. Jo shouted from across the room that it had celery, cabbage, and God's indifference.

Near eleven, when the first rush loosened enough for the room to hear itself again, Lin came down from the mezzanine with a canvas satchel banging against their hip and a stack of copied intake forms hugged to their chest. They had grown into the terminal's chaos faster than Mira had. They carried themselves with a settled alertness now, moving through crowds with the practiced tilt of someone who knew when to give a frightened stranger space and when to stand close enough that the stranger did not bolt. Today they wore a navy peacoat from the donation rack, too big in the shoulders, and Jo's green scarf wound twice around the throat. In the outer pocket of the satchel, Mira could see the square edges of saved transit stubs lined up under a rubber band. Lin had started keeping them from each trip, date stamps turned outward, like proof that leaving and coming back could both be real.

"Did you eat?" Mira asked.

Lin looked briefly offended. "Jo watched me eat."

"That isn't an answer."

"I had eggs, toast, and a lecture about ferries after dark."

From the snack bar, Jo called, "Then hear it again. If the weather turns, you sleep in the church office and you don't try to become maritime."

Lin set the copied forms on the counter. At the top of each page, in their careful hand, they had added one new line beneath Maker Stamp and Scar Position: Who else knows. Mira had not asked them to. It was better than anything she would have thought to add.

"The South Basin group wants six more of these," Lin said. "And the church nurse says their supply ledger is a joke. She keeps insulin in a biscuit tin with the tea bags."

"That sounds like Jo's influence spreading."

"It sounds like survival," Lin said, then smiled to take the edge off it. "Also she really does use the biscuit tin. I checked."

Mira sorted the pages. Some had notes already clipped to them: a cleaner whose right hand remembered knife work, a warehouse renter who swore his borrowed lungs cried before he did, three unnamed bodies sleeping in the back room of a union office because none of them trusted official intake anymore.

"How many people?" she asked.

"In South Basin? Eight definite. Maybe eleven if the brother who called last night stops pretending he's asking for a friend. Two of them are from the tribunal video. The rest heard it sideways."

"Heard what sideways?"

"That there's a place here where somebody writes it down before they tell you you're defective."

The old protective answer rose in Mira on schedule: it was too soon, too visible, too easy for a city to notice a pattern once poor people started using paper instead of panic. She did not say it. Lin could hear the parts she left out better than most.

"You think I should wait," they said.

"I think I want impossible things. I'd like the law to get better and the weather to warm up and for you to carry three separate knives."

"I've got two."

"That isn't the reassuring number you think it is."

Lin's mouth twitched. Under the fluorescent hum and ferry wind rattling the glass, they looked older to Mira than when they had woken on Tavi's table, and younger too. Less raw. More willing to occupy their own outline. Safety would have made them smaller. Instead they stood there with Jo's scarf bright at the throat, finally filling the coat instead of disappearing inside it.

"There are more than me," they said, quieter. "If I stay here being the example, they'll keep acting like I'm the only one. I don't want my face doing that work for them."

Mira folded the top page once, hard, to give her hands something to do. "I know."

Lin watched her for another beat, then nodded as if the answer had been adequate, if not pleasant. "Good. Because the south cutter leaves in twenty minutes."

Tavi appeared at Mira's shoulder with a zip pouch of sterile strips and a cheap penlight. "Take these. Don't lend the light to anybody who says they'll give it right back. That's how civilization ends."

Lin took the pouch. "I thought civilization ended in corporate insolvency."

"It was already sick," Tavi said. "I'm talking about my penlight."

He reached past them to tuck the pouch deeper into the satchel, fingers quick and paternal only until he noticed himself doing it. Then he cleared his throat and stepped back.

"If anybody shows you a flank seam with the tissue puckered inward," he said, "don't let them tell you it's a field repair scar. Call us first."

Lin tilted their head. "You're saying that like you don't want me asking why."

Tavi rubbed a thumb over the burn scar on his forearm, an old habit he only seemed to catch after it started. "I'm saying I know what cheap looked like when the other shops got interested. That's all."

Mira heard the rest of the sentence because she always did. That's all and too much. That's all and partly my fault. Lin heard enough of it too. They put the penlight away with careful gentleness.

"I'll call," they said.

Jo packed them a paper bag so overstuffed with food it looked illicit. Gabe wrote three emergency numbers on the inside flap, plus a fourth for a tide mechanic in South Basin who owed him money and hated being reminded. Celia, without ceremony, slid one of the annotated maintenance sheets under the clipped forms.

"If you see maker marks from Pelorus or Eastline," she said, "circle them. They use cleaner resin, but the budget seams delaminate in cold."

Lin blinked. "Thank you."

Celia recapped her pen. "This isn't altruism. I prefer data to rumor."

Jo pointed a soup ladle at her. "Lies. You're getting attached."

"I'm getting accurate," Celia said, and because the room had learned her by now, nobody let her have the last word for free.

Mira walked Lin as far as the south doors, where the wind from the bay came straight through the cracked vestibule and made everybody entering hunch like they had been insulted. Outside, the active commuter slip two piers over still took the noon cutter. Lin's scarf snapped at their shoulder like a flag. The satchel looked too light for what it carried and too heavy for comfort.

"You call when you land," Mira said.

"I know."

"You call if the room feels wrong."

Lin made a face. "All rooms feel wrong at first."

"You know what I mean."

"I do."

For a moment Mira thought Lin might hug her. She had never gotten used to wanting that and not knowing whether asking would make it smaller. Lin spared them both the negotiation. They leaned in, quick and hard, their cheek cold against hers, then stepped back.

"The soup here is better," they said, and the smile that came with it was half mischief, half nerves laid bare. "That's my official comparative report so far."

Then they turned and went down the walk toward the working pier, boots thudding over salt-stiff planks. Mira stood in the vestibule until they disappeared into the line of bodies boarding the cutter, navy coat swallowed by other coats, green scarf the last thing she lost sight of. Behind her the board clacked, resetting from BEDS to CLEAN PORTS. The room had another name in it already.

When she came back from the doors, Tavi was waiting by the supply closet with the expression he wore when he wanted to say something private and did not trust himself to attempt subtlety. He jerked his chin toward the former ticket supervisor's office. Inside, the filing cabinets held antibiotics, thermal socks, condoms, spare batteries, and the little stash of good tea Jo pretended everybody could touch but tracked like contraband. The room smelled of dust, antiseptic, and the cedar block somebody had tucked into the blanket drawer for optimism.

"You look like you're about to wear a trench in the floor," Tavi said once the door shut.

"I was watching Lin leave."

"I gathered that from the haunting widow pose, yeah."

She gave him a look. He lifted both hands in surrender, then used one of them to push a curl back behind her ear with such ordinary tenderness that the insult failed to hold.

"I'm not saying they shouldn't go," he said. "I'm saying if those other rooms start showing the same seam patterns, it's not going to stay small."

"There wasn't any small left once it hit tribunal."

"That's not what I mean."

"I know what you mean."

He exhaled through his nose, frustrated not at her but at the fact that knowledge kept coming shaped like blame. The same hands that now taped splints and cleaned ports had once helped make bargain tissue cheaper, stretch donor residue farther, turn a person into yield. Mira had not forgotten that. Forgetting would have been a lie, and their life together worked better when it did not need those. But she had also watched those hands close the eyes of bodies nobody could save, and steady Lin through nights when memory came in like broken weather, and count out the last of their cash without pretending numbers were mercy. History had not vanished. It had grown joints.

"If you start apologizing in engineering terms," she said, "I'm charging by the minute."

His mouth twitched. "Co-op rate?"

"Extortion."

"Fair."

She put both palms against his sweater and pushed him back into the filing cabinet. The metal thunk made the aspirin bottle on top rattle. He laughed once, surprised, then stopped laughing when she kissed him. His mouth tasted like burnt coffee and clove gum. His hands came to her waist slow at first, asking, then less slow when she answered by biting his lower lip until he swore softly into her mouth. She slid a hand under his thermal shirt and felt the long spared muscle of his side go tight under her palm. Cold air leaked through the window frame. The rest of him was warm.

"This is a terrible room for romance," he said against her throat.

"This isn't romance."

"What is it?"

Mira thumbed the cartoon-frog tape still stuck to his sweater. "Inventory control."

That made him grin, and the grin broke something loose in her chest she had been holding shut since the doors. He kissed her again, one hand at the back of her thigh now, the other braced by her head on the cabinet. There was space for exactly one bad decision in the room and not enough time to enjoy it properly. She liked the honesty of that. She liked that desire could still survive next to blankets, antibiotics, and a ledger full of ruined people who would be back tomorrow. When his palm slipped under her shirt, cold from washing up, she jerked and almost laughed; he used the sound to find her mouth again.

Then Gabe's voice came through the door, muffled but delighted. "If you two are reinventing morale in there, do it quieter. I've got a dock uncle outside crying because his borrowed jaw keeps singing at him."

Tavi let his forehead drop to hers. "Your family has no respect for medicine."

"My family has excellent timing."

"That is the opposite of what just happened."

She kissed the corner of his mouth once more, quick this time, a promise instead of a theft. "Wash up. If the jaw man bites you, I'm not doing your paperwork."

"I'd haunt you terribly."

"You already do."

They stepped back into the terminal separately enough to satisfy nobody. Jo took one look at Tavi's sweater riding half out of his waistband and tossed him a roll of tape like a curse. Celia kept writing, but the corner of her mouth moved. Gabe made a show of not looking at either of them and failed.

The afternoon settled over the terminal in layers: gulls beyond the glass, the flipboard's clack, soup cooling to film at the edges, Tavi cursing at a stripped screw, Jo telling a volunteer not to stack clean blankets beside bleach, Celia discovering an old ferry wiring diagram that could turn the dead upstairs lounge into one more heated ward if they found the right relay. Mira took the next intake slip, then the next. By dusk her wrist ached from stamping and her coat smelled like antiseptic and cabbage. She was tired in the ordinary way, the earned way. When the last daylight went blue over the water, Gabe flipped the board to NIGHT QUEUE and somebody in bed three started laughing at nothing Mira could hear. The sound moved through the room without frightening anyone. That, more than the locks on the doors or the lookout on the curb, was how she knew they had made something sturdier than a hiding place.

***

Five days later sleet came in sideways off the bay and sanded the terminal windows white by early evening. The heaters lost the argument in the corners. Everybody kept their coats on. Mira was at the intake counter with the cash tin, separating actual money from the day's other currencies: two packets of antibiotic ointment, a spool of copper wire, three transit vouchers, a jar of pickled carrots, and thirty-eight damp dollars folded so many times they had started to feel like cloth. The cooperative had made enough that week to pay for printer resin, lamp oil, and half of Brine Court's newest threat letter. It had not made enough to stop counting anything twice.

On the corkboard above the ledger, Lin's last message was pinned under a tide chart. They had written it on the back of a harbor safety flyer in the careful block hand they used when they wanted nobody to mistake a word: SOUTH BASIN HAS HEAT, BAD TEA, AND FOUR PEOPLE WHO THINK THEY'RE THE ONLY ONES. BACK THURSDAY IF THE BOATS BEHAVE. Under that, in smaller letters: TELL JO THE CHURCH COOK PUT CINNAMON IN THE LENTILS AND IT WAS A CRIME.

Jo had circled CRIME in red pen and added, beneath it, ARREST THEM.

Gabe was off his stool at last, leaning one hip against the dispatch booth and stretching out the foot that dragged when the cold got into it. He had traded the orange cap for a scarf in dockworker stripes and was teaching a volunteer how to reset the board without jamming the letters. Every third instruction turned into gossip. At the repair tables, Tavi was stripping down a rented forearm actuator with a concentration so complete it made him look almost gentle from far away. Up close it never quite did; up close you saw the speed in him, the impatience he kept filing into usefulness. Celia sat near the windows with a space heater at her knees and a ruler laid across one of the copied seed maps, building a cross-index of maker stamps that had started as triage and was turning into a census.

"We're out of the narrow-gauge cannulas again," she said.

Jo, decanting broth into smaller pots for the night line, didn't turn around. "Then write miracle pending on the order sheet and see if God brings any."

"I prefer suppliers who answer the phone."

"Then your standards are too high for this building."

Mira kept one ear on all of them and the rest of herself on the ledger. Lead triage meant the ledger never left her reach and the room never left her ear. The city had never granted them command. What it had left Mira was the mess itself, and her job was to keep it moving. She tracked which cots stayed dry, which ports had been flushed, which clients could be trusted with antibiotics and which needed someone to watch them swallow the first dose. She knew whose service fee would clear and whose would not. She knew which names in the registry had started sending other names. The knowledge did not feel like victory. It felt like holding a door against weather with more people leaning on the safe side of it than the frame had been built for.

Tavi came up beside her with his hands still wet and leaned his shoulder into hers for the length of one breath, just enough pressure to register as theirs and not enough to invite commentary from the room.

"You eaten anything solid?" he asked.

"I had soup."

"Soup with Jo supervising doesn't count. She thins it when she gets philosophical."

"That's not how philosophy works."

"It's exactly how philosophy works."

She finally looked at him. There was printer resin under one thumbnail and a new white nick across the knuckle of his left hand. He noticed her noticing and tucked the hand behind the towel like a teenager hiding a cigarette burn.

"Lin says they're back tomorrow," she said.

"If the boats behave."

"The boats never behave."

"Yeah," he said. "They take after the rest of the city."

He did not kiss her. He did not need to. The contact at her shoulder and the ease of the joke sat in her longer than a kiss would have, because there was history under it and no attempt to mop the history up afterward. Then the outer doors banged wide and all of them turned at once.

The woman who came in with the weather looked like she had traveled in pieces and kept losing some on the way. Her raincoat had been shortened by hand with blue thread that did not match anything else she wore. She carried a red lunch tin against her chest hard enough to dent it further, and every few seconds her thumb checked the latch as if the act of arriving might shake it loose. Salt had dried white on her boots. She was maybe forty, maybe younger and just overworked, with one false eyelash hanging on from whatever job had required her to look more rested than she felt. She paused inside the doors not because she was shy but because people who had been chased by official rooms learned to take stock before they sat down in them.

Jo saw her first. Of course Jo saw her first.

"Close that door or pay the heating bill," she said, then took in the coat, the face, the way the woman held the lunch tin like evidence. Her voice changed by half a degree. "Come in, honey. If you're here to faint, do it near the soup."

The woman shut the door behind her. "Lin sent me," she said.

The room tightened by a degree at that. Nobody went quiet if the cooperative could help it, but attention traveled. Gabe straightened. Tavi dropped the towel. Mira was already moving.

Up close, the woman smelled of bus heat, stale perfume, and the penny-metal tang of old fear. There was a transit band still looped around one wrist, four different punch marks stacked over each other. She set the red tin on the counter carefully, opened it, and took out not lunch but a sheaf of damp papers wrapped in wax paper and a terminal address card Mira recognized at once. They had cut the cards from old ferry schedule stock. On the back, in Lin's hand, was written: SOUTH BASIN, ST. BRIGID BASEMENT, PIA DIZON. Under that: LEFT FLANK SCAR. MAKER STAMP NOT COMMONFRAME.

"I'm Pia," the woman said, touching the note like she needed to make sure her name was still in there. "The one with the light eye said to come before somebody marked this body defective. She said you'd write it down first."

"I'll write it down," Mira said.

Pia let out a breath that seemed to have started three neighborhoods away. "Good. Because I didn't come for charity. I brought records."

She pushed the papers forward. Bus receipts. Rental contracts. A housekeeping schedule from the South Basin conference hotel with somebody else's shifts crossed out and hers added in blue ink. At the bottom was a list of six names and three maker stamps, all written in a hand Mira did not know. Pia had underlined two of them so hard the paper thinned.

Lin's hand showed in the whole bundle: the labeled scar, the wax paper, the names underlined hard enough to thin the page. They weren't just sending bodies here now. They were teaching other people what to carry in with them.
